The Universal as Aid, Not Proof
Explains the universal and the isti'ana/istidlal distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the universal concept can only aid in conceiving attributes of God and cannot serve as proof for spatiality, and how the distinction between isti'ana and istidlal resolves an apparent objection.

Core Idea
The universal aids imagination but does not prove external existence.

This passage tackles a subtle but critical point: when we speak of a universal essence like 'human', it seems to imply specific limbs and dimensions. The objector uses this to argue that God would have spatiality. But the author shows that the universal is taken without specific measures, and that it only helps us conceive, not prove—since proof would require the controversial existence of the natural universal externally.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the structure: first a clarification about the universal, then an objection, then the answer, then the reason for using 'isti'ana' instead of 'istidlal'.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the objection and its answer
Find where the objector says 'if you say...' and note the author's response. The objection is about the universal human having specific limbs; the answer is that these limbs are taken as universal, not particular.
3
Understand the key distinction
The passage ends by explaining why the author used 'isti'ana' (aid) rather than 'istidlal' (proof). Understand that proof requires the existence of the natural universal externally, which is disputed, while aid does not.
This distinction is crucial for kalam methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كذلك حالاً فيه لاختص بمقدار معين ووضع مخصوص فلا يطابق أفراداً متباينة المقادير والأوضاع فلا يكون مشتركاً بينها . وأما الثاني فلأن العلم بالماهية الكلية لا يختص بمقدار ووضع مخصوصين وإلا لم يكن علماً بتلك الماهية، فإن قلت : الإنسان المشترك لا بد أن يكون له أعضاء مخصوصة من عين ويد وظهر وبطن وغيرها على أوضاع مختلفة ومقادير متناسبة وأبعاد متفاوتة ولا شك في أنه من حيث هو يكون متحيزاً قلت هذا إنما يلزم إذا لم توجد تلك الأعضاء من حيث أنها كلية مشتركة؛ ولا شبهة أنها في الإنسان الكلي مأخوذة كذلك، وإنما قال وربما يستعان في تصوره ولم يقل وربما يستدل عليه لأن الاستدلال به موقوف على وجود الكلي الطبيعي ووجود العلم به في الخارج مع أنه مختلف فيه بخلاف الاستعانة المذكورة، فإنها تتم مع ذلك الاختلاف
Thus it would not apply to a specific measure and particular position, so it would not match individuals differing in measures and positions, thus it would not be common among them. As for the second, because knowledge of the universal essence is not restricted to a specific measure and particular position, otherwise it would not be knowledge of that essence. If you say: the common human must have specific limbs of eye, hand, back, stomach, etc., in different positions, proportional measures, and varying dimensions, and there is no doubt that as such it is spatial. I say: this only follows if those limbs are not considered as universal common; and there is no doubt that in the universal human they are taken as such. He only said 'perhaps it is used as aid in its conception' and did not say 'perhaps it is used as proof for it' because proof by it depends on the existence of the natural universal and the existence of knowledge of it externally, which is disputed, unlike the mentioned aid, which is complete despite that disagreement.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection about the universal concept. It first explains that a universal essence cannot be restricted to a specific size or position, otherwise it would not be universal. The objector says that the common human must have specific limbs in specific positions, implying spatiality. The answer is that the limbs are taken as universal, not particular. Then it clarifies why the earlier author used 'aid' (isti'ana) rather than 'proof' (istidlal): proof requires the external existence of the natural universal, which is debated, while aid does not.

Key Terms
الكلي الطبيعي
الاستعانة
الاستدلال
الماهية الكلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the universal concept directly proves that God must have spatial attributes because common humans have spatial bodies.
Correction: The passage shows that the universal is only an aid to conception, not a proof, and that even the universal human is taken without specific measures.

Checkpoint
Why did the author use 'يستعان' (aid) instead of 'يستدل' (proof)?
A) Because proof is stronger than aid.
B) Because proof depends on the existence of the natural universal, which is disputed.
C) Because aid is only for imagination.
D) Because proof requires specifying a measure.

Key Takeaway
The universal concept aids in conceiving attributes of God but cannot serve as proof because that would require settling the disputed existence of the natural universal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own intellectual journey, when have you used a universal concept as an aid to understand something about Allah, but then falsely treated it as a proof, demanding that the concept exist externally? How does this passage correct that?
?
Consider a time you argued for a theological truth using a universal concept. Did you assume that concept's external existence without evidence? How can you now distinguish between aid and proof in your own reasoning?
?
This passage shows that even a universal as common as 'human' must be treated as universal, not particular, to avoid spatiality. What does this teach you about the limits of using created concepts to understand the Creator? Can you truly conceive of an attribute without inadvertently imposing spatial or temporal constraints? How will you guard against that?

Objection and Answer: The First Two Conditionals
Al-Jurjani records and rebuts an objection that the antecedent and consequent are identical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Jurjani defends the proof's conditionals against the charge of circularity by distinguishing between non-existence and negation, and between transitive and passive meanings.

Core Idea
Logical identity is avoided through subtle but valid semantic distinctions.

In this passage, Al-Jurjani responds to an objection from Sharh al-Maqasid that challenges the logical structure of the proof. The objection claims the antecedent and consequent in the first two conditionals are identical, making the argument circular. Al-Jurjani's answer shows how careful attention to wording resolves the apparent identity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Identify the claim that the consequent in each of the first two conditionals is identical to the antecedent, which would make the reasoning circular.
The objection is raised in Sharh al-Maqasid.
2
Examine the answer for the first conditional
Note the distinction between 'non-existence of the temporal event' (antecedent) and 'negation of the temporal event' (consequent). These are not the same: one is a state, the other a logical operation.
3
Examine the answer for the second conditional
Note the distinction: 'it is not dependent' (antecedent) vs. 'non-dependence' (consequent). The answer shows that 'non-dependence' can be transitive, avoiding identity, and it need not be taken as a passive verbal noun.
The commentator admits the answer is somewhat forced but asks for reflection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فإن لم يوجد فهو الأمر (الأول) اعترض عليه في شرح المقاصد بأن التالي في كل من الشرطيتين الأوليتين عين المقدم وقديجاب بأن المقدم في الشرطية الأولى عدم وجود الحادث، والتالي نفي الحادث والمغايرة بينهما ظاهرة ولا ضرورة داعية إلى جعل النفي بمعنى الانتفاء، حتى يلزم ما ذكر والمقدم في الثانية أن لا يكون مستنداً والتالي عدم إلى الاستناد يجوز أن يكون متعدياً ولا يلزم أن يجعل مصدراً من المجهول، ولا يخفى ما فيه من التعسف فتأمل.
His saying: 'For if it does not exist, it is the first matter'—an objection was raised in Sharh al-Maqasid that the consequent in each of the first two conditionals is identical to the antecedent. It was answered that the antecedent in the first conditional is the non-existence of the temporal event, and the consequent is the negation of the temporal event, and the difference between them is apparent, and there is no necessity to take negation as meaning non-existence, so that what was mentioned [i.e., circularity] would follow. And the antecedent in the second is that it is not dependent, and the consequent is non-dependence—it is possible that 'non-dependence' (al-isti'nad) is transitive, and it is not necessary to make it a passive verbal noun. And the difficulty in that is not hidden, so reflect.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage discusses an objection raised against the proof of divine power. The objection, found in Sharh al-Maqasid, claims that in the first two conditional statements of the proof, the consequent is the same as the antecedent, making the reasoning circular. The answer explains that in the first conditional, the antecedent is 'the non-existence of the temporal event' and the consequent is 'the negation of the temporal event'—these are different because non-existence is a state while negation is a logical operation. Thus no circularity. In the second conditional, the antecedent is 'it is not dependent' and the consequent is 'non-dependence'—here 'non-dependence' can be understood as a transitive noun, not necessarily a passive one, avoiding identity. The commentator admits the answer is somewhat forced but asks the reader to reflect.

Key Terms
شرطية
مقدم
تالي
نفي
انتفاء
استناد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The antecedent and consequent in the first two conditionals are identical, making the proof circular.
Correction: Al-Jurjani shows they are not identical: in the first, 'non-existence' vs. 'negation' differ; in the second, 'non-dependence' can be transitive, not passive.

Checkpoint
What is the objection raised against the first two conditionals in the proof?
A) The consequents are false.
B) The antecedents are impossible.
C) The consequent in each is identical to the antecedent.
D) The conditionals are not implied by the premise.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani defends the proof from circularity by showing that apparent identity between antecedent and consequent disappears with precise logical and grammatical analysis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a subtle logical distinction that feels forced (like the one in this passage), do you immediately dismiss it or take the time to understand it? How does your reaction reveal your commitment to truth over convenience?
?
The commentator himself says 'the difficulty in that is not hidden, so reflect.' What areas of your own belief system are you avoiding because they require this kind of painstaking reflection?
?
If you were in a debate and your argument faced a subtle objection, would you honestly try to answer with the same precision as Al-Jurjani, or would you resort to dismissing the objection as overly pedantic? What does that say about your intellectual integrity?

First Objection: Consequent Identical to Antecedent
Objection and response on logical identity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how kalam scholars defend logical proofs by distinguishing between terms like 'non-existence' and 'negation'.

Core Idea
In kalam, logical distinctions save proofs from tautology.

The commentator addresses a serious objection from Sharh al-Maqasid that threatens the logical validity of the main proof. The objection claims that the consequent is identical to the antecedent, making the conditionals tautological. The response shows subtle but crucial differences between the terms used, preserving the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage that states the objection: the objector claims that in each of the first two conditionals, the consequent is exactly the same as the antecedent.
Notice the claim of identity.
2
Examine the response
Read the response that distinguishes between 'non-existence of the originated' and 'negation of the originated', and between 'not being based' and 'non- to being based'.
The response argues these are not identical because negation is a judgment.
3
Reflect on the logical nuance
Consider why this distinction matters for the overall proof and how it defends against the objection.
The commentator admits some strain in the response but asks for reflection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فإن لم يوجد فهو الأمر (الأول) اعترض عليه في شرح المقاصد بأن التالي في كل من الشرطيتين الأوليتين عين المقدم وقد أجيب بأن المقدم في الشرطية الأولى عدم وجود الحادث، والتالي نفي الحادث والمغايرة بينهما ظاهرة ولا ضرورة داعية إلى جعل النفي بمعنى الانتفاء، حتى يلزم ما ذكر والمقدم في الثانية أن لا يكون مستنداً والتالي عدم إلى الاستناد يجوز أن يكون متعدياً ولا يلزم أن يجعل مصدراً من المجهول، ولا يخفى ما فيه من التعسف فتأمل. قوله : (فإن لم ينته فهو الثالث قيل عليه كان الأنسب أن يقول فإن لم ينته إلى قديم أو انتهى بواسطة صفات لا تتناهى وأجيب بأن المذكور في الدليل هو التسلسل في الحوادث الممكنة الغير القائمة بذاته تعالى وأما احتمال التسلسل في الحوادث القائمة به تعالى فقد أشار إلى بطلانه بقوله واعلم أن هذا الاستدلال إلخ .
His saying: ((For if it does not exist, then it is the first matter.)) An objection was raised against it in Sharh al-Maqasid, that the consequent in each of the two first conditionals is identical to the antecedent. And it was answered that the antecedent in the first conditional is the non-existence of the originated thing, and the consequent is the negation of the originated thing, and the difference between them is apparent, and there is no necessity to make negation mean non-existence so that what was mentioned would follow. And the antecedent in the second is that it is not based, and the consequent is 'non-' to 'based' which can be transitive, and it is not necessary to make it a passive verbal noun. The strain in this is not hidden, so reflect. His saying: ((For if it does not terminate, then it is the third.)) It was said about it: It would be more appropriate to say: 'For if it does not terminate at an eternal, or it terminates by means of infinite attributes.' And it was answered that what is mentioned in the proof is the infinite regress in possible originated things that do not subsist in His essence. As for the possibility of infinite regress in originated things that subsist in Him, He indicated its falsity by His saying: 'And know that this demonstration...' etc.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage contains two objections and their answers. The first objection is from Sharh al-Maqasid, criticizing the logical structure of the main proof. The objector says that in the first two conditionals, the consequent is the same as the antecedent (i.e., tautological). The answer explains that in the first conditional, the antecedent is 'non-existence of the originated' (adam wujud al-hadath) while the consequent is 'negation of the originated' (nafy al-hadath); these are different because negation is a judgment, not the same as non-existence. In the second conditional, the antecedent is 'not being based' (la yakunu mustanidan) and the consequent is 'non- to being based' ('adam ila al-istinad) which can be transitive, so it is not necessarily a tautology. The answer admits some strain but asks for reflection. The second objection is about the third alternative: the proof says 'if it does not terminate', but it would be more precise to say 'if it does not terminate at an eternal, or terminates through infinite attributes.' The answer clarifies that the proof only concerns infinite regress in possible originated things not subsisting in God's essence. The possibility of infinite regress in things subsisting in Him (like attributes) is addressed later by the author's statement 'and know that this demonstration...'

Key Terms
شرطية
مقدم
تالي
نفي
انتفاء
مصدر مجهول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objector believes that if the consequent is identical to the antecedent, the proof is invalid.
Correction: The response shows that the terms are not identical upon careful analysis, preserving the proof's validity.

Checkpoint
How does the response to the first objection differentiate between the antecedent and consequent in the first conditional?
A) The antecedent is 'negation of the originated' and the consequent is 'non-existence of the originated'.
B) The antecedent is 'non-existence of the originated' and the consequent is 'negation of the originated'.
C) The antecedent and consequent are identical.
D) The response does not differentiate them.

Key Takeaway
Logical distinctions between terms like 'non-existence' and 'negation' can defend a proof from charges of tautology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever rejected a theological argument because a surface-level objection seemed convincing? What does this lesson teach you about the need to examine logical distinctions carefully before dismissing a proof?
?
The commentator acknowledges 'strain' in the response. Are you willing to accept that some answers may be imperfect yet still sufficient? Where in your own faith journey do you need to trust the depth of scholarship even when the explanation feels strained?
?
If you were to defend the proof from this objection, would you have spotted the distinction between 'negation' and 'non-existence'? This lesson challenges you to train your mind in precise theological reasoning. What step will you take to deepen your own understanding of kalam arguments?

Second Objection: Scope of the Third Alternative
Objection about missing alternatives and response limiting scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a kalam proof can exclude infinite regress in certain types of things by making a precise distinction about what the proof addresses.

Core Idea
Kalam proofs are precise about their scope; they don't need to rule out every form of regress.

The second objection points out that the proof's third alternative is stated too simply: it should account for termination at an eternal or via infinite attributes. The answer restricts the proof's scope to possible things not subsisting in God, leaving other possibilities to be treated separately. This clarifies the proof's focus and avoids unnecessary complications.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection: the proof says 'if it does not terminate', but it would be more precise to mention termination at an eternal or via infinite attributes.
The objector wants the proof to address all possibilities explicitly.
2
See the answer's restriction
Read the response: the proof only concerns infinite regress in possible things not subsisting in God's essence.
The proof does not need to cover things subsisting in God, like attributes.
3
Connect to later discussion
The response indicates that the possibility of regress in things subsisting in God is addressed later in the text by the author's statement 'and know that this demonstration...'.
This shows the proof is part of a larger argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فإن لم يوجد فهو الأمر (الأول) اعترض عليه في شرح المقاصد بأن التالي في كل من الشرطيتين الأوليتين عين المقدم وقد أجيب بأن المقدم في الشرطية الأولى عدم وجود الحادث، والتالي نفي الحادث والمغايرة بينهما ظاهرة ولا ضرورة داعية إلى جعل النفي بمعنى الانتفاء، حتى يلزم ما ذكر والمقدم في الثانية أن لا يكون مستنداً والتالي عدم إلى الاستناد يجوز أن يكون متعدياً ولا يلزم أن يجعل مصدراً من المجهول، ولا يخفى ما فيه من التعسف فتأمل. قوله : (فإن لم ينته فهو الثالث قيل عليه كان الأنسب أن يقول فإن لم ينته إلى قديم أو انتهى بواسطة صفات لا تتناهى وأجيب بأن المذكور في الدليل هو التسلسل في الحوادث الممكنة الغير القائمة بذاته تعالى وأما احتمال التسلسل في الحوادث القائمة به تعالى فقد أشار إلى بطلانه بقوله واعلم أن هذا الاستدلال إلخ .
His saying: ((For if it does not exist, then it is the first matter.)) An objection was raised against it in Sharh al-Maqasid, that the consequent in each of the two first conditionals is identical to the antecedent. And it was answered that the antecedent in the first conditional is the non-existence of the originated thing, and the consequent is the negation of the originated thing, and the difference between them is apparent, and there is no necessity to make negation mean non-existence so that what was mentioned would follow. And the antecedent in the second is that it is not based, and the consequent is 'non-' to 'based' which can be transitive, and it is not necessary to make it a passive verbal noun. The strain in this is not hidden, so reflect. His saying: ((For if it does not terminate, then it is the third.)) It was said about it: It would be more appropriate to say: 'For if it does not terminate at an eternal, or it terminates by means of infinite attributes.' And it was answered that what is mentioned in the proof is the infinite regress in possible originated things that do not subsist in His essence. As for the possibility of infinite regress in originated things that subsist in Him, He indicated its falsity by His saying: 'And know that this demonstration...' etc.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage contains two objections and their answers. The first objection is from Sharh al-Maqasid, criticizing the logical structure of the main proof. The objector says that in the first two conditionals, the consequent is the same as the antecedent (i.e., tautological). The answer explains that in the first conditional, the antecedent is 'non-existence of the originated' (adam wujud al-hadath) while the consequent is 'negation of the originated' (nafy al-hadath); these are different because negation is a judgment, not the same as non-existence. In the second conditional, the antecedent is 'not being based' (la yakunu mustanidan) and the consequent is 'non- to being based' ('adam ila al-istinad) which can be transitive, so it is not necessarily a tautology. The answer admits some strain but asks for reflection. The second objection is about the third alternative: the proof says 'if it does not terminate', but it would be more precise to say 'if it does not terminate at an eternal, or terminates through infinite attributes.' The answer clarifies that the proof only concerns infinite regress in possible originated things not subsisting in God's essence. The possibility of infinite regress in things subsisting in Him (like attributes) is addressed later by the author's statement 'and know that this demonstration...'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objector believes the proof is incomplete because it doesn't address all forms of infinite regress.
Correction: The proof is only concerned with regress in possible things not subsisting in God; other forms are addressed separately.

Checkpoint
According to the response, what is the scope of the infinite regress mentioned in the proof?
A) All originated things including those subsisting in God.
B) Only possible originated things that do not subsist in God's essence.
C) Only originated things that subsist in God's essence.
D) Both eternal and originated things.

Key Takeaway
Kalam proofs are precisely scoped; they do not need to address every possible form of regress in a single statement.

The Quality in Contingent Power: Attribution vs. External Existence
Al-Jurjani clarifies that a quality found only in contingent power does not imply a disposition in the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that attributing a quality to contingent power does not imply the quality is a separate existing entity, preserving the Ash'ari doctrine.

Core Idea
Attribution is not external existence.

Al-Jurjani addresses a subtle point: when we say contingent power has a quality not found in eternal power, we might think that quality is a distinct entity. He clarifies that it is merely a property attributed to the power, not an independent existence. This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Note the key terms: صفة, معنى, وجود, خارج.
The passage is very concise; read carefully.
2
Identify the central claim
The passage argues that the existence of a quality in contingent power does not entail that the quality is a 'meaning' (معنى) subsisting in it, which would contradict Ahl al-Haqq.
This is a defensive move against an implied objection.
3
Understand the Ash'ari background
In Ash'ari theology, only substances and accidents exist. Additional 'meanings' (معان) are not real entities. The quality here is merely a property, not a separate accident.
This background is essential to grasp why the author emphasizes 'thubūt' (establishment) rather than 'wujūd' (existence).
4
Grasp the conclusion
The phrase 'thubūtuhā lahā' means the quality is established for the power—i.e., it is an attribute, not a thing existing externally. This aligns with Ash'ari ontology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( في صفة غير موجودة في القدرة القديمة ) ولا يلزم من كون تلك الصفة موجودة في القدرة الحادثة قيام المعنى بالسمعنى حتى يخالف مذهب أهل الحق لأن المراد من اشتراك القدرة الحادثة فيها ومن وجودها فيها ثبوتها لها ولا يلزم من هذا أن يكون نفسها أمراً موجوداً في الخارج
His saying: 'in a quality not present in the eternal power' — and it does not follow from the existence of that quality in contingent power that a meaning subsists in the [hearing] such that it would contradict the doctrine of Ahl al-Haqq, because what is meant by the contingent power's sharing in it and its existence in it is its being established for it, and it does not follow from this that it itself is something existing externally.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage explains the earlier statement about a quality present only in contingent power. It clarifies that saying contingent power 'has' that quality does not imply that the quality is a real entity (معنى) subsisting in the power, which would be a separate attribute in the Ash'ari sense. The Ash'ari doctrine (Ahl al-Haqq) holds that only substances and accidents exist; additional 'meanings' are not separate realities. Here, the quality is simply a property attributed to the power, not an external existent.

Key Terms
صفة
معنى
وجود
خارج
ثبوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Attributing a quality to contingent power means that quality is a separate existing entity.
Correction: The quality is merely established (ثبوت) as a property, not an external existent. The Ash'ari position denies that attributes are real 'meanings' (معان) apart from the essence.

Checkpoint
What does 'thubūtuhā lahā' (ثبوتها لها) indicate about the quality?
A) The quality is a real, external thing.
B) The quality is merely attributed to the power, not necessarily a separate entity.
C) The quality is an accident inhering in the power.
D) The quality is the same as the power itself.

Key Takeaway
A quality attributed to contingent power is a property (thubūt), not an external existent, preserving Ash'ari theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I have the power to do good,' do you imagine that power as something stored inside you like a separate object? What does al-Jurjani's distinction teach you about the nature of your own attributes, and how should that reshape your humility before Allah?
?
Consider one of your negative traits, like anger or impatience. Do you treat it as an external force that 'overtakes' you? In light of this lesson, how would it change your self-accountability to see that trait as an attribution (thubut) rather than an independent entity?
?
If you mistakenly think that Allah's attributes are separate things added to His essence, what danger does that pose to your tawhid? How does the logic of this passage guard you against that error, and what practical step will you take to affirm that Allah's attributes are not other than Him?

The Condition of Distinction in Knowledge
Al-Ījī discusses the necessity of taghayyur for knowledge and refines its condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critical condition of intrinsic distinction (taghayyur) in knowledge and why it is a condition of the presence itself, not an additional condition after presence.

Core Idea
Knowledge requires intrinsic distinction between knower and known.

In this passage, al-Ījī addresses a subtle point: can a thing know itself? The discussion revolves around whether the condition of distinction (taghayyur) is required for knowledge. Al-Ījī clarifies that this condition applies to the very presence that constitutes knowledge, not as an extra condition. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping the nature of knowledge in Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing; then read the translation. Get the overall flow of the argument.
The passage is dense; don't worry about every detail yet.
2
Identify the objection and initial support
Find where the author presents the opponent's view: why not require taghayyur? Then see the initial support: everyone necessarily understands knowledge as something distinct.
3
Analyze the criticism of the commentator's reading
Notice the criticism: if taghayyur is a condition after presence, then presence could exist without knowledge, which is absurd. This shows the need for a correct interpretation.
This step highlights the danger of misplacing conditions.
4
Understand the correct position and the analogy
Grasp the correct view: taghayyur conditions presence itself, so without it there is no presence and thus no knowledge. The Indian-human analogy clarifies that knowledge is a specific type of presence.
This analogy is key to resolving the issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( لكن لم لا يجوز أن يشترط فيه التغاير) قد يؤيد هذا بأن العلم مما يفهم بالضرورة كل أحد إما بكنهه أو بما يميز عن سائر أغياره، ونحن نعلم قطعا أن مجرد عدم غيبية الشيء عن نفسه الذي سموه بالحضور عند نفسه سواء كان مجرداً أو مادياً ليس مما يصدق عليه هذا المفهوم بل ربما يدعي أن عدم غيبية الشيء عن نفسه ليس فيه تفاوت بين المجرد وغيره، بحيث يكون أحدهما علماً والآخر غير علم قيل : المفهوم من تقرير الشارح أنه حمل هذا الكلام على أن التغاير شرط بعد تحقق الحضور ولا وجه له لأنه يؤدي إلى أن يوجد حقيقة الشيء بدونه لانتفاء شرطه وهو غير معقول. فالصواب أن مراد المصنف لم لا يجوز أن يشترط في حضور الشيء للشيء المغايرة الذاتية بين الحاضر وما حضر عنده حتى لا يتعلق علم الشيء بنفسه لانتفاء الحضور المستلزم لتلك المغايرة لا لانتفائها مع تحقق الحضور الذي هو حقيقة العلم على الفرض وأجيب بأن الإضافة في قول الشارح حقيقة العلم ما ذكرتموه مثل الإضافة في قولنا : حقيقة الهندي الإنسان لا مثلها في قولنا : حقيقة الإنسان الحيوان الناطق، وحينئذ يكون العلم حصة من حقيقة الحضور مشروطاً بالمغايرة الذاتية فلا يلزم من وجود الحضور وجود العلم كما لا يلزم من وجود الإنسان وجود الهندي وإن كان حقيقته ذلك .
His saying: (But why is it not permissible that distinction is required in it?) This may be supported by the fact that knowledge is understood by everyone necessarily, either by its essence or by what distinguishes it from other things. And we know with certainty that mere non-absence of a thing from itself, which they call 'presence to itself', whether it is abstract or material, is not something to which this concept applies. Rather, one might claim that non-absence of a thing from itself has no variation between the abstract and other things, such that one of them would be knowledge and the other not knowledge. It was said: The understanding from the commentator's explanation is that he interpreted this statement as requiring distinction after presence is actualized, but there is no justification for this because it leads to the existence of the reality of a thing without its condition, which is unintelligible. The correct view is that the author's intent is: why is it not permissible that in the presence of a thing to another, intrinsic distinction is required between the present and that to which it is present, so that a thing's knowledge of itself does not occur due to the lack of presence that entails that distinction, not due to the lack of distinction while presence—which is the reality of knowledge on the assumption—is actualized. And it was answered: The addition in the commentator's statement 'the reality of knowledge is what you mentioned' is like the addition in our statement 'the reality of the Indian is the human', not like that in our statement 'the reality of the human is the rational animal'. In that case, knowledge is a specific portion of the reality of presence conditioned by intrinsic distinction, so the existence of presence does not necessitate the existence of knowledge, just as the existence of a human does not necessitate the existence of an Indian even though its reality is that.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and argumentative discussion about the condition of taghayyur (intrinsic distinction) for knowledge. The author begins by citing an objection: why not require distinction in knowledge? An initial support is offered: knowledge is understood necessarily by everyone as something that can be distinguished from other things; mere presence to oneself is not sufficient to be called knowledge, as there is no difference between abstract and material in that respect. Then a criticism is raised: the commentator (al-Sharīf al-Jurjānī) seems to treat taghayyur as a condition after presence is already realized, which is absurd because it would posit the reality of a thing without its condition. The correct interpretation of the author (al-Ījī) is that taghayyur is a condition for the presence itself, not for knowledge after presence; absence of presence (which requires distinction) prevents self-knowledge, not the absence of distinction while presence is actual. The final answer clarifies through an analogy: the relation between knowledge and presence is like that between 'Indian' and 'human': the Indian is a specific type of human, so not every human is an Indian. Similarly, knowledge is a specific kind of presence conditioned by intrinsic distinction, so presence does not entail knowledge.

Key Terms
التغاير
الحضور
الحقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that taghayyur is a condition after presence is realized.
Correction: The correct view is that taghayyur conditions the presence itself, so without it there is no presence and thus no knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is mere presence to oneself not sufficient for knowledge?
A) Because presence to oneself is impossible for abstract entities.
B) Because knowledge requires intrinsic distinction (taghayyur) between the knower and the known.
C) Because knowledge is a form of absence, not presence.
D) Because presence to oneself only applies to material things.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is a specific kind of presence conditioned by intrinsic distinction, so not all presence is knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often claim to 'know' yourself, but does your self-knowledge truly involve the intrinsic distinction this passage demands? Or have you merely assumed that your presence to yourself qualifies as knowledge? How might this condition force you to reevaluate the depth of your self-understanding, and what specific step will you take today to move from mere presence to genuine knowledge of your own soul?
?
Think of a deeply held belief or conviction you consider 'knowledge'—perhaps about another person or about Allah. According to this lesson, if it lacks the intrinsic distinction between you and the known, it may not be true knowledge. How does this challenge you to examine the foundations of your most cherished beliefs? What would it take for you to transform that mere presence into knowledge that meets the condition of distinction?

The Condition for a Valid Refutation: Conceptual Knowledge of the Essence
Commentary conditions al-Razi's refutation on full conceptual knowledge of essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Razi's refutation of the identity of will and essence depends on complete conceptual knowledge of God's essence, and why this condition limits the argument.

Core Idea
A refutation about God's essence only works if we fully know that essence.

This lesson examines a subtle condition attached to al-Razi's argument against Dirar's view. The commentator points out that the refutation is only effective if we have exhaustive knowledge of the divine essence—something beyond human reach. Understanding this condition clarifies the limits of theological argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the original Arabic and literal translation carefully. Notice the key phrases: 'إنما يتم إذا كان متصوراً بالكنه' and 'إنما يبطل الاتحاد في المفهوم'.
Pay attention to the conditional phrasing.
2
Identify the condition
The commentator states that the refutation is 'only complete if He is conceived in His essence.' Ask yourself: why would this be necessary?
3
Understand the distinction
The argument only disproves identity in concept, not necessarily in reality. Consider how this relates to previous discussions in the text.
This distinction appears repeatedly in the commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ويبطل الأول أنا نعلمه إلخ فيه أنه إنما يتم إذا كان متصوراً بالكنه على أنه إنما يبطل الاتحاد في المفهوم وقد مر مثله مراراً .
His saying: (And the first is invalidated by that we know Him, etc.) In it [there is] that it [the refutation] is only complete if He is conceived in His essence, and that it only invalidates the union in concept, and similar has passed repeatedly.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on al-Razi's refutation of the first position (Dirar's view that God's being willing is identical to His essence). The commentator points out that the refutation—based on our knowing God while doubting His being willing—only works if we have complete conceptual knowledge of the divine essence (التصور بالكنه). Without such exhaustive knowledge, the argument only disproves that 'willing' and 'essence' are identical in concept (الاتحاد في المفهوم), not that they could be identical in reality. This nuance has been mentioned repeatedly before.

Key Terms
كنه
مفهوم
اتحاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The refutation of Dirar's view is straightforward and valid without any further assumption.
Correction: The commentator states it is only complete if we conceive God in His essence (بالكنه), which is unattainable, so the refutation only disproves conceptual identity.

Checkpoint
What condition does the commentator place on the validity of al-Razi's refutation of the first position (will = essence)?
A) That we can know God's attributes separately
B) That we conceive God in His essence (بالكنه)
C) That we accept the union of will and essence in reality
D) That we reject all negative attributes

Key Takeaway
The validity of al-Razi's refutation rests on the unattainable condition of fully knowing God's essence, so it only disproves conceptual identity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you argue about Allah's attributes, do you do so with the humility that your knowledge is incomplete? How does this lesson's condition—that a refutation requires full conceptual knowledge of God's essence—confront your own intellectual pride? ?

After this lesson, what accountability do you feel to examine the limits of your own understanding before making definitive claims about Allah?
?
If even al-Razi’s sophisticated argument is conditional on an unattainable condition, how should that affect the way you listen to others' theological positions? Are you willing to extend grace and patience knowing that our concepts may fall short of the divine reality? What specific step will you take today to embody this humility?

Proof from Temporal Relation to Created Events
Proof for createdness of Quran from temporal relation to created events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the temporal relation of the Quran to created events demonstrates its createdness, both when it is delayed after a future event and when it precedes an event by a short time.

Core Idea
Any temporal relation to a created event proves the Quran is created.

This passage presents two arguments for the createdness of the Quran based on its temporal position relative to created events. The first argument focuses on the Quran being delayed after a future created will, while the second considers it preceding a created event by a short time. Together, they establish that the Quran cannot be eternal because it is temporally related to contingent things.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Start by reading the original Arabic text. Even if you don't understand every word, notice the structure: it presents two cases (delay and precedence) and then a clarifying remark.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the two temporal relations
The proof mentions two scenarios: the Quran being delayed after a future created event, and the Quran preceding a created event by a short time. Find these in the translation.
These correspond to 'التأخر' and 'التقدم'.
3
Understand the logical implication
Why does delay or precedence indicate createdness? The commentary explains that the eternal (qadeem) cannot be delayed after anything or precede anything in a temporal sense because it is eternal by essence. Any relation to created events breaks eternity.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends with 'so reflect upon that.' Take a moment to think about why this argument is considered compelling in Islamic theology.
This argument is part of a broader debate about the Quran's nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التأخر عن الإرادة الحادثة في المستقبل فلأن التأخر عن الشيء يوجب الحدوث خصوصاً إذا كان ذلك الشيء حادثاً واقعاً في الاستقبال، وأما التقدم على الكائن الحادث بمدة يسيرة فظاهر أيضاً دلالته على الحدوث. قوله : ( فلأن التأخر عن الشيء يوجب الحدوث لانحصار القديم عندهم في القديم بالذات وعدم تأخره عن شيء لا بالزمان ولا بالذات فليتأمل .
Delay after the created will in the future: for delay after something necessitates createdness, especially if that something is a created event occurring in the future. As for preceding the occurring created event by a short time, it is also apparent that it indicates createdness. And the saying: 'For delay after something necessitates createdness because, according to them, the eternal (qadeem) is confined to that which is eternal by essence, and it is not delayed after anything either temporally or essentially, so reflect upon that.'
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the createdness of the Quran based on its temporal relation to created events. It presents two aspects: first, if the Quran comes after a future created event (like the will), that delay indicates it is created; second, if it precedes a created event by a short time, that also indicates createdness. The commentary then clarifies that the concept of 'eternal' (qadeem) is restricted to that which is eternal by essence, and such an entity cannot be delayed after anything either in time or in essence. Therefore, any delay implies createdness.

Key Terms
القديم
الحادث
التأخر
التقدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that being preceded or succeeded by time is unrelated to createdness.
Correction: The proof shows that any temporal relation to created events necessitates createdness.

Checkpoint
What is the central claim of this proof?
A) The Quran is eternal because it is uncreated.
B) Because the Quran is either delayed after or preceded by a created event, it must itself be created.
C) The Quran is created because it is a miracle.
D) The Quran is eternal because it is the speech of God.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's temporal relation — whether delayed after or shortly before a created event — proves it cannot be eternal and must therefore be created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Quran's temporal relation to created events proves it is created, then it is a word that enters time and addresses your specific moment. Does this make you take its commands more seriously today, or do you still treat it as a distant, abstract scripture?
?
You are a created being in time. The Quran's createdness means it shares your temporality. How does that reality strip away the comfort of thinking of the Quran as an eternal, untouchable ideal? Are you willing to submit to its timely guidance right now?
?
The proof shows that any temporal relation — even a short precedence — proves createdness. Are you living as if the Quran is a timeless artifact to be admired, or as a fresh message that demands immediate obedience? What specific action will you take today to honor its timely authority?

Second Objection: Musa Asked for a Sign of the Hour
Refutes the ta'wil that Musa asked for a sign of the Hour, not seeing Allah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the interpretation that Musa asked for a sign of the Hour rather than to see Allah is invalid according to the text.

Core Idea
If Allah said 'lan tarani', He negated seeing Himself, not a sign.

This lesson unpacks a subtle objection raised by al-Ka'bi and the Baghdadis, who claimed that Musa's request 'Show me, I look at You' was actually an ellipsis for 'Show me Your signs of the Hour.' While ingenious, this interpretation collapses under the divine response 'lan tarani,' which clearly denies seeing Allah Himself. We'll see why the author rejects it as contrary to the apparent meaning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the passage to grasp the claim: Musa didn't ask for God's essence but for a sign of the Hour, with an ellipsis (حذف المضاف).
2
Identify the author's first refutation
Notice that the author says the interpretation is against the apparent meaning and requires evidence.
The principle: لا يرتكب خلاف الظاهر إلا بدليل
3
Analyze the second refutation
Focus on the phrase 'lan tarani': it negates seeing God, not a sign. If Musa had asked for a sign, the negation would be about the sign, not God.
This is the key argument.
4
Consider the textual variant
Some manuscripts read 'signs indicating His essence' instead of 'the Hour'. The author notes this but prefers the context.
Shows depth of scholarly analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الثاني ) من وجوه الاعتراض على الأول ( أنه ) لم يسأله إراءة ذاته بل ( سأل أن يريه علماً ) وإمارة ( من أعلامه وإماراته ( الدالة على الساعة، وتقدير الكلام أنظر إلى علمك ) فحذف المضاف وأقام المضاف إليه مقامه ) فقال أنظر إليك ( نحو واسأل القرية أي أهلها فتكون الرؤية المطلوبة متعلقة بالعلم أيضاً، والمعنى أرني أعلامك أنظر إلى علمك ) وهذا تأويل الكعبي والبغداديين والجواب أنه خلاف الظاهر فلا يرتكب إلا لدليل ( و ) مع ذلك ( لا يستقيم أما أولا فلقوله : لن تراني ) ، فإنه نفي لرؤيته تعالى لا لرؤية علم من أعلام الساعة بإجماعهم فلا يطابق قوله : (من أعلامه الدالة على الساعة وفي بعض النسخ من أعلامه الدالة على ذاته وهو المناسب لما في نهاية العقول وإن كان الظاهر من السياق والموافق للأبكار هو الأولى
The second aspect of objection to the first [proof]: that he did not ask for showing His essence, but rather he asked that He show him knowledge and a sign from His signs and indications indicating the Hour. And the estimation of the speech is: 'look at Your knowledge' — so the added [word] was deleted and the addressee was put in its place, so he said 'look at You', like 'and ask the village' i.e. its people. So the requested vision is also related to knowledge, and the meaning is: show me Your signs that I may look at Your knowledge. This is the interpretation of al-Ka'bi and the Baghdadis. The answer: that it is contrary to the apparent, so it is not undertaken except with evidence. And despite that, it is not sound: first because of His saying 'lan tarani', for it is a negation of vision of Him, Exalted, not of the vision of a sign from the signs of the Hour, by their consensus, so it does not match. His saying: 'from His signs indicating the Hour' — and in some copies 'from His signs indicating His essence', which is more appropriate for what is in Nihayat al-'Uqul, even if the apparent from the context and that which agrees with al-Abkar is the first.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection to the first proof, attributed to al-Ka'bi and the Baghdadis. They claim that Musa (alayh al-salam) did not ask to see Allah's essence, but rather asked for a sign of the Hour, with the phrase 'arini anzur ilayk' being an ellipsis for 'arini a'lamaka anzur ila 'ilmika' (show me Your signs that I may look at Your knowledge). The author refutes this by noting that it contradicts the apparent meaning, which requires evidence, and more importantly, the divine response 'lan tarani' negates seeing God Himself, not a sign, and by consensus this negation does not match the supposed request. The gloss at the end notes a variant reading in some manuscripts: 'signs indicating His essence' rather than 'the Hour', which is more consistent with other sources like Nihayat al-'Uqul, but the context favors the first reading.

Key Terms
الرؤية
أعلام الساعة
حذف المضاف
الظاهر
لن تراني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Musa's request 'arini anzur ilayk' was really a request to see signs of the Hour.
Correction: The response 'lan tarani' clearly negates seeing Allah Himself, so the request must have been for seeing Allah. The ellipsis interpretation fails because it would require a different negation.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the author rejects the interpretation that Musa asked for a sign of the Hour?
A) Because Musa could not ask for a sign of a future event.
B) Because the response 'lan tarani' negates seeing God Himself, not a sign.
C) Because al-Ka'bi was not a reliable scholar.
D) Because the request would be blasphemous.

Key Takeaway
The divine negation 'lan tarani' proves that Musa's request was to see Allah Himself, not a sign of the Hour.

The objection tried to reinterpret clear words using a grammatical trick to avoid a theological difficulty. The author insists on sticking to the apparent meaning unless there is decisive evidence. Refining the Definition of Temporality
Commentary refines the definition of temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of temporality (al-ḥudūth) as existence after non-existence, and the implication for the object of divine vision.

Core Idea
Temporality is not prior non-existence but existence after non-existence.

The commentary refines the definition of temporality, moving from a negative notion (prior non-existence) to a positive relational one (existence after non-existence). This subtle shift has implications for what is the object of vision: is it existence itself or the state of being preceded by non-existence? The passage leaves this as an open question.

Guided Path
1
Read the original commentary
Read the Arabic text and literal translation to grasp the core critique and the proposed definition.
2
Understand the refinement
Identify why 'temporality as prior non-existence' is insufficient, and why 'existence after non-existence' is preferred. The commentary also rejects the sum of existence and non-existence.
3
Explore the implication
Consider the question raised: if temporality is existence after non-existence, then the attachment of vision is existence. But why can't it be the 'being preceded by non-existence'? Reflect on the nuance.
This leads to deeper reflection on the attribute of temporality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ليس الحدوث هو العدم السابق الأولى أن يضم إليه ولا مجموع الوجود اللاحق والعدم السابق بقي ينبغي أن يقال: الحدوث هو الوجود بعد العدم، فتعلق الرؤية هو الوجود لكن لم لا يجوز أن يكون المسبوقية بالعدم؟
His statement: 'Temporality is not the prior non-existence'—it would be better to add to it 'nor the sum of the subsequent existence and the prior non-existence.' It remains that one should say: Temporality is existence after non-existence, so the attachment of vision is existence. But why is it not permissible that it is the being preceded by non-existence?
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (قوله) on the fifth objection's answer. It critiques the phrasing that temporality is prior non-existence, suggesting instead that it should be defined as existence after non-existence (or the sum of existence and non-existence). The comment then raises a question: if temporality is existence after non-existence, then the object of vision is existence itself. But why can't the object be the 'being preceded by non-existence'? This is left as a point for consideration.

Key Terms
الحدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Temporality is simply prior non-existence (al-ʿadam al-sābiq).
Correction: Temporality is existence after non-existence, not the prior non-existence alone. The prior non-existence is a condition, not the definition.

Checkpoint

B) The sum of subsequent existence and prior non-existence
C) Existence after non-existence
D) The mere fact of being preceded

Key Takeaway
Temporality is existence after non-existence, not prior non-existence, and this distinction affects theological discussions on divine vision.

?
If temporality is defined as existence after non-existence, then every moment you exist is a gift. Are you using your time as a gift to please the One who gave you existence? What is one concrete action you will take today to show gratitude for your existence after non-existence?

The True Nature of the Commonality of Existence
Al-Jurjani clarifies the type of verbal commonality posited by deniers of absolute existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meaning of 'verbal commonality' (ishtirak lafẓi) as used by the deniers of absolute existence, and how it differs from conventional homonymy.

Core Idea
The commonality of existence is not mere homonymy but a special case of general coinage with particular coinage-for.

Al-Jurjani addresses an important nuance in the debate over the concept of existence. Some thinkers deny a single absolute existence and claim that the word 'existence' is only verbally common. But what do they mean by 'verbal commonality'? This passage clarifies that they do not mean pure homonymy (multiple unrelated meanings), because that would be absurd. Instead, they mean a subtle coinage structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's words set the stage.
Pay attention to the technical terms: اشتراك لفظي, الوضع العام, etc.
2
Identify what is NOT meant
The passage first refutes the idea that the deniers mean conventional verbal commonality with multiple coinings. Why would that be absurd?
They would have to claim a separate coining for each existent, which is stubborn denial (mukābara).
3
Understand the positive claim
The deniers say the word 'existence' is applied to each existent without a common meaning. This is explained as general coinage (waḍʿ ʿāmm) with specified coinage-for (khuṣūṣ al-mawḍūʿ lahu).
The word is coined for a general concept but assigned to each particular individually.
4
Reflect on the application-based commonality
The second gloss clarifies that the commonality is only in terms of what the word applies to (ma ṣadaqa ʿalayhi)—not a single concept in the mind.
This distinguishes their view from conceptual commonality (ishtirak maʿnawī).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فوجب أن يكون الاشتراك في الوجود عندكم ليس اشتراك الوجود بين الوجودات عند النافين للوجود المطلق اشتراكاً لفظياً بالمعنى المتعارف إذ دعوى تعدد الأوضاع بحسب تعدد الموجودات مكابرة بل مرادهم بالاشتراك اللفظي أن لفظ الوجود هو المشترك بينهما، أي يطلق على كل منهما بدون أن يكون هناك معنى مشترك فوضع لفظ الوجود بإزاء تلك الوجودات من قبيل الوضع العام مع خصوص الموضوع له كما حقق في نظائره . قوله : ( إنما هو باعتبار ما صدقا عليه بمعنى أن ما صدق عليه أحدهما صدق عليه الآخر كما صرح به المصنف
His saying: (Thus it follows that the commonality of existence in your view is not a commonality of existence among existents according to the deniers of absolute existence as a verbal commonality in the conventional sense, for the claim of multiple coinings according to the multiplicity of existents is a stubborn denial, but rather their intent by verbal commonality is that the word 'existence' is common between them, i.e., it is applied to each without there being a common meaning. So the coinage of the word 'existence' for those existents is of the type of general coinage with a specified coinage-for, as has been verified in its parallels. His saying: (It is only with regard to what they apply to, meaning that what one applies to also applies to the other, as the author stated.)
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a gloss explaining the type of commonality attributed to existence by those who deny absolute existence (al-nafun li al-wujud al-mutlaq). It clarifies that they do not mean pure homonymy (ishtirak lafẓi) in the conventional sense, where the same word has multiple unrelated meanings, because that would require multiple coinings (awḍaʿ) for each existent—which is absurd. Rather, they mean that the word 'existence' is applied to each existent individually without there being a single common meaning shared by all. This is a case of general coinage with a specified coinage-for (al-waḍʿ al-ʿamm maʿ khuṣuṣ al-mawḍuʿ lahu), where the word is coined for each individual instance separately but one meaning covers all? Actually, it's subtle: the gloss states that the coinage is general (the word is placed for a general concept) but the coinage-for is particular (each existent). The second gloss confirms that the commonality is only in terms of what the word applies to (ma ṣadaqa ʿalayhi), meaning that the word applies to many, not that there is a single concept in the mind. However, the author (al-Iji) holds that existence is conceptually common, so this gloss explains the opposing view.

Key Terms
اشتراك لفظي
اشتراك معنوي
الوجود المطلق
الوضع العام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'verbal commonality' (ishtirak lafẓi) always means pure homonymy with no conceptual connection whatsoever.
Correction: In this context, the deniers of absolute existence use 'verbal commonality' to mean that the word 'existence' is applied to each existent individually without a single common meaning, but via a specific coinage structure (general coinage with particular coinage-for).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do the deniers of absolute existence mean by 'verbal commonality' (ishtirak lafẓi) of the word 'existence'?
A) That the word has multiple unrelated meanings
B) That the word is applied to each existent without a single common meaning shared by all
C) That the word is coined separately for each existent with different coinages
D) That the word has a single meaning but applies to many

Key Takeaway
The deniers of absolute existence mean by 'verbal commonality' that the word 'existence' is applied to each existent without a single common meaning, via general coinage with particular coinage-for.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage warns against 'mukaabara'—stubborn denial. Is there any area of your faith where you are insisting on your own understanding without seriously examining the nuanced positions of scholars? What would it take for you to submit to the truth, even when it challenges your intellectual comfort or pride?
?
If the word 'existence' applies to you and to your Creator only by way of general coinage (as some claim), does that thought shake the tawheed in your heart, or does it deepen your sense that He alone is the source of existence? How do you actively cultivate reliance on Him as the ultimate Reality?
?
Consider your own existence: you are a particular instance of a general concept. Have you ever thanked Allah for creating you as a specific individual, with your unique soul and circumstances? Or do you take your existence for granted, as if it were merely a label? What specific action can you take today to show gratitude for the existence He has given you?

Refuting the Claim of Breaking Consensus
Sanusi refutes Razi's argument that a third position would break consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why affirming possibility while denying occurrence does not break consensus, and how a detailed position (tafsil) can be compatible with existing views.

Core Idea
A detailed position that agrees partly with each side does not break consensus.

Imam al-Razi argued that if we affirm that seeing Allah is possible but deny that it will occur, that would be a third opinion breaking consensus. Sanusi refutes this by clarifying what truly counts as breaking consensus. He shows that a detailed position (tafsil) that agrees with each group on one part does not violate consensus. This lesson unpacks that refutation and the logical distinctions involved.

Guided Path
1
Understand Razi's argument
Read the first part where Razi presents his case for why a third position would break consensus.
Pay attention to the claim that possibility and occurrence must go together.
2
Identify the refutation's core logic
Sanusi states that not every new opinion breaks consensus. He distinguishes between affirming/negating universal vs. particular propositions.
Notice the examples of universal affirmative, etc.
3
Examine the 'tafsil' concept
The third position is a detailed position (tafsil) that agrees with one group on possibility and another on non-occurrence.
This is key to why it does not break consensus.
4
Consider the analogy
Sanusi provides an analogy from fiqh: killing a Muslim for a dhimmi. See how the same logic applies.
This reinforces the point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني: في وقوع الرؤية أن المؤمنين سيرون ربهم يوم القيامة قال الإمام الرازي مستدلاً على وقوع الرؤية الأمة في هذه المسألة على قولين فقط الأول يصح ويرى والثاني لا يرى ولا يصح وقد أثبتنا أنه يصح فلو قلنا مع القول بالصحة أنه لا يرى لكان قولاً ثالثاً خارقاً للإجماع على عدم الافتراق بين الصحة والوقوع في النفي والإثبات بل كلاهما مثبتان معا أو منفيان معا وهو غير صحيح كما ذكره الآمدي لأن خرق الإجماع إثبات ما نفاه كما إذا ذهب بعض المجمعين إلى السالبة الكلية وآخرون إلى السالبة الجزئية فأحدث القول بالموجبة الكلية أو نفي ما أثبته كما إذا ذهب بعضهم إلى الموجبة الكلية وبعضهم إلى الموجبة الجزئية فأحدث القول بالسالبة الكلية وأما إذا ذهب بعضهم إلى الموجبة الكلية وآخرون إلى السالبة الكلية فإحداث القول بالموجبة الجزئية والسالبة الجزئية معاً ليس خارقاً للإجماع إذ ليس بين القولين قدر مشترك بل هو تفصيل وموافقة لطائفة في إحدى المسألتين والأخرى في أخرى كما فيما نحن بصدده وإليه أشار بقوله وهذا القول الثالث إنما هو التفصيل وهو القول بالجواز والقول بعدم الوقوع وشيء منهما لا يخالف الإجماع ولا يخرقه بل كل واحد من قولي التفصيل مما قال به طائفة من طائفتي المجمعين وإن كان خارقاً لما قال به الطائفة الأخرى وذلك الذي ذكرناه في مسألتنا هذه كما في مسألة قتل المسلم بالذمي والحر بالعبد فإن القائل قائلان مثبت لهما معاً كالحنفية وناف لهما معاً كالشافعية والتفصيل بينهما مما لم يقل به أحد من الأمة ولكن لو قيل به لا يكون خارقاً للإجماع بل موافقة للمثبت في مسألة وللنافي في مسألة أخرى ولا يكون هذا التفصيل ممنوعاً عنه بل يكون جائزاً بالإجماع فهذا المسلك في إثبات الوقوع مردود
The second station: on the occurrence of vision [ru'ya], that the believers will see their Lord on the Day of Resurrection. Imam al-Razi said, arguing for the occurrence of vision: 'The community in this matter is upon two opinions only: the first is that it is possible and will occur, and the second is that it will not occur and is not possible. We have proven that it is possible. So if we say, together with the statement of possibility, that it will not occur, that would be a third opinion breaking the consensus that there is no distinction between possibility and occurrence in negation and affirmation; rather both are affirmed together or negated together.' This (i.e., this argument) is not correct, as al-Amidi mentioned, because breaking consensus is affirming what has been negated, as when some of those forming consensus go to universal negative and others to particular negative, and then someone produces the statement of universal affirmative, or negating what has been affirmed, as when some go to universal affirmative and some to particular affirmative, and then someone produces the statement of universal negative. But when some go to universal affirmative and others to universal negative, then producing the statement of particular affirmative and particular negative together is not breaking consensus, for there is no common ground between the two statements; rather it is a detailed position [tafsil] and agreement with one group in one of the two issues and with the other in another issue, as in what we are discussing. And to this he alludes by his saying: 'This third statement is only the detailed position, which is the statement of possibility and the statement of non-occurrence; neither of them contradicts consensus nor breaks it. Rather each one of the two statements of the detailed position is something said by a group of the two groups forming consensus, even if it breaks what the other group said.' And that which we have mentioned in our issue here is like the issue of killing a Muslim for a dhimmi and a free man for a slave: there are two speakers, one affirming both together like the Hanafis, and one negating both together like the Shafi'is, and the detailed position between them is something none of the community has said, but if it were said, it would not break consensus; rather it would be agreement with the affirmer in one issue and with the negator in another issue. Nor is this detailed position prohibited; rather it is permissible by consensus. So this method [maslak] in proving occurrence is rejected.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage refutes an argument by Imam al-Razi that affirming the possibility of ru'ya (seeing Allah) while denying its occurrence would constitute a third opinion that breaks the consensus of the community. The author explains that true consensus-breaking requires asserting a universal positive or universal negative that contradicts both existing positions simultaneously. In this case, the two existing positions are: (1) it is possible and will occur, and (2) it is not possible and will not occur. A third position that says 'it is possible but will not occur' is not a simple third alternative; it is a detailed position (tafsil) that agrees with the first group on possibility and with the second group on non-occurrence. Therefore, it does not break consensus. The author provides an analogy: the ruling on killing a Muslim in retaliation for a dhimmi or a free man for a slave. There are two positions: one affirms both (Hanafis), the other negates both (Shafi'is). A detailed position that differentiates between them, even if not previously stated, would not break consensus because it agrees with each group on part of the issue. The author concludes that this particular method of proving the occurrence of ru'ya is invalid.

Key Terms
إجماع
تفصيل
رؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any new opinion not explicitly held by earlier scholars automatically breaks consensus.
Correction: A detailed position (tafsil) that agrees with each group on part of the issue does not break consensus because it does not contradict either group's full position.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the third position (affirming possibility but denying occurrence) not break consensus?
A) Because it is a detailed position that agrees with each group on one part
B) Because it is held by a minority
C) Because the first two positions are not agreed upon
D) Because consensus only applies to fundamental matters

Key Takeaway
A new opinion that agrees with each existing group on part of the issue does not break consensus; it is a permissible detailed position (tafsil).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own interactions, have you ever dismissed someone’s nuanced opinion as 'breaking consensus' without carefully examining whether it truly contradicts an agreed-upon universal? How might this lesson change your approach to disagreeing with others on matters of religion or even everyday life? Take a moment to recall a specific instance and consider how you could have applied the principle of tafsīl to listen more carefully before judging.
?
This lesson shows that a position which agrees with each side on different parts can be valid even if it is new. Is there a belief or practice you have rejected because it seemed novel, even though it actually affirmed parts of what you already hold? Ask Allah to help you distinguish between what truly breaks from the path of the salaf and what is merely a legitimate detailed position that you have not encountered before.
?
Sanusi uses an analogy from fiqh (killing a Muslim for a dhimmi) to clarify a theological principle. Are you willing to be taught about Allah’s attributes through rigorous logical reasoning, even when it challenges your initial understanding? Commit to studying the proofs behind your beliefs with humility, trusting that the scholars who engaged in kalam did so to protect the deen, not to confuse it.

Proof from the Verse: Looking at the Lord
Sanusi presents a linguistic proof for ru'ya from the Quranic verse using the preposition 'ila'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses the linguistic rule that 'nazar' with 'ila' means vision to prove ru'ya Allah from the Quranic verse.

Core Idea
The preposition 'ila' after 'nazar' is the key to proving the vision of Allah.

Sanusi begins his proof for the vision of Allah by turning to the Quran. The verse 'Wujuhun yawma'idhin nadiratun ila rabbiha naziratun' contains the word 'nazar' connected with 'ila'. In Arabic, the meaning of 'nazar' changes depending on the preposition used. Sanusi systematically lists these meanings and shows that only one fits the context. This lesson walks through his linguistic argument and its theological conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the source text
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Sanusi lists different meanings of 'nazar' with examples.
Pay attention to the prepositions used for each meaning.
2
Identify the key linguistic point
Focus on the prepositions that accompany 'nazar' for each meaning. Which preposition appears in the verse?
3
Understand the conclusion
Why does the presence of 'ila' force the meaning of vision? Reflect on how this linguistic rule leads to the proof of ru'ya.
The verse uses 'ila', so it must mean vision, not waiting or reflection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتمد فيه مسلكان: المسلك الأول قوله تعالى: {وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة} وجه الاحتجاج بالآية الكريمة أن النظر في اللغة جاء بمعنى الانتظار ويستعمل بغير صلة بل يتعدى بنفسه قال تعالى: {انظرونا نقتبس من نوركم} أي انتظرونا وقال: {وما ينظرون إلا صيحة واحدة} أي ما ينتظرون ومنه قوله تعالى: {فناظرة بم يرجع المرسلون} أي منتظرة وقول الشاعر: وإن يك صدر هذا اليوم ولى فإن غداً لناظره قريب أي لمنتظره وجاء بمعنى التفكر والاعتبار ويستعمل حينئذ بفي يقال: نظرت في الأمر الفلاني أي تفكرت واعتبرت وجاء بمعنى الرأفة والتعطف ويستعمل حينئذ باللام يقال: نظر الأمير لفلان أي رأف به وتعطف وجاء بمعنى الرؤية ويستعمل بإلى قال الشاعر: نظرت إلى من أحسن الله وجهه ففي نظرة كادت على وامق يقضي والنظر في الآية موصول بإلى فوجب حمله على الرؤية فتكون واقعة في ذلك اليوم وهو المطلوب
And the relied-upon method in it are two methods: the first method is His saying, the Most High: {Faces on that day are radiant, looking at their Lord}. The aspect of argumentation from the noble verse is that 'nazar' (looking) in the language has come to mean waiting, and it is used without a preposition, rather it is transitive by itself. He said, the Most High: {Look at us so that we may borrow from your light}, i.e., wait for us. And He said: {And they wait not but for a single cry}, i.e., they do not wait. And from it is His saying, the Most High: {So she is waiting with what the messengers will return}, i.e., waiting. And the saying of the poet: 'And if the first part of this day has passed, then tomorrow to its waiter is near', i.e., to its waiter. And it has come to mean reflection and consideration, and it is then used with 'fi'; one says: 'I looked into such and such matter', i.e., I reflected and considered. And it has come to mean mercy and compassion, and it is then used with 'lam'; one says: 'The prince looked at so-and-so', i.e., he had mercy on him and showed compassion. And it has come to mean vision and is used with 'ila'. The poet said: 'I looked at one whose face God has made beautiful, and it was a look that almost decreed the death of a lover.' And 'nazar' in the verse is connected with 'ila', so it must be interpreted as vision, and thus it occurs on that day, and that is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents the first and primary proof for the occurrence of ru'ya (seeing Allah) from the Quranic verse: {Faces on that day are radiant, looking at their Lord} (Qiyamah 75:22-23). The author conducts a detailed linguistic analysis of the word 'nazar'. He first enumerates its various meanings: 1) waiting, used without a preposition (e.g., Quranic examples); 2) reflection/consideration, used with 'fi'; 3) mercy/compassion, used with 'lam'; 4) physical vision, used with 'ila'. Since the verse uses the preposition 'ila' with 'nazar', the meaning must be vision, not waiting or any other meaning. The author supports this with a line of poetry where 'nazara ila' clearly means 'looked at'. Therefore, the verse proves that believers will see their Lord on that Day, which is the desired conclusion.

Key Terms
نظر
رؤية
إلى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'nazar' in the verse means 'waiting' because it can mean that in other contexts.
Correction: The presence of 'ila' forces the meaning of vision; waiting requires no preposition or sometimes other prepositions, but not 'ila'.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue about the word 'nazara' in the verse 'Wujuhun yawma'idhin nadiratun ila rabbiha naziratun'?
A) It means waiting
B) It means thinking
C) It means seeing
D) It means mercy

Key Takeaway
The use of 'ila' after 'nazar' in the verse proves that believers will see Allah with their eyes on the Day of Judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly long for the vision of Allah on that Day, or are you content with just knowing about it? What in your daily prayers shows your desire to see Him?
?
When you read the Quran, do you pause to ponder the precise meanings of words like 'nazar' and the prepositions that follow them, or do you settle for a surface understanding?
?
If you were to stand before Allah today, would your face be radiant with hope of seeing Him? What one change can you make this week to live in expectation of that moment?
