Refuting Two Objections to the Verse Proof
Sanusi addresses two objections to the verse proof and provides responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main objections raised against the verse proof for the vision of Allah and how Sanusi refutes each using Arabic grammar, semantics, and rhetoric.

Core Idea
Objections to the verse proof fail when examined with precise linguistic tools.

Sanusi anticipates two objections that might be raised against his proof from the verse 'unto their Lord looking'. The first is a grammatical objection claiming that 'ila' is not a preposition but a noun meaning 'blessings'. The second is a logical objection concerning the implication of restriction from the fronted word order. By addressing these, Sanusi solidifies the proof and shows the coherence of the Ash'ari position on the beatific vision.

Guided Path
1
First objection and its refutation
Read the first objection: 'We do not concede that 'ila' is a preposition for 'nazar'; rather it is one of the blessings (ala') and 'nazar' means waiting.' Then follow Sanusi's answer: the word 'ila' in the verse indicates a period, as in the Quranic verse {Then a deferment to easy circumstances}. This shows the objection is based on a misunderstanding.
Note the use of a parenthetical clarification to strengthen the refutation.
2
Second objection and its refutation
Read the second objection: fronting the object implies restriction (hasr), which would mean 'they see none but Allah', yet they see other things. Then follow the answer: the fronting here is for emphasis (ihtimam) and to maintain the rhyme (fasiqa), not for restriction.
This demonstrates the importance of rhetorical context in interpreting Quranic word order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض عليه بوجوه الأول: لا نسلم أن لفظ إلى صلة للنظر بل هو واحد الآلاء ومفعول به للنظر بمعنى الانتظار فمعنى الآية نعمة ربها منتظرة ومنه قول الشاعر: قوله: (ويستعمل بإلى إلخ) فإن قلت: قد يستعمل النظر بها كما في قوله تعالى: {فنظرة إلى ميسرة} مع أن النظر ها هنا بمعنى الإمهال والتسويف قلت: لفظة إلى في الآية الكريمة ليست صلة للنظر بل لبيان المدة. قوله: (على وامق يقضي) قضى عليه أي قبله والوامق من المقة وهي المحبة والهاء عوض من الواو، ومقه يمقه بالكسر فيهما أي أحبه فهو وامق وما قيل من أنه اسم عاشق معروف يكنى به عن المطلق كالحاتم عن الجواد لا أعرف له وجه صحة. إذ بعد تسليم تأخر الشاعر عن الوامق المشهور لا وجه للمصير إليه بعد ما ثبت أن المكنى عنه يزعمه معنى حقيقي للفظ إن قامت العشق فرط الحب لأنفسه. قلت: درجات الحب متفاوتة والعشق درجة منها. قوله: (فوجب حمله على الرؤية إلخ) فإن قلت: تقديم المفعول يفيد الحصر كما في قوله تعالى: {ألا إلى الله تصير الأمور} ونظائره فلو حمل على الرؤية لدل على أنهم لا يرون غير الله تعالى مع أنهم يرون الجنة والنار ومواقف القيامة فوجب حمله على الأنظار ولا محذور حينئذ لأنهم لا ينظرون سوى رحمة ربهم. قلت: التقديم هاهنا للاهتمام ورعاية الفاصلة
And it was objected to with aspects: The first: We do not concede that the word 'ila' is a preposition for 'nazar'; rather it is one of the blessings (ala') and an object of 'nazar' in the sense of waiting, so the meaning of the verse is: 'waiting for the blessing of their Lord'. And from it is the saying of the poet: His saying: (And it is used with 'ila' etc.) For you might say: It is sometimes used with 'ila' as in His saying, the Most High: {Then a deferment to easy circumstances}, although 'nazar' here means respiting and postponing. I say: The word 'ila' in the noble verse is not a preposition for 'nazar' but to indicate the period. His saying: (on a lover decrees) 'qada' upon him means he judged against him; 'al-wamiq' is from 'al-miqah' which is love; the 'ha' is a substitute for the 'waw'; 'maqahu yamaquhu' with kasra in both means he loved him, so he is 'wamiq'. And what has been said that it is the name of a famous lover used as a metonym for the absolute, like 'Hatim' for the generous, I do not know its validity. For, after conceding the lateness of the poet from the famous 'Wamiq', there is no reason to resort to it after it is established that the metonym is claimed to be a real meaning for the word, if love is extreme love for itself. I say: The degrees of love are varied and love ('ishq) is a degree of it. His saying: (So it must be interpreted as vision etc.) For you might say: Fronting the object indicates restriction, as in His saying, the Most High: {Surely to Allah (alone) is the return of affairs} and its likes. So if it is interpreted as vision, it would indicate that they see none but Allah, while they see Paradise, Hellfire, and stations of the Resurrection. Therefore, it must be interpreted as 'looking' (intizar) and there is no difficulty then because they look only at the mercy of their Lord. I say: The fronting here is for emphasis and for the sake of the rhyme.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents two objections to the previous proof from the verse and their respective answers. The first objection claims that the word 'ila' in the verse is not a preposition but a noun (ala', meaning blessings) and that 'nazara' means waiting, so the verse means 'waiting for the blessing of their Lord'. The answer, given in a parenthetical note, explains that when 'nazara' is used with 'ila' in the sense of waiting, it can still take 'ila' as a preposition to indicate a period, as in the verse {Then a deferment to easy circumstances}. Thus, the objection is not valid. Before the second objection, there is a digression clarifying the meaning of 'wamiq' (lover) in the poetic citation, emphasizing that it simply means 'lover' and not a proper name. The second objection concerns the word order: the fronting of the object (ila rabbiha) might imply restriction (hasr), meaning they see only their Lord, but they also see other things. The answer is that the fronting here is for emphasis (ihtimam) and to maintain the rhyme (fasiqa) of the verse, not for restriction. Therefore, both objections are refuted.

Key Terms
آلاء
حصر
نظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the word 'ila' in the vision verse is a noun meaning 'blessings' rather than a preposition.
Correction: Linguistic analysis of Quranic usage shows that 'ila' with 'nazar' in the sense of waiting indicates a period, as in Qur'an 2:280, so the objection is invalid.

Checkpoint
What is the second objection mentioned in the passage?
A) That 'ila' is a noun meaning blessings
B) That the word order implies only God is seen
C) That the verse is abrogated
D) That the verse refers to the Day of Judgment

Key Takeaway
Sanusi defends the verse proof for the beatific vision by skillfully refuting grammatical and logical objections, showing that proper understanding of Arabic usage eliminates doubts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever relied on a surface-level understanding of a Quranic word that led you to misinterpret a verse, only to later discover your error? How does Sanusi's meticulous linguistic analysis challenge you to approach Allah's words with greater humility and precision starting today?
?
When someone objects to your beliefs, do you quickly retort with familiar responses without deep understanding, or do you, like Sanusi, examine the objection carefully and address it with solid evidence? Which approach will you adopt from now on, and what specific objection will you prepare for?
?
This lesson shows that even a single letter (ila) can be contested by skeptics. Are you willing to invest time in learning Arabic grammar and rhetoric to defend your faith, or will you remain content with blind following? What is one concrete step you can take this week to master a linguistic tool that strengthens your conviction in a Quranic verse?

The Eight Conditions for Vision
Enumeration of eight conditions for vision with a ninth condition note.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eight conditions necessary for vision as enumerated in the text, including the commentary's mention of a ninth condition.

Core Idea
Vision depends on eight conditions that ensure proper perception.

This passage from the commentary lists the classic Ash'ari conditions for visual perception. The author enumerates eight matters that must be present for sight to occur, and then notes a ninth condition related to luminosity. We will examine each condition and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Begin by reading the Arabic text from the beginning up to 'المعتبرة في الشرط الثاني'. This covers the eight conditions and the note about the ninth condition.
2
Identify each condition
Look for the eight numbered items: soundness of sense, permissibility of vision, opposition, absence of extreme smallness, absence of extreme subtlety, absence of extreme distance, absence of extreme closeness, and absence of an obstructing veil.
Each condition is introduced by a ordinal number in the Arabic.
3
Understand the ninth condition
Notice the statement that there is a ninth condition – being luminous – and the commentary’s suggestion that it can be included under the second condition.
This shows the author's awareness of a more complete list.
4
Reflect on the logic
For each condition, consider why it is necessary. For example, why must the object not be extremely close? Because it would touch the eye and prevent perception.
The conditions are based on empirical observation and rational reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثمانية أمور : الأول : ( سلامة الحاسة ) ولذلك تختلف مراتب الإبصار بحسب اختلاف سلامة الإبصار وتنتفي بانتفائها . (و) الثاني: (كون الشيء جائز الرؤية مع حضوره للحاسة بأن تكون الحاسة متلفتة إليه ولم يعرض هناك ما يضاد الإدراك كالنوم والغفلة والتوجه إلى شيء آخر . ( و ) الثالث : ( مقابلته ) للباصرة في جهة من الجهات أو كونه في حكم المقابلة كما في المرئي بالمرآة . ( و ) الرابع ) عدم غاية الصغر فإن الصغير جداً لا يدركه البصر قطعاً . (و) الخامس : ( عدم غاية اللطافه ) بأن يكون كثيفاً أي ذا لون في الجملة وإن كان ضعيفاً. (و) السادس: ( عدم غاية البعد ) وهو مختلف بحسب قوة الباصرة وضعفها . ( و) السابع عدم غاية ( القرب ) فإن المبصر إذا التصق بسطح البصر بطل إدراكه بالكلية. (و) الثامن: ) عدم الحجاب الحائل ) وهو الجسم الملون المتوسط بينهما وهناك شرط تاسع هو أن يكون مضيئاً بذاته أو بغيره ولم يذكره هاهنا لكونه مذكوراً في بحث الكيفيات المبصرة. مع أنه يمكن إدراجه في حضوره للحاسة المعتبرة في الشرط الثاني ( ثم لا قوله : (ثمانية أمور أي شرائطها على ما ذكره المصنف هاهنا ثمانية وإلا فالتحقيق أنها عشرة على ما نقل من الشارح واعترض على انحصار الشرائط فيما ذكر بأنا نرى ذرات الغبار عند اجتماعها ولا نراها عند تفرقها مع حصول الشرائط المذكورة في الحالتين لا يقال : بل ذلك لانتفاع شرط الكثافة وتحقق مانع الصغر، لأنا نقول : فحينئذ يكون رؤية كل ذرة مشروطة بانضمام الأخرى إليها وهو دور وأجيب بأنه دور معية لا تقدم كذا في لباب الأربعين وشرح المقاصد ويمكن أن يجاب بمنع الدور، فإن رؤية كل ذرة يتوقف على انضمام الأخرى ولم يلزم منهما مما ذكره هذا وفي رؤية كل واحدة من الذرات ولو عند الاجتماع كلام ستطلع عليه
Eight matters: first: soundness of the sense; therefore the levels of sight vary according to the variation in soundness of sight and are nullified by its nullification. Second: the thing being permissible of vision while present to the sense, such that the sense is directed to it and nothing opposes perception like sleep, heedlessness, and attention to something else. Third: its being opposite the seer in some direction or being in the ruling of opposition like in the case of the seen in a mirror. Fourth: absence of extreme smallness, for a very small thing is certainly not perceived by sight. Fifth: absence of extreme subtlety, such that it is dense, i.e., having a color in general even if weak. Sixth: absence of extreme distance, and it varies according to the strength and weakness of the seer. Seventh: absence of extreme closeness, for if the object of sight is attached to the surface of the eye, its perception is completely nullified. Eighth: absence of an obstructing veil, which is the colored body intermediate between them. And there is a ninth condition: that it be luminous by itself or by another, but he did not mention it here because it is mentioned in the discussion of visible qualities. Yet it can be included in its presence to the sense considered in the second condition. Then he said: (Eight matters) i.e., its conditions according to what the author mentioned here are eight, but the verification is that they are ten according to what is transmitted from the commentator. And an objection was raised against the exclusivity of the conditions to what was mentioned: we see dust particles when they are together and do not see them when they are scattered, while the mentioned conditions are present in both cases. It cannot be said: Rather that is due to the absence of the condition of density and the existence of the impediment of smallness, for we say: Then the vision of each particle would be conditioned by the joining of another to it, which is a circularity. And it was answered that it is a circularity of simultaneity, not priority, as in Lubab al-Arba'in and Sharh al-Maqasid. And it is possible to answer by denying the circularity, for the vision of each particle depends on the joining of another, and nothing follows from that. And there is further discussion on the vision of each one of the particles, even when they are together, which you will come across.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage first enumerates the eight conditions for vision as stated in the matn, then the commentary explains that according to the author these are eight, but in fact they are ten. Then an objection is raised: we see dust particles when they are together but not when they are scattered, even though the conditions are met. The answer is that for each dust particle, vision requires the joining of another particle, which involves a circularity. This circularity is answered as a circularity of simultaneity, not priority. An alternative answer is to deny the circularity. The passage ends noting there is further discussion on seeing each particle individually.

شرائط الرؤية
سلامة الحاسة
جواز الرؤية
مقابلة
غاية الصغر
غاية اللطافة
غاية البعد
غاية القرب
حجاب حائل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the eight conditions are complete and sufficient for vision.
Correction: The commentary points out that there is a ninth condition (luminosity) and verification says they are actually ten conditions.

Checkpoint
How many conditions does the passage explicitly enumerate before mentioning the ninth?
A) 7
B) 8
C) 9
D) 10

Key Takeaway
The eight conditions cover sense health, attentive presence, spatial orientation, and absence of physical extremes that would prevent perception, with luminosity implied.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the eight conditions for vision do you most neglect when seeking to understand the truth of your faith? For example, do you avoid 'extreme distance' by getting close to scholars, or do you allow 'veils' of bias to block your understanding? Commit to removing one veil this week.
?
The author notes that an object must be luminous, either by itself or by illumination. In your spiritual life, are you seeking the external light of revelation (Quran and Sunnah) or relying on your own inner light? What specific step will you take today to expose yourself to that light?

The Dust Particle Objection and Circularity
Objection regarding dust particles and resolution through circularity of simultaneity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dust particle objection that challenges the sufficiency of these conditions, and the resolution involving a circularity of simultaneity.

Core Idea
Dust particles are visible together but not separately, resolved by a circularity of simultaneity.

After establishing the conditions for vision, the commentary raises a classic objection: dust particles are visible when clustered but not when scattered, even though all conditions are met. The response introduces a nuanced discussion of circularity (دور) and its two possible answers: one accepting circularity as simultaneity, the other denying circularity altogether.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection statement
Read the Arabic from 'ثم لا قوله' to the end. It states the objection: we see dust particles when together but not when scattered, though conditions are present.
2
Understand the proposed answer
The response says that for each particle to be visible, it needs to be joined by another particle, leading to circularity (each particle's vision depends on another).
This is not a simple answer; it invokes logical circularity.
3
Distinguish between two types of circularity
The first answer accepts circularity but says it is a circularity of simultaneity (دور معية), not priority, so it's acceptable. The second answer denies the circularity outright.
Circularity of simultaneity means A depends on B and B depends on A simultaneously, which is not logically problematic in this context.
4
Reflect on the significance
This objection shows that abstract reasoning about vision must account for real-world phenomena like dust particles, and theological reasoning sometimes employs technical logical solutions.
The passage ends with a note that further discussion exists.

Eight matters: first: soundness of the sense; therefore the levels of sight vary according to the variation in soundness of sight and are nullified by its nullification. Second: the thing being permissible of vision while present to the sense, such that the sense is directed to it and nothing opposes perception like sleep, heedlessness, and attention to something else. Third: its being opposite the seer in some direction or being in the ruling of opposition like in the case of the seen in a mirror. Fourth: absence of extreme smallness, for a very small thing is certainly not perceived by sight. Fifth: absence of extreme subtlety, such that it is dense, i.e., having a color in general even if weak. Sixth: absence of extreme distance, and it varies according to the strength and weakness of the seer. Seventh: absence of extreme closeness, for if the object of sight is attached to the surface of the eye, its perception is completely nullified. Eighth: absence of an obstructing veil, which is the colored body intermediate between them. And there is a ninth condition: that it be luminous by itself or by another, but he did not mention it here because it is mentioned in the discussion of visible qualities. Yet it can be included in its presence to the sense considered in the second condition. Then he said: (Eight matters) i.e., its conditions according to what the author mentioned here are eight, but the verification is that they are ten according to what is transmitted from the commentator. And an objection was raised against the exclusivity of the conditions to what was mentioned: we see dust particles when they are together and do not see them when they are scattered, while the mentioned conditions are present in both cases. It cannot be said: Rather that is due to the absence of the condition of density and the existence of the impediment of smallness, for we say: Then the vision of each particle would be conditioned by the joining of another to it, which is a circularity. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dust particle objection shows the conditions are invalid or insufficient.
Correction: The objection is resolved by recognizing that the perceived insufficiency is due to a circularity that is acceptable as simultaneity, or by denying the circularity altogether.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what type of circularity is proposed as an answer to the dust particle objection?
A) Circularity of priority (دور تقدم)
B) Circularity of simultaneity (دور معية)
C) Circularity of causation (دور علية)
D) Circularity of dependence (دور توقف)

?

?

The Inscription Theory of Vision
Describes the inscription theory of vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical kalām theory of vision through inscription of form in the frozen humor.

Core Idea
Vision depends on the angle of an imaginary cone of light.

This lesson presents the theory of inscription (الانطباع) which explains how we see objects and why distance affects visibility. The theory posits that the image of the object is impressed from the frozen humor at the vertex of an imagined cone.

Guided Path
1
Read the theory description
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then follow the translation. Notice the key terms: frozen humor, imagined cone, vertex angle.
2
Understand the role of the cone angle
The theory states that the angle at the vertex determines perceived size. When the object is very close or far, the angle becomes extreme, causing loss of vision.
3
Consider the implications
Think about how this theory explains everyday experience of size variation with distance. This sets up the refutation in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخطين المتصلين منه بطرفي المرئي وسعتها . فإن القائلين بالانطباع ذهبوا إلى أن صورة المرئي إنما ترتسم من الرطوبة الجليدية في زاوية رأس مخروط متوهم قاعدته عند المرئي، وإن اختلافه بالصغر والكبر في الرؤية إنما هو بحسب ضيق تلك الزاوية وسعتها ولهذا إذا قرب المرئي في الغاية أو بعد في الغاية صارت الزاوية لسعتها في الغاية حال القرب أو لضيقها في الغاية حال البعد كالمعدوم فانعدمت الرؤية حينئذ لعدم انطباع الصورة قال المصنف : وضعفه ظاهر بناء على تركب الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ إذ على هذا التقدير إن رأى الأجزاء كلها وجب أن يرى الجسم كما هو في الواقع سواء كان قريبا أو بعيدا وذلك لأن الرؤية لك منها أو بعضها أصغر مما هو عليه توجب الانقسام فيما لا يتجزئ لثبوت ما هو أصغر ورؤيته أي رؤية كل من الأجزاء أكبر مما هو عليه بمثل أو بأزيد منه توجب أن لا يرى إلا ضعفاً أو أكبر من ذلك وهو باطل قطعاً ورؤيته أكبر بأقل من مثل الانقسام ورؤية بعضها على ما هو عليه بعضها أكبر بمثل توجب ترجيحا بلا مرجح فوجب أن يرى الكل على حاله فلا تفاوت حينئذ بالصغر والكبر، فتعين أن يكون التفاوت بحسب رؤية بعض دون بعض فتمت معارضتنا لدليلهم على وجوب الرؤية عند اجتماع شرائطها
The two lines connected from it to the two sides of the visible object and their width. For the proponents of inscription hold that the image of the visible object is only impressed from the frozen humor at the vertex angle of an imagined cone whose base is at the visible object, and that its variation in smallness and largeness in vision is only according to the narrowness and width of that angle. Therefore, when the visible object is extremely close or extremely far, the angle becomes, due to its extreme width in the case of nearness or due to its extreme narrowness in the case of distance, like non-existent, so vision ceases then due to the lack of impression of the image. The author said: 'Its weakness is apparent based on the composition of indivisible parts.' For on this assumption, if he sees all the parts, he must see the body as it is in reality, whether near or far. That is because seeing each of them or some of them smaller than it is entails division in what is indivisible due to the existence of something smaller; and seeing it—i.e., seeing each of the parts—larger than it is by an equal amount or more entails that he would only see double or more than that, which is absolutely false; and seeing it larger by less than an equal amount entails division; and seeing some of them as they are and some larger by an equal amount entails preference without a preponderant. So he must see the whole as it is, and thus no variation in smallness and largeness. So it is determined that variation is due to seeing some parts without others. Thus our counterargument against their proof of the necessity of vision when its conditions are met is complete.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage first explains the inscription theory of vision: the image of the seen object is impressed from the frozen humor at the vertex of an imaginary cone whose base is at the object, and differences in perceived size are due to the width or narrowness of that cone's angle. When the object is extremely close or far, the angle becomes very wide or very narrow, effectively non-existent, so vision ceases. The author then refutes this theory by noting its weakness if space is composed of indivisible parts (atoms). The argument proceeds as follows: if all parts are seen, the object must appear as it is regardless of distance. If each or some parts appear smaller, that would require dividing an atom (indivisible), which is impossible. If each part appears larger by an equal or greater amount, only a double or larger image would be seen, which contradicts experience. If larger by less than equal, division again follows. If some parts appear as they are and others larger, that would be an arbitrary preference without cause. Since none of these options are acceptable, the only possibility is that the object is seen as it actually is, meaning no size variation exists. Therefore, the observed variation must be due to seeing some parts and not others. This completes the refutation of the claim that vision necessarily occurs when all conditions are met.

Key Terms
الانطباع
الرطوبة الجليدية
مخروط متوهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the vertex angle of the imagined cone correspond to in the inscription theory?
A) The size of the visible object
B) The distance of the visible object
C) The perceived size of the visible object
D) The brightness of the visible object

Key Takeaway
The inscription theory explains vision through the angle of an imagined cone from the object to the frozen humor of the eye.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that vision depends on a precise angle, and when you are too close or too far, you see nothing. In your spiritual life, are there truths you are too close to (too attached) or too far from (neglecting) that you fail to perceive? What angle adjustment is needed?
?
The inscription theory relies on an imagined model to explain a natural phenomenon. In your own understanding of God and creation, are you treating any human model as if it were reality itself? How can you distinguish between the model and the truth it points to?

Refuting Inscription with Indivisible Parts
Refutes the inscription theory using atomistic composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalām argument that the inscription theory is invalid if space is composed of indivisible atoms.

Core Idea
If parts are indivisible, perceived size cannot vary without contradiction.

This lesson examines the author's refutation of the inscription theory by assuming the atomistic hypothesis of indivisible parts. The argument systematically considers possible ways parts might be perceived and shows each leads to an absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Read the Arabic passage from 'قال المصنف' to the end. Follow the translation and note the logical steps.
2
Identify the cases considered
The argument considers different scenarios: seeing all parts, seeing some smaller, larger by an equal amount, etc. List them.
3
See why each leads to a contradiction
Each scenario either requires dividing an atom (impossible) or leads to implausible results like seeing double. The only consistent outcome is that the object appears as it is.
4
Understand the conclusion
The variation in perceived size must be due to seeing some parts and not others, thus refuting the claim that vision necessarily occurs when conditions are met.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

How might this logical principle challenge you to seek real justifications for your actions in worship and morality? The Objection of Unseen Mountains and the Analogy of Ordinary Matters
Objection and initial response using ordinary matters analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the response to the unseen mountains objection uses the analogy of ordinary matters to show that allowing a possibility does not entail sophistry.

Core Idea
Possibility and certainty can coexist, as shown by ordinary matters.

The objection claims that if vision is not necessary when conditions are met, we must allow huge mountains in our presence unseen—which would be sophistry. The answer appeals to ordinary matters: we allow their opposites yet are certain they won't occur, and this is not sophistry. The same logic applies to the mountains.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection: if vision is not necessary, then we must allow unseen mountains in our presence, which would be sophistry. Understand the arguer's fear of relativism.
The objection tries to force a dilemma: either accept necessary vision or fall into sophistry.
2
Identify the analogy of ordinary matters
Find the response that uses 'al-adiyat' (ordinary matters). The answer says that in daily life we allow opposites (e.g., fire not burning) yet are certain they won't happen, and this is not sophistry.
This shows that allowing a possibility does not undermine certainty.
3
Apply the analogy to the mountains
See how the same reasoning applies: we can allow the existence of unseen mountains while being certain they don't exist. Allowance (jawaz) does not mean doubt.
The key is distinguishing possibility from actual belief.
4
Reflect on the concept of tajwiz
Note that 'tajwiz' (allowance) here means mere logical possibility, not probability. This distinction is crucial for the rest of the discussion.
The passage later refines this meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : قوله إن لم يجد حصول الرؤية عند اجتماعها يلزم تجويز جبال شاهقة بحضرتنا لا نراها وهو سفسطة . قلنا : هذا معارض أي منقوض بجملة العاديات فإن الأمور العادية تجوز نقائضها مع جزمنا بعدم وقوعها ولا سفسطة هاهنا فكذا الحال في الجبال الشاهقة التي لا نراها فإنا نجوز وجودها ونجزم بعدمها وذلك لأن الجواز لا يستلزم الوقوع ولا ينافي الجزم بعدمه فمجرد تجويزها لا يكون سفسطة ثم نقول : إن كان مأخذ الجزم بعدم الجبل المذكور ما ذكرتم من وجوب الرؤية عند اجتماع شرائطها لوجب أن لا نجزم به إلا بعد العلم بهذا واللازم باطل لأنه يجزم به من لا يخطر بباله هذه المسألة، ولأنه ينجر إلى أن يكون ذلك الجزم نظرياً مع اتفاق الكل على كونه ضرورياً، وأما ثانياً : فهو القياس الاستثنائي وهي أن اللازم باطل مستنداً بلزوم مثله في جملة العاديات مع أنه لا سفسطة فيه قطعاً لكن فيه بحث لجواز أن يريد المستدل بالتجويز أن يكون محتملاً عندنا كما في الظن، وحينئذ لا يرد النقض بالعاديات إذ لا يجوز نقائضها تجويز الطرف الآخر في الظن، بل الجواب حينئذ منع الملازمة المستفادة من قوله : وإلا لجاز أن يكون بحضرتنا إلخ، واعترض أيضاً بأن قولهم : يلزم تجويز جبال شاهقة لا نراها يحتمل معنيين أحدهما تجويز ثبوتها في الواقع ونحن لا نراها والثاني تجويز أن لا نراها على تقدير ثبوتها ولزوم الأول للشرط اتفاقي لا عقلي ولزوم الثاني عقلي للشركة في تمام علة الرؤية، فإذا لم ير أحدهما مع تمام علته لم يرد الآخر لة في الشرط والظاهر أن هذا هو مراد المستدل فلا نقض بالعاديات والمخلص عنه منع تمام العلة وأنت خبير بأن المتبادر من العبارة هو الاحتمال الأول ولذا سبق ظاهر كلام الشارح عليه، ثم إن النقض على الاحتمال الثاني منعات أيضاً، إذ لا سفسطة في نفس تجويز أن لا نراها على تقدير ثبوتها كما لا سفسطة في تجويز نقائض العاديات وإن جزمنا بانتفاء المجوز في الصورتين فليتأمل. قوله : لوجب أن لا نجزم إلخ اعترض عليه الأبهري بأنه لا يلزم من كون الشيء مستنداً إلى شيء آخر وجوب عدم حصول العلم به إلا بعد العلم بذلك الآخر لجواز أن يوجد طرق شتى يحصل العلم به بكل من تلك الطرق كيف ولا يلزم من نفي الدليل المعني نفي المدلول وجوابه أن الاستدلال يتوقف على انحصار مأخذ الجزم فيما ذكر إذ لو لم ينحصر لم يلزم من انتفاء ذلك المأخذ تجويز الجبال المذكورة لجواز أن نجزم بعدمها من طريق آخر. قوله : ولأنه ينجر إلى أن يكون نظرياً إن قلت : يجوز أن يكون الأخذ بطريق الحدث فلا يلزم النظرية قلت : هي ليست من الحدسيات العامة لأنها مشروطة بتكرر المشاهدة وهذه ليست كذلك وليست من الحدسيات الخاصة أي بالنسبة إلى من له قوة قدسية لأنها عامة لكل أهل عادة من العوام والصبيان على أنه بعد تسلمه لا يقدح في لزوم نظريته بالنسبة إلى العامة وهي التي ادعى بطلانها بقي فيه بحث وهو أنه يجوز أن يكون من القضايا الفطرية القياس فإن رد بحصول الجزم بها لمن لا يخطر بباله ذلك القياس رجع إلى الوجه الأول، وأما الاعتراض بجواز أن يكون الحكم ضرورياً ولميته نظرية كإدراك البصر فإنه ضروري وقد عللوه بخروج الشعاع من البصر أو بانطباع الصورة واحتجوا عليه فجوابه أن المراد إن كان لمية نفس الحكم العقلي، فذلك يقتضي نظريته وإن كان المراد لمية متعلق الحكم، أعني انعدام الجبل فلميته عدم خلق الله تعالى إياه وليس المذكور في البيان هذا فليتأمل.
Then we say: His statement: 'If the occurrence of vision when its conditions are met is not found, it necessitates the allowance of huge mountains in our presence that we do not see, and that is sophistry.' We said: This is countered—i.e., refuted—by the whole set of ordinary matters. For ordinary matters allow their opposites while we are certain of their non-occurrence, and there is no sophistry here. So too is the case with the huge mountains that we do not see; for we allow their existence and are certain of their nonexistence, because allowance does not entail occurrence nor does it contradict certainty of nonexistence. Mere allowance of them is not sophistry. Then we say: If the basis of certainty about the nonexistence of the mentioned mountain is what you mentioned—the necessity of vision when its conditions are met—then we would not be certain of it except after knowledge of this. But the necessary consequent is false, because one who does not have this issue in mind is certain of it, and because it would lead to that certainty being theoretical, despite all agreeing that it is necessary. And secondly, it is the exceptional syllogism: the consequent is false, based on the occurrence of the like of it in the set of ordinary matters, and there is no sophistry in that at all. But there is investigation because the arguer might mean by 'allowance' that it is probable in our view, as in opinion. In that case, the refutation by ordinary matters does not apply, for their opposites are not allowed as a probability of the other side in opinion. Rather, the answer then is to deny the implication derived from his statement: 'Otherwise it would be possible that there are in our presence, etc.' And it is also objected that their statement: 'It necessitates the allowance of huge mountains that we do not see' admits two meanings: one is the allowance of their actual existence while we do not see them; the second is the allowance that we do not see them given their existence. The necessity of the first is conventional, not rational, while the necessity of the second is rational due to sharing the complete cause of vision. So if one does not see despite the complete cause, the other does not apply due to the condition. Apparently, this is what the arguer means, so there is no refutation by ordinary matters. The way out of this is to deny the completeness of the cause. And you are aware that the apparent meaning of the expression is the first probability, hence the apparent meaning of the commentator's words preceded it. Then the refutation of the second probability is also prevented, for there is no sophistry in the very allowance that we do not see them given their existence, just as there is no sophistry in allowing the opposites of ordinary matters, even though we are certain of the absence of the allowing factor in both cases. So let it be considered. His statement: 'Then we would not be certain, etc.' Al-Abhari objected to it: It does not follow from something being based on something else that knowledge of it cannot be obtained except after knowledge of that other, because it is possible that there are various paths by which knowledge is obtained, each of them. Moreover, the negation of a specific proof does not entail the negation of the proved. The answer is that the inference depends on the exclusivity of the basis of certainty to what was mentioned; if it were not exclusive, the absence of that basis would not necessitate allowing the mentioned mountains, because it is possible that we are certain of their nonexistence through another path. His statement: 'And because it leads to it being theoretical.' If you say: It is possible that the acquisition is by way of intuition, so theoretical nature does not follow. I say: It is not among general intuitions because they are conditioned by repeated observation, and this is not so; nor is it among special intuitions (i.e., for one with a holy power) because it is common to all people of custom, including commoners and children. Moreover, even if that is granted, it does not harm the necessity of its being theoretical for the general public, and it is the invalidity that is claimed. There remains investigation: it is possible that it is among the innate propositions with a syllogism. If it is replied that certainty is obtained by one who does not have that syllogism in mind, it returns to the first point. As for the objection that the judgment could be necessary while its reason is theoretical, like visual perception—it is necessary yet they explain it by the emission of rays or the impression of the image and argue for it—the answer is: If what is meant is the reason for the rational judgment itself, that entails its being theoretical; but if what is meant is the reason for the object of the judgment (i.e., the nonexistence of the mountain), then its reason is God's not creating it, and this is not mentioned in the explanation. So let it be considered.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage presents a complex objection and response cycle. It begins with an objection: if one denies that vision necessarily occurs when conditions are met, then one must allow the possibility that huge mountains exist in our presence but are unseen—which would be sophistry. The answer counters by appealing to ordinary matters (al-adiyat): we allow that ordinary events could have opposite outcomes while still being certain of their non-occurrence, and this is not sophistry. The same logic applies to the mountains: we can allow their existence and still be certain of their nonexistence. The passage then raises a further objection: if the basis for certainty about the mountain's nonexistence is the condition of vision, then certainty would only be obtained after knowing that condition, but we are certain without it, and moreover that would make the certainty theoretical, not necessary. The response examines the logical form (qiyas istithna'i) and distinguishes two meanings of 'allowance' (tajwiz): probability in opinion vs. mere possibility. If the opponent means probability, the counter by ordinary matters fails because we do not consider ordinary opposites as probable. The proper answer then is to deny the implication. Another objection arises: the phrase 'allowance of huge mountains that we do not see' has two interpretations—allowing their actual existence, or allowing that we do not see them given their existence. The first is conventional (not rational), the second rational. The arguer likely intends the second, so the counter by ordinary matters does not apply unless the completeness of the cause is denied. The commentator notes that the apparent meaning is the first interpretation. Further discussion addresses whether the certainty is theoretical or necessary, involving intuition (hads), innate propositions, and the distinction between the judgment itself and its reason. The passage concludes by urging careful consideration.

Key Terms
سفسطة
العاديات
الجواز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allowing a possibility means one doubts its non-occurrence.
Correction: The passage clarifies that mere rational allowance does not contradict certainty; we can be certain of the non-occurrence of something while still admitting its logical possibility.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the answer to the mountain objection?
A) The mountains are not actually huge.
B) We have no way to know if mountains exist.
C) Ordinary matters also have possibilities yet we are certain of their non-occurrence.
D) The objection is based on a false premise.

Key Takeaway
Allowing a logical possibility does not entail doubt; we can coexist possibility and certainty, as demonstrated by ordinary matters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, you allow that it is possible for the sun not to rise tomorrow, yet you are certain it will. Do you treat the promises of Allah in the Qur'an with the same distinction? Are you certain of His reward despite allowing, by your sins, the possibility of His punishment? What change must you make today to align your allowance (possible sin) with your certainty (His justice)?
?
When you commit a sin, do you 'allow' its possibility while being 'certain' of its wrongness? How does that mental split affect your repentance? Are you using the concept of 'allowance' as an excuse to persist, or are you actively working to turn your certainty into action?
?
The objector tried to call the response 'sophistry'. Have you ever dismissed a valid Islamic argument as 'too philosophical' because it challenged your comfort? Confront one such area in your faith where you need to accept a logical truth even if it disturbs your current practice.

Refining the Response: Two Meanings of Allowance and the Exceptional Syllogism
Refinement of the response, distinguishing meanings of tajwiz and using exceptional syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the response is refined by distinguishing two meanings of 'allowance' and by using the exceptional syllogism.

Core Idea
The meaning of 'allowance' determines whether the analogy holds.

The objection is further scrutinized. Some argue that if 'allowance' means probability (zann), then the ordinary matters analogy fails because we do not consider their opposites probable. The proper counter is to deny the implication. Another issue is whether the allowance refers to actual existence or to the failure to see them given existence. The passage clarifies these logical subtleties.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first refinement
Read the part where the passage considers that 'tajwiz' might mean probability (zann). If so, ordinary matters do not have opposites as probable, so the refutation fails. The response then becomes to deny the implication.
This shows that the meaning of key terms is crucial.
2
Identify the two meanings of the objection's phrase
The phrase 'allowance of huge mountains that we do not see' can mean: (1) allowing their actual existence while unseen, or (2) allowing that we do not see them given their existence. The passage examines which is intended.
The arguer likely means the second, which is rational.
3
See the exceptional syllogism
The passage uses a qiyas istithna'i (exceptional syllogism) to argue that the consequent is false because a similar case in ordinary matters also leads to no sophistry.
This logical form is used to strengthen the response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : قوله إن لم يجد حصول الرؤية عند اجتماعها يلزم تجويز جبال شاهقة بحضرتنا لا نراها وهو سفسطة . قلنا : هذا معارض أي منقوض بجملة العاديات فإن الأمور العادية تجوز نقائضها مع جزمنا بعدم وقوعها ولا سفسطة هاهنا فكذا الحال في الجبال الشاهقة التي لا نراها فإنا نجوز وجودها ونجزم بعدمها وذلك لأن الجواز لا يستلزم الوقوع ولا ينافي الجزم بعدمه فمجرد تجويزها لا يكون سفسطة ثم نقول : إن كان مأخذ الجزم بعدم الجبل المذكور ما ذكرتم من وجوب الرؤية عند اجتماع شرائطها لوجب أن لا نجزم به إلا بعد العلم بهذا واللازم باطل لأنه يجزم به من لا يخطر بباله هذه المسألة، ولأنه ينجر إلى أن يكون ذلك الجزم نظرياً مع اتفاق الكل على كونه ضرورياً، وأما ثانياً : فهو القياس الاستثنائي وهي أن اللازم باطل مستنداً بلزوم مثله في جملة العاديات مع أنه لا سفسطة فيه قطعاً لكن فيه بحث لجواز أن يريد المستدل بالتجويز أن يكون محتملاً عندنا كما في الظن، وحينئذ لا يرد النقض بالعاديات إذ لا يجوز نقائضها تجويز الطرف الآخر في الظن، بل الجواب حينئذ منع الملازمة المستفادة من قوله : وإلا لجاز أن يكون بحضرتنا إلخ، واعترض أيضاً بأن قولهم : يلزم تجويز جبال شاهقة لا نراها يحتمل معنيين أحدهما تجويز ثبوتها في الواقع ونحن لا نراها والثاني تجويز أن لا نراها على تقدير ثبوتها ولزوم الأول للشرط اتفاقي لا عقلي ولزوم الثاني عقلي للشركة في تمام علة الرؤية، فإذا لم ير أحدهما مع تمام علته لم يرد الآخر لة في الشرط والظاهر أن هذا هو مراد المستدل فلا نقض بالعاديات والمخلص عنه منع تمام العلة وأنت خبير بأن المتبادر من العبارة هو الاحتمال الأول ولذا سبق ظاهر كلام الشارح عليه، ثم إن النقض على الاحتمال الثاني منعات أيضاً، إذ لا سفسطة في نفس تجويز أن لا نراها على تقدير ثبوتها كما لا سفسطة في تجويز نقائض العاديات وإن جزمنا بانتفاء المجوز في الصورتين فليتأمل. قوله : لوجب أن لا نجزم إلخ اعترض عليه الأبهري بأنه لا يلزم من كون الشيء مستنداً إلى شيء آخر وجوب عدم حصول العلم به إلا بعد العلم بذلك الآخر لجواز أن يوجد طرق شتى يحصل العلم به بكل من تلك الطرق كيف ولا يلزم من نفي الدليل المعني نفي المدلول وجوابه أن الاستدلال يتوقف على انحصار مأخذ الجزم فيما ذكر إذ لو لم ينحصر لم يلزم من انتفاء ذلك المأخذ تجويز الجبال المذكورة لجواز أن نجزم بعدمها من طريق آخر. قوله : ولأنه ينجر إلى أن يكون نظرياً إن قلت : يجوز أن يكون الأخذ بطريق الحدث فلا يلزم النظرية قلت : هي ليست من الحدسيات العامة لأنها مشروطة بتكرر المشاهدة وهذه ليست كذلك وليست من الحدسيات الخاصة أي بالنسبة إلى من له قوة قدسية لأنها عامة لكل أهل عادة من العوام والصبيان على أنه بعد تسلمه لا يقدح في لزوم نظريته بالنسبة إلى العامة وهي التي ادعى بطلانها بقي فيه بحث وهو أنه يجوز أن يكون من القضايا الفطرية القياس فإن رد بحصول الجزم بها لمن لا يخطر بباله ذلك القياس رجع إلى الوجه الأول، وأما الاعتراض بجواز أن يكون الحكم ضرورياً ولميته نظرية كإدراك البصر فإنه ضروري وقد عللوه بخروج الشعاع من البصر أو بانطباع الصورة واحتجوا عليه فجوابه أن المراد إن كان لمية نفس الحكم العقلي، فذلك يقتضي نظريته وإن كان المراد لمية متعلق الحكم، أعني انعدام الجبل فلميته عدم خلق الله تعالى إياه وليس المذكور في البيان هذا فليتأمل.
Then we say: His statement: 'If the occurrence of vision when its conditions are met is not found, it necessitates the allowance of huge mountains in our presence that we do not see, and that is sophistry.' We said: This is countered—i.e., refuted—by the whole set of ordinary matters. For ordinary matters allow their opposites while we are certain of their non-occurrence, and there is no sophistry here. So too is the case with the huge mountains that we do not see; for we allow their existence and are certain of their nonexistence, because allowance does not entail occurrence nor does it contradict certainty of nonexistence. Mere allowance of them is not sophistry. Then we say: If the basis of certainty about the nonexistence of the mentioned mountain is what you mentioned—the necessity of vision when its conditions are met—then we would not be certain of it except after knowledge of this. But the necessary consequent is false, because one who does not have this issue in mind is certain of it, and because it would lead to that certainty being theoretical, despite all agreeing that it is necessary. And secondly, it is the exceptional syllogism: the consequent is false, based on the occurrence of the like of it in the set of ordinary matters, and there is no sophistry in that at all. But there is investigation because the arguer might mean by 'allowance' that it is probable in our view, as in opinion. In that case, the refutation by ordinary matters does not apply, for their opposites are not allowed as a probability of the other side in opinion. Rather, the answer then is to deny the implication derived from his statement: 'Otherwise it would be possible that there are in our presence, etc.' And it is also objected that their statement: 'It necessitates the allowance of huge mountains that we do not see' admits two meanings: one is the allowance of their actual existence while we do not see them; the second is the allowance that we do not see them given their existence. The necessity of the first is conventional, not rational, while the necessity of the second is rational due to sharing the complete cause of vision. So if one does not see despite the complete cause, the other does not apply due to the condition. Apparently, this is what the arguer means, so there is no refutation by ordinary matters. The way out of this is to deny the completeness of the cause. And you are aware that the apparent meaning of the expression is the first probability, hence the apparent meaning of the commentator's words preceded it. Then the refutation of the second probability is also prevented, for there is no sophistry in the very allowance that we do not see them given their existence, just as there is no sophistry in allowing the opposites of ordinary matters, even though we are certain of the absence of the allowing factor in both cases. So let it be considered. His statement: 'Then we would not be certain, etc.' Al-Abhari objected to it: It does not follow from something being based on something else that knowledge of it cannot be obtained except after knowledge of that other, because it is possible that there are various paths by which knowledge is obtained, each of them. Moreover, the negation of a specific proof does not entail the negation of the proved. The answer is that the inference depends on the exclusivity of the basis of certainty to what was mentioned; if it were not exclusive, the absence of that basis would not necessitate allowing the mentioned mountains, because it is possible that we are certain of their nonexistence through another path. His statement: 'And because it leads to it being theoretical.' If you say: It is possible that the acquisition is by way of intuition, so theoretical nature does not follow. I say: It is not among general intuitions because they are conditioned by repeated observation, and this is not so; nor is it among special intuitions (i.e., for one with a holy power) because it is common to all people of custom, including commoners and children. Moreover, even if that is granted, it does not harm the necessity of its being theoretical for the general public, and it is the invalidity that is claimed. There remains investigation: it is possible that it is among the innate propositions with a syllogism. If it is replied that certainty is obtained by one who does not have that syllogism in mind, it returns to the first point. As for the objection that the judgment could be necessary while its reason is theoretical, like visual perception—it is necessary yet they explain it by the emission of rays or the impression of the image and argue for it—the answer is: If what is meant is the reason for the rational judgment itself, that entails its being theoretical; but if what is meant is the reason for the object of the judgment (i.e., the nonexistence of the mountain), then its reason is God's not creating it, and this is not mentioned in the explanation. So let it be considered.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage presents a complex objection and response cycle. It begins with an objection: if one denies that vision necessarily occurs when conditions are met, then one must allow the possibility that huge mountains exist in our presence but are unseen—which would be sophistry. The answer counters by appealing to ordinary matters (al-adiyat): we allow that ordinary events could have opposite outcomes while still being certain of their non-occurrence, and this is not sophistry. The same logic applies to the mountains: we can allow their existence and still be certain of their nonexistence. The passage then raises a further objection: if the basis for certainty about the mountain's nonexistence is the condition of vision, then certainty would only be obtained after knowing that condition, but we are certain without it, and moreover that would make the certainty theoretical, not necessary. The response examines the logical form (qiyas istithna'i) and distinguishes two meanings of 'allowance' (tajwiz): probability in opinion vs. mere possibility. If the opponent means probability, the counter by ordinary matters fails because we do not consider ordinary opposites as probable. The proper answer then is to deny the implication. Another objection arises: the phrase 'allowance of huge mountains that we do not see' has two interpretations—allowing their actual existence, or allowing that we do not see them given their existence. The first is conventional (not rational), the second rational. The arguer likely intends the second, so the counter by ordinary matters does not apply unless the completeness of the cause is denied. The commentator notes that the apparent meaning is the first interpretation. Further discussion addresses whether the certainty is theoretical or necessary, involving intuition (hads), innate propositions, and the distinction between the judgment itself and its reason. The passage concludes by urging careful consideration.

القياس الاستثنائي
الظن

What does the passage suggest if 'allowance' means probability (zann)?
A) The ordinary matters analogy still applies.

D) The objector's argument is strengthened.

Key Takeaway
The meaning of 'tajwiz' is critical; the response must be tailored to whether it means probability or mere possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a conviction about your faith, do you examine the precise meaning of the terms you use? Are you certain that your arguments do not rely on ambiguous language? What would it cost you to clarify them?
?
You have just learned that 'allowance' can mean possibility or probability. Think of a belief you hold that you think is 'possible' — but is it truly probable in your mind, or just a remote possibility? How does that distinction affect your certainty? Will you now re-evaluate your positions with this precision?
?
This passage shows that a clever argument can be undermined by a hidden ambiguity. In your own reasoning, are you willing to admit that you might be using a key term in two different ways without realizing it? How will you guard against this in matters of theology that affect your eternal destiny?

Theoretical vs. Necessary Knowledge and the Role of Intuitions
Discussion on whether certainty is theoretical or necessary, involving intuitions and innate propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will analyze the debate on whether certainty about the mountain's nonexistence is theoretical or necessary, and how intuitions and innate propositions are involved.

Core Idea
The source of certainty determines whether knowledge is necessary or theoretical.

The passage examines the claim that certainty about the mountain's nonexistence is necessary (daruri), not theoretical (nazari). It considers whether this certainty could come from intuition (hads), innate propositions, or is simply immediate. The discussion involves general and special intuitions and the role of syllogism in innate propositions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the challenge to theoretical certainty
The objection says that if certainty about the mountain's nonexistence were based on the necessity of vision, it would be theoretical. But it is agreed to be necessary. So the basis must be something else.
This challenges the arguer's assumption.
2
Explore the possibility of intuition
The passage considers whether the certainty comes from intuition (hads). It distinguishes general intuitions (based on repeated observation) and special intuitions (for those with holy power). Neither applies here because the certainty is universal.
Intuition does not explain the common certainty.
3
Consider innate propositions with syllogism
Another possibility is that the certainty is from innate propositions (fitriyya) that involve a syllogism. But if the syllogism is not present in the mind, it returns to the first objection.
This shows the depth of the debate.
4
Distinguish judgment from its reason
A final move: the judgment could be necessary while its reason is theoretical, like visual perception. But the passage argues that if the reason is for the judgment itself, it would make the judgment theoretical.
The distinction between judgment and its object is crucial.

It begins with an objection: if one denies that vision necessarily occurs when conditions are met, then one must allow the possibility that huge mountains exist in our presence but are unseen—which would be sophistry. The answer counters by appealing to ordinary matters (al-adiyat): we allow that ordinary events could have opposite outcomes while still being certain of their non-occurrence, and this is not sophistry. The same logic applies to the mountains: we can allow their existence and still be certain of their nonexistence. The passage then raises a further objection: if the basis for certainty about the mountain's nonexistence is the condition of vision, then certainty would only be obtained after knowing that condition, but we are certain without it, and moreover that would make the certainty theoretical, not necessary. The response examines the logical form (qiyas istithna'i) and distinguishes two meanings of 'allowance' (tajwiz): probability in opinion vs. mere possibility. If the opponent means probability, the counter by ordinary matters fails because we do not consider ordinary opposites as probable. The proper answer then is to deny the implication. Another objection arises: the phrase 'allowance of huge mountains that we do not see' has two interpretations—allowing their actual existence, or allowing that we do not see them given their existence. The first is conventional (not rational), the second rational. The arguer likely intends the second, so the counter by ordinary matters does not apply unless the completeness of the cause is denied. The commentator notes that the apparent meaning is the first interpretation. Further discussion addresses whether the certainty is theoretical or necessary, involving intuition (hads), innate propositions, and the distinction between the judgment itself and its reason. The passage concludes by urging careful consideration.

Key Terms
نظري
ضروري
الحدسيات
الفطريات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the passage reject intuition (hads) as the source of certainty about the mountain's nonexistence?
A) Intuition is only for the elite.
B) The certainty is common to all people, including children.
C) Intuition requires repeated observation, which is not present.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
The certainty about the mountain's nonexistence is widely shared and not easily explained by theoretical inference, but the debate over its basis remains unresolved.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of one belief you hold with absolute certainty—like that the world exists or that you are reading this. Is your certainty based on theoretical reasoning or immediate intuition? If a scholar showed that it could be theoretical, would that shake your conviction? Examine the foundation of your belief honestly; your answer reveals whether your knowledge is truly necessary or just assumed.
?
You claim to believe in Allah with certainty. In light of this lesson's distinction between necessary and theoretical knowledge, is your faith based on repeated observation, innate intuition, or a reasoning process? If it turned out to be theoretical, would you still act upon it with full commitment? Your relationship with Allah depends on the kind of certainty you possess.
?
The passage debates whether certainty requires a syllogism. In your daily life, do you ever pause to ask why you are certain about the fundamentals of your faith? Or do you rely on inherited certainty? Consider that the scholars of kalam subjected their most basic beliefs to rigorous analysis. Are you willing to do the same, even at the risk of discomfort? Your answer determines the depth of your conviction.

A Note on the Quiddity of Vision: Better to Concede Unity
Commentator suggests conceding quiddity unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator suggests conceding unity in quiddity of vision and arguing for differing conditions as a stronger theological strategy.

Core Idea
Concede unity of quiddity to block the opponent's escape.

In this marginal note, the commentator critiques the strategy of insisting that the quiddity of vision differs between this world and the unseen. He argues that such a move inadvertently allows the Mu'tazila to claim that the dispute is not about the real vision of God, but about some other type of perception. Instead, he proposes conceding that the quiddity is the same, and then appealing to differing conditions. This subtle shift in argument has important implications for the debate on divine vision.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
This note comments on a previous claim that the quiddity of vision differs between witness and unseen. Read the passage once to grasp the commentator's alternative proposal.
The commentator uses 'قوله' to refer to the previous statement.
2
Identify the two strategies
The passage contrasts two approaches: (1) insisting that the quiddities differ, and (2) conceding unity in quiddity but allowing different conditions. Notice how each leads to a different outcome.
3
Analyze the Mu'tazila's potential objection
If the first strategy is used, the Mu'tazila can claim the dispute is only about a different type of perception, not the real vision. Understand why this weakens the argument.
The note calls this 'الانكشاف التام' (complete disclosure) and 'العلم الضروري' (necessary knowledge).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (غير ماهية الرؤية في الشاهد الأولى أن يسلم الاتحاد في الماهية ويجوز اختلاف الشروط بحسب الهويات، إذ على القول بتغاير ماهيتي الرؤيتين يتأتي للمعتزلة أن يقولوا : نزاعنا إنما هو في هذا النوع من الإدراك لا في الرؤية المخالفة لها بالحقية المسماة عندكم بالانكشاف التام وعندنا بالعلم الضروري.
His saying: '(different from the quiddity of vision in the witnessed) - it is better to concede unity in quiddity and allow difference of conditions according to the identities, because if one says the two quiddities of vision are different, it becomes possible for the Mu‘tazila to say: Our dispute is only about this type of perception, not about the vision that differs from it in reality, which you call complete disclosure (al-inkishāf al-tāmm) and we call necessary knowledge (al-‘ilm al-ḍarūrī).
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a marginal note commenting on the earlier statement that the quiddity of vision differs between witness and unseen. The note suggests that a better approach for the opponent (the Ash‘arite perhaps) would be to concede that the quiddity of vision is the same, but to argue that the conditions differ according to the realities (hawiyyāt). The reasoning is that if one insists on different quiddities, the Mu‘tazila could then claim that the dispute is not about 'real' vision but only about a different type of perception, which would undermine the argument.

Key Terms

الهويات (al-hawiyyāt)
الانكشاف التام (al-inkishāf al-tāmm)
العلم الضروري (al-‘ilm al-ḍarūrī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Some think that asserting different quiddities of vision strengthens the case for divine vision.
Correction: The commentator shows that this allows the Mu'tazila to redefine the dispute, weakening the argument. It is better to concede unity of quiddity.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Objection and Refutation on Judgment and Conceptualization
The author refutes the opponent's claim that if God's essence is unknown, no judgment about it is possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why affirming something about God does not require complete conceptualization of His essence.

Core Idea
Judgment only needs partial understanding, not full comprehension.

This lesson addresses a common objection: if we cannot fully know God's essence, how can we make any judgments about Him? The passage shows that affirmation (tasdiq) only requires an aspectual conceptualization, not complete knowledge. This is a key methodological point in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the opponent's argument that complete conceptualization is necessary for any judgment.
The opponent claims that without full knowledge, no judgment is possible.
2
Identify the answer
See how the author replies by distinguishing complete conceptualization (bil-kunh) from aspectual conceptualization (bi-wajh ma).
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this distinction is important for Islamic theology, especially for affirming attributes of God.
It allows us to make true statements about God without claiming to fully comprehend Him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فليس المعلوم ذاته لمخصوصة وعكسه ) أعني قولنا : ليس ذاته المخصوصة بالمعلوم ( هو المطلوب احتج الخصم بأنه لو لم يكن ذاته ( متصور معلوماً ( لامتنع الحكم عليها بأنها غير متصورة و) امتنع الحكم عليها ( بالصفات ) الآخر ) والجواب ظاهر وهو أن التصديق لا يتوقف على التصور بالكنه بل بوجه ماء
So the known is not its specific essence, and its opposite — that is, our statement: 'its specific essence is not known' — is what is sought. The opponent argued that if its essence were not conceptually known, judgment upon it that it is not conceptualized would be impossible, and judgment upon it of [other] attributes would be impossible. And the answer is clear: that affirmation (tasdiq) does not depend on complete conceptualization (at-tasawwur bil-kunh) but on some aspect (bi-wajh ma).
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection is: if God's specific essence is unknown, then we cannot judge about it at all, such as saying it is not known. The answer is that judgment (tasdiq) does not require complete conceptualization of the quiddity; a partial or aspectual conceptualization is sufficient. So the objection is invalid.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One must fully comprehend a thing before making any judgment about it.
Correction: Judgment (tasdiq) only requires some aspectual conceptualization, not complete comprehension.

Checkpoint

B) Some aspectual conceptualization

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Theoretical Knowledge from Reflection, Not Recollection
Al-Jurjani examines the Mu'tazilite claim that knowledge is generated from direct reflection, not recollection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that theoretical knowledge arises from direct reflection, not from recollection of reflection, and why recollection is considered a necessary act of God, making knowledge from it not subject to obligation.

Core Idea
Recollection of reflection is a necessary act of God; knowledge from it would be necessary, not commanded.

The Mu'tazilites, known for their emphasis on rational reflection, further specified that the theoretical knowledge required for obligation must come from direct intellectual effort (nazar), not from a later recollection of that effort. This chapter examines their two proofs for this claim and a key objection pointing to an internal inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to get a sense of the argument's flow.
Notice the structure: claim, two reasons, objection, and a rebuttal comment.
2
Identify the two reasons
Find the two reasons the Mu'tazilites give for why knowledge from recollection is not generated but directly produced by divine power.
The first reason focuses on necessity, the second on the inability of humans to produce recollection directly.
3
Examine the objection and rebuttal
Note the objection that this contradicts the first branch (generated acts cannot be direct). Then see the rebuttal that obligation is conditioned by ignorance.
This internal critique shows the complexity of Mu'tazilite kalam.
4
Reflect on the implication for obligation
Consider how the argument narrows what counts as commanded knowledge: only knowledge from direct reflection, not from recollection, can be the basis of obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : ( من الفروع ( قالوا : العلم النظري يتولد من النظر ابتداء ولا يتولد من تذكر النظر يعني أنه إذا أغفل عن النظر والعلم بالمنظور فيه ثم تذكر النظر فالعلم الحاصل عند التذكر لا يكون متولداً منه بل مقدوراً مباشراً بالقدرة، وذلك لوجهين : أشار إلى أولهما بقوله : ( لأنه ( أي تذكر النظر (ضروري من فعل الله تعالى وليس مقدوراً للبشر فلو وقعت المعرفة بالله به أي بالنظر حال كونه ( متذكراً لكانت ( المعرفة ( ضرورية ) من فعل الله أيضاً ( فامتنع التكليف بها ) وخرجت عن أن تكون مأموراً بها وهو باطل إجماعاً وأشار إلى ثانيهما بقوله : ( ولأنه) أي تذكر النظر فعله قوله : ( ثم تذكر النظر ) أي بلا قصد التذكر قوله : (بل مقدوراً مباشراً بالقدرة أي الحادثة وإلا لم يكن مقدوراً للعبد كما لو كان متولداً من تذكر النظير فيمتنع التكليف بها، لكن يرد عليه أنه يخالف ما ذكره في الفرع الأول وهو أن المتولد من السبب المقدور يمتنع أن يقع مباشراً باتفاق المعتزلة، فتأمل. قوله : ( وخرجت عن أن تكون مأموراً بها فيه دفع بمنع بطلان التالي أعني امتناع التكليف بها، بناء على أن التكليف مقيد بعدم المعرفة إذ تكليف العارف تكليف بتحصيل الحاصل وقد أوضحناه في رابع مقاصد النظر فلينظر فيه .
The third: (of the branches) (They said: Theoretical knowledge is generated from reflection initially and is not generated from the recollection of reflection, meaning that if one neglects reflection and knowledge of the object of reflection, then recollects the reflection, the knowledge obtained at the time of recollection is not generated from it but rather is directly produced by power. That is for two reasons: He indicated the first by his saying: (Because) i.e., recollection of reflection (is necessary from the act of God Most High and is not within human power; if knowledge of God occurred by it, i.e., by reflection while being a recollector, then the knowledge would be necessary) from the act of God as well, (so obligation would be impossible) and it would cease to be commanded, which is false by consensus. And he indicated the second by his saying: (And because) i.e., the recollection of reflection is His act. He said: (Then the recollection of reflection) i.e., without intending recollection. He said: (Rather, it is directly produced by power) i.e., the originated power; otherwise it would not be within the servant's power, as if it were generated from recollection of the similar, so obligation would be impossible. But it is objected that this contradicts what he mentioned in the first branch, that the generated from a destined cause cannot occur directly by agreement of the Mu'tazilites; so reflect. He said: (And it ceases to be commanded) in it is a rebuttal by denying the falsity of the consequent, i.e., the impossibility of obligation, based on the premise that obligation is conditioned by lack of knowledge, since obligating the knower is obligating the attainment of the already attained, and we have explained it in the Fourth Purpose of Reflection, so consider it.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage presents the third branch. The Mu'tazilites argued that theoretical knowledge (العلم النظري) is generated from direct reflection (النظر), not from recollection of that reflection (تذكر النظر). Two reasons: first, recollection is a necessary act of God, not voluntary for humans, so knowledge from it would be necessary and thus not subject to obligation; second, recollection itself is not a human power. The commentary adds an inconsistency: the first branch claimed the generated act cannot occur directly, but here knowledge from recollection is said to be directly produced, contradicting that. The response suggests that obligation is conditioned by ignorance, so knowledge from recollection would be a repetition of what is already known.

Key Terms
العلم النظري
النظر
تذكر النظر
المعرفة
التكليف
الضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that recollection is a cause that generates knowledge, like the initial reflection.
Correction: Recollection is not a cause; it is a necessary act of God, so knowledge from it would be necessary, not generated, and thus not a basis for obligation.

Checkpoint
Why does the Mu'tazilite claim in the third branch deny that knowledge from recollection is generated?
A) Because recollection is not a cause but a necessary act of God.
B) Because knowledge from recollection is impossible.
C) Because the Mu'tazilites forbid any knowledge from recollection.
D) Because recollection is a voluntary human action.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites argue that theoretical knowledge must come directly from reflection, not recollection, because recollection is a necessary act of God, making the resulting knowledge necessary and not commanded.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever relied on memorized arguments about God without genuinely reflecting on them? What steps will you take to ensure your knowledge of God comes from active, personal reflection rather than passive recollection?
?
The Mu'tazilites argue that knowledge from recollection is necessary and thus not commanded. Examine your own practice: Is your faith a product of active reflection, or are you merely recalling what others have said? What will you do differently starting today?
?
This lesson asserts that obligation is impossible when knowledge is necessary. How does this challenge the way you approach your duties toward God? Are you content with a secondhand faith, or are you willing to engage in the hard work of direct reflection as a commanded duty?
