The Doctrine of Divine Price-Setting
Ash'ari claim with hadith and Mu'tazila disagreement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that God is the sole determiner of prices, supported by the hadith, and the contrasting Mu'tazila views.

Core Idea
God alone sets prices; human agreement does not determine them.

This lesson introduces the Ash'ari stance on pricing, rooted in the prophetic hadith where the Prophet refused to fix prices, declaring 'al-musa`ir Allah' (the price-setter is God). It then presents the Mu'tazila's internal disagreement, setting the stage for the theological debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the core argument and the hadith citation.
Pay attention to the contrasting Mu'tazila views.
2
Identify the Ash'ari claim
Locate the sentence that states the Ash'ari view and the hadith evidence. Note how the hadith is used as proof.
3
Analyze the Mu'tazila positions
List the two Mu'tazila views: price as direct human act vs. generated from God's acts. Understand the difference.
This shows the internal division among the Mu'tazila.
4
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider why the Ash'aris insist on attributing prices to God and how this relates to divine sovereignty.
This connects to broader kalam themes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس في الأسعار) وهو الرخص والغلاء ( المسعر هو الله على أصلنا كما ورد في الحديث حين وقع غلاء في المدينة فاجتمع أهلها إليه عليه الصلاة والسلام وقالوا : سعر لنا يا رسول الله ، فقال : « المسعر الله» . ( وأما عندهم فمختلف فيه فقال بعضم : هو ( أي السعر ( فعل مباشر من العبد، إذ ليس ذلك إلا مواضعة منهم على البيع والشراء بثمن مخصوص، وقال آخرون : هو متولد من فعل الله تعالى ( وهو تقليل الأجناس وتكثير الرغبات بأسباب هي فعله تعالى .
قوله : ( وهو خلاف (الإجماع أبطل اللازم بكونه خلاف الإجماع لا بقوله تعالى : وما من دابة في الأرض إلا على الله رزقها ﴾ [هود : ٦]، كما ذكر في تفسير القاضي وغيره، لأن المعتزلة أجابوا عنه بأنه تعالى قد ساق إليه كثيراً من المباح إلا أنه أعرض عنه بسوء اختياره وهذا وإن سهل دفعه بأن يفرض أن الله تعالى لم يسق إليه شيئاً من المباح، لكن النقض بمن مات ولم يأكل حلالاً ولا حراماً قوي، والجواب بأن معنى الآية والله أعلم وما من دابة يتصف بالمرزوقية إلا على الله رزقها وعلى تقدير تسليم كون هذا المعنى مشترك الدفع فإن من أكل الحرام طول عمره لم يتصف بالمرزوقية عند الخصم المخصص للرزق بالحلال .
قوله : ( الخامس في الأسعار إلخ الظاهر ان هذا بحث لغوي لا كلامي إذ لا نزاع في أن تقليل الأجناس وتكثير الرغبات من الله تعالى، ولا في أن تعيين المبيع والثمن من العبد وإنما النزاع في إطلاق السعر لكن لما ورد في الحديث تعرضوا له .
(The fifth topic: on prices), i.e., cheapness and dearness. (The one who sets prices is God according to our school), as narrated in the hadith when there was a price increase in Medina, so the people gathered to him (peace and blessings be upon him) and said: 'Set prices for us, O Messenger of God.' He said: 'The one who sets prices is God.' (But according to them [the Mu'tazila], there is disagreement. Some of them said: it [i.e., price] is a direct act of the servant, since it is nothing but an agreement among them to buy and sell at a specific price. Others said: it is generated from God's act, i.e., decreasing the varieties and increasing desires by means that are His acts.

His saying: (And it is contrary to consensus). He invalidates the necessary consequence by its being contrary to consensus, not by His saying: 'And there is no creature on earth but that upon God is its provision' [Hud 6], as mentioned in the commentary of al-Qadi and others, because the Mu'tazila answered it by saying that He has conveyed to him much of the permissible, but he turned away from it by his evil choice. And this, although easy to refute by supposing that God did not convey to him any permissible thing, the objection regarding one who dies without having eaten anything lawful or unlawful is strong. And the answer is that the meaning of the verse (and God knows best) is: 'There is no creature that is described as being provided for but that God's provision is upon it.' And on the assumption that this meaning is common and defensible, the one who eats unlawful food all his life is not described as being provided for according to the opponent who restricts provision to the lawful.

His saying: (The fifth topic: on prices, etc.) It is apparent that this is a linguistic discussion, not a theological one, since there is no dispute that the decrease of varieties and increase of desires are from God, nor that the determination of the commodity and the price is from the servant. The dispute is only about the usage of the term 'price.' But because it occurs in the hadith, they discuss it.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the topic of pricing (al-as'ār). It begins by stating the Ash'ari position that the one who sets prices (al-musa`ir) is God, citing the hadith where the Prophet refused to set prices and said "al-musa`ir Allah." Then it presents the Mu'tazila disagreement: some say the price is a direct human act (voluntary agreement), others say it is generated (mutawallid) from God's act of decreasing supply and increasing demand. The commentator then addresses a textual point: the phrase "and it is contrary to consensus" is clarified as a refutation by appealing to consensus, not by the verse "And there is no creature but that upon God is its provision" (Hud 6). The Mu'tazila had argued against using this verse by saying that God may have provided permissible things, but the servant turns away. The commentator says this can be countered by supposing no permissible provision was given, but a strong objection remains: someone who dies without having eaten anything lawful or unlawful. The answer is that the verse means that provision is only what is lawful (at-tawqīf). Finally, the commentator notes that this whole discussion is linguistic, not theological, because the facts are agreed upon; the dispute is only about the term 'price', but they discuss it due to the hadith.

Key Terms
المسعر
الأسعار
الرخص
الغلاء
المتولد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the hadith proves God directly fixes prices in a deterministic way, leaving no room for human action.
Correction: The hadith actually denies human interference in price manipulation, but does not negate that human agreements and natural causes play a role; God is the ultimate cause.

Checkpoint
What is the Ash'ari position on pricing according to the passage?
A) Price is determined by human agreement.
B) God is the one who sets prices.
C) Prices are generated solely from natural causes.
D) The Prophet was the price-setter.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'aris affirm that God is the ultimate determiner of prices, based on the Prophet's refusal to fix prices and his statement that God is the price-setter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face financial hardship or unexpected price increases, do your anxieties reveal a trust that God alone controls these matters, or do you rely solely on human planning and negotiation? How can you actively remind yourself of the hadith in this lesson when tempted to panic?
?
The Prophet refused to fix prices, teaching that human intervention cannot override divine decree. Are there areas in your life where you try to 'fix prices'—control outcomes through your own efforts—instead of surrendering to God's will? What specific step can you take this week to relinquish that control and submit to His determination?

The Consensus Argument and Linguistic Nature of the Dispute
Refutation by consensus, analysis of provision verse, and linguistic conclusion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator refutes the Mu'tazila by appealing to consensus and why the dispute over pricing is ultimately linguistic, not theological.

Core Idea
The debate on pricing is about word usage, not about who causes supply and demand.

This lesson unpacks the commentator's refutation of the Mu'tazila, showing how the verse on provision (Hud 6) is set aside in favor of a consensus argument. It concludes that the entire disagreement is merely semantic, since all parties agree that God causes scarcity and desire, while humans set the actual price. The lesson highlights how a kalam discussion can sometimes boil down to terminology.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, noting the argument structure.
The passage contains a refutation and a final linguistic note.
2
Understand the consensus argument
The commentator says the Mu'tazila position is refuted by consensus, not by the verse on provision. Identify why the verse is not used.
The Mu'tazila had a counter-argument involving permissible provision.
3
Trace the counter-arguments
Follow the exchange: Mu'tazila objection, possible reply, and strong objection about someone who dies without any food.
The commentator offers an answer by reinterpreting 'provision' as lawful food.
4
Absorb the linguistic conclusion
The final sentence states that the dispute is linguistic (about the term 'price'), not theological, because all agreed on the facts.
This places the entire discussion in perspective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس في الأسعار) وهو الرخص والغلاء ( المسعر هو الله على أصلنا كما ورد في الحديث حين وقع غلاء في المدينة فاجتمع أهلها إليه عليه الصلاة والسلام وقالوا : سعر لنا يا رسول الله ، فقال : « المسعر الله» . ( وأما عندهم فمختلف فيه فقال بعضم : هو ( أي السعر ( فعل مباشر من العبد، إذ ليس ذلك إلا مواضعة منهم على البيع والشراء بثمن مخصوص، وقال آخرون : هو متولد من فعل الله تعالى ( وهو تقليل الأجناس وتكثير الرغبات بأسباب هي فعله تعالى .
قوله : ( وهو خلاف (الإجماع أبطل اللازم بكونه خلاف الإجماع لا بقوله تعالى : وما من دابة في الأرض إلا على الله رزقها ﴾ [هود : ٦]، كما ذكر في تفسير القاضي وغيره، لأن المعتزلة أجابوا عنه بأنه تعالى قد ساق إليه كثيراً من المباح إلا أنه أعرض عنه بسوء اختياره وهذا وإن سهل دفعه بأن يفرض أن الله تعالى لم يسق إليه شيئاً من المباح، لكن النقض بمن مات ولم يأكل حلالاً ولا حراماً قوي، والجواب بأن معنى الآية والله أعلم وما من دابة يتصف بالمرزوقية إلا على الله رزقها وعلى تقدير تسليم كون هذا المعنى مشترك الدفع فإن من أكل الحرام طول عمره لم يتصف بالمرزوقية عند الخصم المخصص للرزق بالحلال .
قوله : ( الخامس في الأسعار إلخ الظاهر ان هذا بحث لغوي لا كلامي إذ لا نزاع في أن تقليل الأجناس وتكثير الرغبات من الله تعالى، ولا في أن تعيين المبيع والثمن من العبد وإنما النزاع في إطلاق السعر لكن لما ورد في الحديث تعرضوا له .
(The fifth topic: on prices), i.e., cheapness and dearness. (The one who sets prices is God according to our school), as narrated in the hadith when there was a price increase in Medina, so the people gathered to him (peace and blessings be upon him) and said: 'Set prices for us, O Messenger of God.' He said: 'The one who sets prices is God.' (But according to them [the Mu'tazila], there is disagreement. Some of them said: it [i.e., price] is a direct act of the servant, since it is nothing but an agreement among them to buy and sell at a specific price. Others said: it is generated from God's act, i.e., decreasing the varieties and increasing desires by means that are His acts.

His saying: (And it is contrary to consensus). He invalidates the necessary consequence by its being contrary to consensus, not by His saying: 'And there is no creature on earth but that upon God is its provision' [Hud 6], as mentioned in the commentary of al-Qadi and others, because the Mu'tazila answered it by saying that He has conveyed to him much of the permissible, but he turned away from it by his evil choice. And this, although easy to refute by supposing that God did not convey to him any permissible thing, the objection regarding one who dies without having eaten anything lawful or unlawful is strong. And the answer is that the meaning of the verse (and God knows best) is: 'There is no creature that is described as being provided for but that God's provision is upon it.' And on the assumption that this meaning is common and defensible, the one who eats unlawful food all his life is not described as being provided for according to the opponent who restricts provision to the lawful.

His saying: (The fifth topic: on prices, etc.) It is apparent that this is a linguistic discussion, not a theological one, since there is no dispute that the decrease of varieties and increase of desires are from God, nor that the determination of the commodity and the price is from the servant. The dispute is only about the usage of the term 'price.' But because it occurs in the hadith, they discuss it.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the topic of pricing (al-as'ār). It begins by stating the Ash'ari position that the one who sets prices (al-musa`ir) is God, citing the hadith where the Prophet refused to set prices and said "al-musa`ir Allah." Then it presents the Mu'tazila disagreement: some say the price is a direct human act (voluntary agreement), others say it is generated (mutawallid) from God's act of decreasing supply and increasing demand. The commentator then addresses a textual point: the phrase "and it is contrary to consensus" is clarified as a refutation by appealing to consensus, not by the verse "And there is no creature but that upon God is its provision" (Hud 6). The Mu'tazila had argued against using this verse by saying that God may have provided permissible things, but the servant turns away. The commentator says this can be countered by supposing no permissible provision was given, but a strong objection remains: someone who dies without having eaten anything lawful or unlawful. The answer is that the verse means that provision is only what is lawful (at-tawqīf). Finally, the commentator notes that this whole discussion is linguistic, not theological, because the facts are agreed upon; the dispute is only about the term 'price', but they discuss it due to the hadith.

Key Terms
الإجماع
الرزق
المتولد
اللغوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the verse 'upon God is its provision' directly proves God gives physical food to every creature, including those who starve.
Correction: The verse is interpreted to mean that God provides lawful sustenance; the notion of 'provision' is restricted to lawful food, avoiding the problem of those who die without eating.

Checkpoint
What is the final conclusion of the commentator regarding the dispute on pricing?
A) The dispute is theological and affects core beliefs.
B) The dispute is linguistic, concerning only the use of the term 'price'.
C) The Mu'tazila position is definitively refuted by the verse.
D) The hadith alone settles the matter.

Key Takeaway
The commentator concludes that the debate on pricing is essentially about word usage, not about divine action, since all parties agree on the underlying realities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to trust that God provides for you, yet how often do you lie awake worrying about your income? If this verse truly settled your heart, what would change tonight?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that humans turn away from God's provision by evil choice. When have you, like them, blamed your circumstances for your own turning away? Repent of one thing right now.
?
The author says the dispute is just about words. Are there arguments in your life where you fight over labels while ignoring the reality you both agree on? Name one and commit to dropping it.

Inconsistency in Logical Conversion
Commentary on the inversion of the universal proposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical controversy over converting universal propositions and how the commentator points out a methodological inconsistency in the main text.

Core Idea
Later logicians rejected the classical conversion of universals, causing a conflict in the author's own argument.

This gloss addresses a subtle point about logical conversion. The commentator highlights a tension between the logical method used in the main argument and the author's own stance elsewhere in the book. Let's examine the objection, the possible reply, and the final counter-response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: objection, reply, and counter.
The passage is compact; take your time.
2
Identify the objection
The commentator notes that the main text uses the classical conversion of universals (عكس النقيض), which later logicians (المتأخرون) reject.
This is the core issue.
3
Follow the reply and counter
A possible reply says: even if the conversion is invalid universally, it can be used in this particular case. The commentator responds that this contradicts what the author himself said elsewhere (in the fourth objective).
The inconsistency is not about logic per se, but about the author's own consistency.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why consistency matters in scholarly works. The commentator is not rejecting the logical validity of the contrapositive entirely, but pointing out a methodological flaw.
This is a common pitfall in complex arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وذلك لأنه ينعكس بعكس النقيض إلخ) فيه بحث لأن انعكاس الكلية كنفسها على طريقة القدماء مردود عند المتأخرين وهذا الاستدلال مبني عليه فإن قلت: مردودتيه باعتبار عدم كليته لا ينافي الاستدلال به في المادة الجزئية قلت : هذا حق في نفسه لكن ينافي ذكره في المقصد الرابع من المرصد الثالث في أقسام العلم حيث رد قول المدعي فيه بعد انعكاس الكلية كنفسها وقد أشير إلى هذا هناك أيضاً
His saying: 'And because it converts by contrapositive, etc.' There is discussion in this because the conversion of a universal proposition as such, according to the method of the ancients, is rejected by the later scholars, and this inference is based upon it. If you say: Its being rejected due to it not being universal does not preclude inferring by it in a particular matter, I say: This is true in itself, but it contradicts its mention in the fourth objective of the third observation on the divisions of knowledge, where he refuted the claim of the claimant there after the conversion of the universal as such, and reference was made to this there as well.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This gloss addresses the logical contrapositive used in the main argument. The commentator notes that converting universal propositions as such, in the method of the ancients (قُدَمَاء), is rejected by later logicians (مُتَأَخِّرُونَ). The inference in the main text depends on this conversion. One might object that even if the conversion is invalid universally, it can still be used in this particular matter. The commentator responds that, although this is true in itself, it contradicts what was said earlier in the book (in the fourth objective of the third observation) where such conversion was rejected. Thus there is an inconsistency.

Key Terms
انعكاس الكلية
عكس النقيض
القدماء
المتأخرون
المادة الجزئية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the commentator rejects the logical validity of the contrapositive entirely.
Correction: He only notes a methodological inconsistency: the author used a method he himself rejected elsewhere, not that the contrapositive is logically invalid.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator find a problem with the logical conversion used in the main text?
A) Because conversion is always invalid.
B) Because later logicians reject the classical method of converting universals, and the author previously rejected it elsewhere.
C) Because the conversion only works for particulars.
D) Because the conversion leads to a contradiction with the consensus.

Key Takeaway
The commentator highlights an inconsistency in using a logical method that the author himself had previously rejected.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Grammar of 'Hal...Am' in Kalam Arguments
Commentary on the grammatical form of the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical issue raised about the use of 'am' after 'hal' and its implications for theological argumentation.

Core Idea
Grammar rules matter even in kalam; a small particle can affect an argument's validity.

This gloss examines a grammatical subtlety in the phrasing of the first objection. The author points out that the pairing of 'am' with 'hal' is non-standard unless 'am' is disjunctive. Understanding this helps us see how meticulous scholars were in their use of language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the grammatical issue
Read the passage and note that the phrase 'hal yutī'uhu am lā' is criticized because using 'am' after 'hal' is generally rejected by grammarians.
2
Understand the exception
The exception is when 'am' is disjunctive (munqaṭiʿa), as in the hadith example. But the context here does not support that interpretation.
3
Consider the alternative opinion
The text mentions the school of Ibn Mālik, but this is also rejected. Reflect on why scholars are strict about such details.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : هل يطيعه أم لا فيه إتيان المعادل بهل والنحاة يأبونه إلا أن يحمل أم على المنقطعة كما في قوله عليه السلام هل تزوجت بكراً أم ثيباً ؟ والمقام لا يساعده كما لا يخفى، اللهم إن تحمل على مذهب ابن مالك وإن كان مردوداً كما حققناه في حواشي المطول . قوله : ( فلأنه إذا أتى العبد بالفعل إلخ) فيه بحث لأنه إذا لم يكن طالباً لفعله حقيقة لم تكن الصيغة أمراً إلا ظاهراً، فيجوز أن يكون ذلك القول بالنظر إلى ظاهر الحال لعدم الاطلاع على سريرة المقال . اللهم إلا أن يقال : المعلوم فيما نحن فيه أيضاً تحقق صيغة الأمر بالنسبة إلى إيمان الكافر وأما تحقق حقيقته إذا اعتبر فيه الاقتضاء حقيقة فقد لا نسلم فإن قلت : إذا لم يتحقق حقيقة الأمر يلزم أن لا يؤاخذ أحد بترك الامتثال قلت: لم لا يجوز أن تكون المؤاخذة لترك الامتثال بما أمر به ظاهراً إذ ليس له اطلاع على الحقيقة؟ فتأمل. قوله : أجيب بأنه قد يأمر به إلخ فيه أنه كلام على السند مع أن العاقل إذا علم حصول الفائدة بصورة لطلب لا حاجة له إلى حقيقة الطلب
His saying: 'Whether he obeys him or not' – there is the use of the correlative 'am' with 'hal', and the grammarians reject it unless 'am' is taken as munqaṭiʿa (disjunctive), as in his saying (peace be upon him): 'Did you marry a virgin or a non-virgin?' And the context does not support it, as is not hidden. Unless it is taken according to the school of Ibn Mālik, although it is rejected as we verified in the ḥawāshī al-Muṭawwal. His saying: 'For when the slave performs the act, etc.' – there is discussion in this because if he is not truly seeking his act, then the form (ṣīgha) is not a command except apparently. It is possible that this statement is in view of the apparent state due to lack of access to the inner intention. Unless it is said: What is known in our case as well is the actual occurrence of the form of the command concerning the disbeliever's faith. But as for its reality (ḥaqīqa) when considering real demand (iqtiḍāʾ), we may not concede it. If you say: If the reality of the command is not achieved, then no one would be taken to task for leaving compliance, I say: Why would it not be permissible that the punishment is for leaving compliance with what was commanded apparently, since he has no access to the reality? So reflect. His saying: 'It is answered that he may command it, etc.' – in it is that it is speech about the chain (sanad), while if a rational person knows that benefit is obtained by the form of the request, he has no need for the reality of the request.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage contains three glosses on the response to the first objection. The first gloss criticizes the grammar of the phrase 'هَلْ يُطِيعُهُ أَمْ لا' – the use of 'أم' after 'هل' is not standard; it is only acceptable if 'أم' is disjunctive (munqaṭiʿa), but the context does not support that. The second gloss discusses the example of the master testing his slave. It argues that if the master does not actually seek the act, the command is only apparent, not real. It raises the problem that if the command is not real, then the slave cannot be punished for disobedience, since the command is not genuinely demanded. The gloss suggests that punishment could still be based on the apparent command. The third gloss responds to the answer in the second point: it objects that if the rational person knows benefit comes from the form of the request, he has no need for the real request. This questions the necessity of a genuine intention behind the command.

Key Terms
هل وأم
أم المنقطعة
صيغة الأمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the grammatical issue raised in the first gloss?
A) The use of 'am' after 'hal' is grammatically incorrect unless 'am' is disjunctive.
B) The sentence lacks a subject.
C) The verb 'yuti'u' is in the wrong tense.
D) The phrase is too long.

Key Takeaway
The grammatical issue with 'hal...am' serves as a reminder that theological arguments must be expressed in proper Arabic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about your faith, are you as precise with your words as the scholars were in this lesson? What is one phrase you commonly use that might be grammatically or theologically imprecise? Commit to correcting it today.
?
The author takes time to correct a small particle like 'am'. Are there small, seemingly insignificant details in your religious practice that you overlook? How might giving attention to such details deepen your understanding and worship?
?
Consider the hadith example: the Prophet (peace be upon him) used precise language even in everyday questions. How can you imitate his precision in your own speech, especially when discussing matters of belief? What change will you make starting this week?

Apparent vs Real Command and Accountability
Discussion on the distinction between apparent and real command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the form of a command and its real demand, and how apparent commands can still entail accountability.

Core Idea
A command's form alone can be enough for accountability, even without real intention.

This gloss delves into the heart of the first objection. It discusses a scenario where a master's command is only apparent, not real. The central question is whether the slave can be held accountable for not complying. The author explores the implications and offers a resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the dialectical structure: statement, objection, response.
2
Identify the core problem
If the master does not truly seek the act, the command is only apparent. Does that affect accountability?
3
Understand the objection
The objection says: if the command is not real, no one can be punished for leaving compliance.
4
Analyze the response
The response argues that punishment can be based on the apparent command, since the slave has no access to the inner intention.

The Sufficiency of the Form of Request
Critique of the previous response, arguing form suffices.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that if a rational person knows benefit comes from the form of a request, there is no need for a real request behind it.

Core Idea
Form alone can suffice; the reality of the request becomes unnecessary if benefit is clear.

This third gloss criticizes the answer given in the second gloss. It argues that if a rational person knows that benefit comes from the form of the request, then there is no need for the reality of the request. This challenges the necessity of a genuine intention behind the command.

Guided Path
1
Read the short passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that it criticizes the previous answer as being about the chain (sanad) rather than substance.
2
Identify the critique
The gloss says the answer is 'kalam ala al-sanad' meaning it misses the real point.
3
Understand the substantive point
If a rational person knows benefit is obtained by the form of the request, he has no need for the real request. This undercuts the need for a genuine intention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : هل يطيعه أم لا فيه إتيان المعادل بهل والنحاة يأبونه إلا أن يحمل أم على المنقطعة كما في قوله عليه السلام هل تزوجت بكراً أم ثيباً ؟ والمقام لا يساعده كما لا يخفى، اللهم إن تحمل على مذهب ابن مالك وإن كان مردوداً كما حققناه في حواشي المطول . قوله : ( فلأنه إذا أتى العبد بالفعل إلخ) فيه بحث لأنه إذا لم يكن طالباً لفعله حقيقة لم تكن الصيغة أمراً إلا ظاهراً، فيجوز أن يكون ذلك القول بالنظر إلى ظاهر الحال لعدم الاطلاع على سريرة المقال . اللهم إلا أن يقال : المعلوم فيما نحن فيه أيضاً تحقق صيغة الأمر بالنسبة إلى إيمان الكافر وأما تحقق حقيقته إذا اعتبر فيه الاقتضاء حقيقة فقد لا نسلم فإن قلت : إذا لم يتحقق حقيقة الأمر يلزم أن لا يؤاخذ أحد بترك الامتثال قلت: لم لا يجوز أن تكون المؤاخذة لترك الامتثال بما أمر به ظاهراً إذ ليس له اطلاع على الحقيقة؟ فتأمل. قوله : أجيب بأنه قد يأمر به إلخ فيه أنه كلام على السند مع أن العاقل إذا علم حصول الفائدة بصورة لطلب لا حاجة له إلى حقيقة الطلب
His saying: 'Whether he obeys him or not' – there is the use of the correlative 'am' with 'hal', and the grammarians reject it unless 'am' is taken as munqaṭiʿa (disjunctive), as in his saying (peace be upon him): 'Did you marry a virgin or a non-virgin?' And the context does not support it, as is not hidden. Unless it is taken according to the school of Ibn Mālik, although it is rejected as we verified in the ḥawāshī al-Muṭawwal. His saying: 'For when the slave performs the act, etc.' – there is discussion in this because if he is not truly seeking his act, then the form (ṣīgha) is not a command except apparently. It is possible that this statement is in view of the apparent state due to lack of access to the inner intention. Unless it is said: What is known in our case as well is the actual occurrence of the form of the command concerning the disbeliever's faith. But as for its reality (ḥaqīqa) when considering real demand (iqtiḍāʾ), we may not concede it. If you say: If the reality of the command is not achieved, then no one would be taken to task for leaving compliance, I say: Why would it not be permissible that the punishment is for leaving compliance with what was commanded apparently, since he has no access to the reality? So reflect. His saying: 'It is answered that he may command it, etc.' – in it is that it is speech about the chain (sanad), while if a rational person knows that benefit is obtained by the form of the request, he has no need for the reality of the request.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage contains three glosses on the response to the first objection. The first gloss criticizes the grammar of the phrase 'هَلْ يُطِيعُهُ أَمْ لا' – the use of 'أم' after 'هل' is not standard; it is only acceptable if 'أم' is disjunctive (munqaṭiʿa), but the context does not support that. The second gloss discusses the example of the master testing his slave. It argues that if the master does not actually seek the act, the command is only apparent, not real. It raises the problem that if the command is not real, then the slave cannot be punished for disobedience, since the command is not genuinely demanded. The gloss suggests that punishment could still be based on the apparent command. The third gloss responds to the answer in the second point: it objects that if the rational person knows benefit comes from the form of the request, he has no need for the real request. This questions the necessity of a genuine intention behind the command.

Key Terms
اقتضاء
صيغة الأمر
حقيقة الأمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the third gloss, why is there no need for the reality of the request?
A) Because the person is already obeying.
B) Because the rational person knows benefit comes from the form of the request.
C) Because the command is always real.
D) Because the request is a metaphor.

Key Takeaway
If the form of request delivers the benefit, the reality of the request is unnecessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a command of Allah in the Quran, do you ever doubt its sufficiency because you feel your inner state doesn't match the 'reality' of submission? How does this lesson's claim that the form alone suffices challenge or affirm your worship?
?
If the form of a command is enough for accountability, what does that imply about your tendency to seek extra reassurances or signs before obeying? Will you now act solely on the basis of the command's form, trusting that God's word is sufficient?
?
Consider an area of your life where you know the 'form' of obedience (e.g., prayer, charity) but struggle with the 'reality' of sincerity. Does this lesson give you comfort that the form still counts, or does it push you to ensure that the reality aligns? What concrete step will you take today?

Linguistic Critique of Qada and Qadr Definitions
The author critiques the Ash'ari definitions of qada and qadr using lexical evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Ash'ari definitions of qada as eternal will and qadr as bringing into existence lack linguistic basis, and that the default assumption is against polysemy and transference of word meanings.

Core Idea
Words carry default meanings; claims of transference require proof.

In this passage, the author presents a linguistic critique of two key Ash'ari definitions. He argues that the original meanings of the words qada and qadr, as attested by lexicons like al-Sihah, do not match the theological definitions given by the Ash'aris. This lesson unpacks the critique and the principle that word meanings cannot be changed without evidence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the default principle
The passage states that the default (al-aṣl) is against al-ishūrāk (polysemy) and al-naql (transference). This means that unless proven otherwise, a word retains its original meaning. This principle is crucial to the critique.
The burden of proof lies on those who claim a word has been transferred to a new meaning.
2
Examine the critique of qada
The author quotes someone who says that qada in language means 'the act with perfection' (al-fi'l ma'a al-itqān), citing al-Sihah. Therefore, defining qada as 'eternal will' requires evidence of transference, which is lacking.
Perfection (itqān) means applying the act in accord with wisdom and stripping it of defects.
3
Examine the critique of qadr
Similarly, qadr is said to lexically mean 'determination' (taḥdīd), not 'bringing into existence'. Since transference is against the default and no evidence is provided, the Ash'ari definition is unsupported.
Qadr as determination means setting the measure of each thing by which it exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (هو إرادته (الأزلية قيل : القضاء في اللغة عبارة عن الفعل مع الإتقان كما دل عليه كلام صاحب الصحاح والإتقان تطبيقه على ما يقتضيه بالحكمة وتعرية له من مظان الخلل، ولهذا وجب الرضاء بالقضاء، فالقول بأنه عبارة عن الإرادة المذكورة يحتاج إلى بيان يدل على وضعه لذلك لغة أو عرفاً أو اصطلاحاً إذ الأصل خلاف الاشتراك والنقل. قوله : ( وقدره إيجاده إياها إلخ قيل : لم يعتبر الإيجاد في وضعه اللغوي والنقل خلاف الأصل ولا دليل عليه كما سلف في القضاء فليس القدرة عبارة عن الإيجاد المذكور بل هو تحديد كل محدود بحده الذي يوجد به .
His saying: 'It is His eternal will.' It is said: al-qaḍā' in language is an expression for the act with perfection, as indicated by the statement of the author of al-Ṣiḥāḥ. Perfection is applying it to what wisdom requires and stripping it of places of defect. For this reason, contentment with al-qaḍā' is obligatory. So the assertion that it is an expression for the mentioned will requires evidence that indicates its designation for that in language, custom, or technical usage, since the default is against polysemy and transference. His saying: 'And al-qadr is His bringing them into existence, etc.' It is said: Bringing into existence is not considered in its linguistic designation, and transference is against the default, and there is no evidence for it as preceded in al-qaḍā'. Thus al-qadr is not an expression for the mentioned bringing into existence, but rather it is the determination of every limited thing by its limit through which it exists.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic critique of the Ash'ari definitions of qada and qadr. The author quotes someone: regarding qada defined as eternal will, it is argued that lexically qada means 'the act with perfection' (al-fi'l ma'a al-itqan), as stated by al-Jawhari in al-Sihah. Perfection means applying the act in accord with wisdom and stripping it of defects. Therefore, one must be content with qada. So claiming that qada means 'will' requires proof that the word has been transferred to that meaning in language, custom, or technical usage, because the default assumption is that words are not transferred from their original meanings or shared. Similarly, for qadr defined as 'bringing into existence', the critic says that bringing into existence is not part of its linguistic meaning, and there is no evidence for transference. Rather, qadr is the determination (tahdid) of every limited thing by its own limit by which it exists. Thus the definitions given by the Ash'aris lack linguistic basis.

Key Terms
القضاء
القدر
الإتقان
الصحاح
الاشتراك
النقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that qada and qadr are simply will and bringing into existence without considering their linguistic origins.
Correction: The passage shows that these words have specific lexical meanings (act with perfection for qada; determination for qadr) that do not automatically align with the theological definitions. Claims of transference require evidence.

Checkpoint
According to the critique, what is the original linguistic meaning of al-qaḍā'?
A) Eternal will.
B) The act with perfection.
C) Bringing into existence.
D) Determination.

Key Takeaway
The default assumption is against lexical transference; claims that qada means 'will' or qadr means 'bringing into existence' must meet this burden of proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you use the words 'qada' and 'qadr' in your own speech, do you actually mean 'act with perfection' and 'determination'? If not, will you correct your usage based on this lesson?
?
You claim to be content with God's decree, but is your contentment rooted in the belief that every decree is 'act with perfection' – flawless and wise? If not, what is your contentment really based on?
?
This lesson warns against accepting transferred meanings without evidence. Are there any theological beliefs you hold that you have never verified from the original sources? Will you investigate them today?

The First Meaning: Good as Perfection, Bad as Imperfection
Definition of good and bad as perfection and imperfection of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first of the three meanings of good (husn) and bad (qubh) according to al-Jurjani: that good is an attribute of perfection and bad is an attribute of imperfection, and that this meaning is intrinsic to attributes.

Core Idea
Good and bad, in their first sense, describe perfection and imperfection in themselves.

Al-Jurjani begins by clarifying the foundational meaning of good and bad in kalam discourse. This meaning is not about legal or ethical judgment but about the intrinsic quality of attributes. The passage defines good as an attribute of perfection and bad as an attribute of imperfection, and notes that this meaning is undisputed and rationally known.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the author's phrasing.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the examples of knowledge and ignorance.
2
Identify the first meaning
Locate where al-Jurjani defines good and bad in terms of perfection and imperfection. Note that this is the first of three meanings.
3
Examine the examples
See how knowledge is presented as good (perfection) and ignorance as bad (imperfection). Ask yourself why these are considered intrinsic.
The examples show the meaning applied to attributes.
4
Understand the scope
Notice that al-Jurjani states there is no dispute that this meaning is established for the attributes themselves and that it is rationally perceived.
This distinguishes it from other meanings that may be disputed or dependent on revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحسن والقبح يقال لمعان ثلاثة الأول صفة الكمال والنقص فالحسن كون الصفة صفة كمال والقبح كون الصفة صفة نقصان ) يقال : العلم حسن) أي لمن اتصف به كمال وارتفاع شأن ) والجهل (قبيح أي لمن اتصف به نقصان واتضاع حال ( ولا نزاع) في أن هذا المعنى أمر ثابت للصفات في أنفسها و أن مدركه
قوله : ( ما لم ينه عنه شرعاً ينبغي أن يراد بالموصول فعل صادر عن رؤية ليخرج عنه فعل البهائم والنائم والساهي . قوله : يقال لمعان ثلاثة الأول إلخ ذكر المصنف في شرح مختصر ابن الحاجب أن الحسن والقبح إنما يطلقان على ثلاثة معان : الأول : موافقة الغرض ومنافرته الثاني : ما أمر الشارع بالثناء على فاعله أو بالذم الثالث : ما لا حرج في فعله وما فيه حرج، ثم قال وفعل الله تعالى باعتبار الأول لا يوصف بحسن ولا قبح لتنزهه عن الغرض والظاهر أن الحصر المذكور إضافي حقيقي إذ المعنى الأول المذكور هاهنا غير المعاني الثلاثة المذكورة في ذلك الشرح، أما مغايرته للأول فظاهر، وأما للأخيرين فلانهما يخصان الأفعال بخلاف ما ذكر هاهنا . فإن قلت : الذي حصره في الثلاثة هناك حسن الأفعال وقبحها لا مطلققهما :قلت لا شك أن الحسن والقبح قد يطلقان على ملاءمة الطبع ومنافرته، وليس هذا عين المعنى الأول المذكور هناك لافتراقهما في الفعل الذي لا يلائم الغرض وينافر الطبع، وبالعكس كتناول الأدوية المرة النافعة والأشربة اللذيذة الغير النافعة فلا يستقيم الحصر الحقيقي فتأمل.
Good and bad are said with three meanings: first, the attribute of perfection and imperfection: good is that the attribute is an attribute of perfection, and bad is that the attribute is an attribute of imperfection. It is said: knowledge is good, i.e., for the one described by it, it is perfection and elevation of status, and ignorance is bad, i.e., for the one described by it, it is imperfection and lowliness of state. And there is no dispute that this meaning is something established for attributes in themselves, and that its perceiver is [the intellect?]. His saying: 'What the Law has not forbidden' — it is intended that the relative pronoun refers to an act emanating from deliberation, to exclude the actions of animals, the sleeping, and the heedless. His saying: 'It is said with three meanings' etc. The author mentioned in Sharh Mukhtasar Ibn al-Hajib that good and bad are applied to three meanings: first: agreement with the purpose and its opposition; second: what the Law has commanded praise of its doer or blame; third: what is not blameworthy in its doing and what is blameworthy. Then he said: And the act of Allah, by the first consideration, is not described as good or bad due to His transcendence above purpose. It appears that the aforementioned enumeration is additional and real, for the first meaning mentioned here is different from the three meanings mentioned in that commentary. As for its difference from the first, it is apparent; as for the latter two, because they are specific to acts, unlike what is mentioned here. If you say: That which he enumerated as three there is the goodness and badness of acts, not their absolute [goodness and badness]? I say: There is no doubt that good and bad may be applied to agreement with nature and its opposition, and this is not the same as the first meaning mentioned there, because they differ in the act that does not agree with the purpose and opposes nature, and vice versa, like taking beneficial bitter medicine and delicious but non-beneficial drinks. So the real enumeration does not hold, so reflect.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage enumerates the first meaning of good and bad as perfection and imperfection, which applies to attributes. It then notes that there is no dispute about this meaning being intrinsic. The commentator then compares this to the three meanings mentioned by the author in another work, which are based on purpose, praise/blame, and hardship. The commentator discusses the differences and notes that the enumeration is not exhaustive. This lesson includes both the main text's first meaning and the commentator's additional analysis.

husn
qubh
kamal
nuqsan

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Good and bad always refer to what is commanded or forbidden by the Law.
Correction: The first meaning of good and bad in kalam is about perfection and imperfection, not about legal commands.

Checkpoint
According to the first meaning in this passage, what makes an attribute 'good'?
A) It is commanded by the Law
B) It is an attribute of perfection
C) It agrees with human nature
D) It does not cause harm

Key Takeaway
The first meaning of good and bad is that good is an attribute of perfection and bad is an attribute of imperfection, and this meaning is intrinsic to the attributes and rationally known.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own character, do you find yourself more drawn to attributes of perfection (kamal) or imperfection (nuqsan)? What does your answer reveal about your inner state?
?
If good and bad are intrinsically known through reason, what excuse do you have for clinging to ignorance when knowledge is clearly a perfection? Will you today seek to replace one attribute of imperfection with its corresponding perfection?
?
The passage states that there is no dispute about this meaning being fixed. Do you live as if moral truths are objective and knowable, or do you treat them as relative to your desires? How will you realign with the truth?

Alternative Classifications of Good and Bad
Comparison of three meanings of good and bad from another work.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three meanings of good and bad mentioned by the author in his commentary on Mukhtasar Ibn al-Hajib, and how they differ from the first meaning of perfection/imperfection.

Core Idea
Good and bad can also mean agreement with purpose, praise/blame, or hardship, but these are distinct from perfection.

After explaining the first meaning, al-Jurjani transitions to discuss an alternative classification found in another work. This classification defines good and bad in terms of purpose, divine praise/blame, and hardship. He then compares these to the first meaning and notes that the enumeration is not exhaustive, as there is also agreement with nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that it refers to a classification from Sharh Mukhtasar Ibn al-Hajib.
This passage is more complex; take your time with the objection and response.
2
Identify the three meanings from the other work
Find the three meanings: (1) agreement with purpose, (2) command of praise/blame, (3) what is blameworthy or not.
These are different from the perfection sense.
3
Understand the comparison
Note that al-Jurjani points out that the first meaning in this lesson (perfection) is different from all three, especially the latter two which apply only to acts.
4
Analyze the objection and response
Read the objection that the three meanings were only about acts, and the response that good and bad also apply to agreement with nature, using the example of bitter medicine.
Consider why this shows the enumeration is not real.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Clarifying Infinite Regress in Acts
Clarification on infinite regress condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific condition under which infinite regress is considered impossible in theological reasoning, and how the commentator refines the scope of that argument.

Core Idea
Infinite regress only follows from acts done by choice, not from acts without choice.

This lesson examines a marginal note that clarifies a common theological argument. The commentator specifies that the impossibility of infinite regress does not apply unconditionally, but only when the action is performed by the servant through his own choice. This specification is crucial for understanding many kalam arguments about human action and divine creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the marginal note
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how the commentator begins by referencing the main text ('His saying: ...') and then adds a clarification.
The commentator assumes familiarity with earlier discussions on infinite regress.
2
Identify the condition
Find the phrase that states when infinite regress follows. The commentator says it only follows if the act is 'by the servant's choice' (باختياره).
Contrast this with acts that issue from the servant 'without choice' (لا به).
3
Consider the broader application
The commentator notes that some objections from a previous section apply here as well. Reflect on why this note prevents unnecessary repetition in theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وتسلسل وهو محال قد مر معنا أنه إنما يلزم التسلسل إذا كان صادراً عن العبد باختياره، وأما إذا صدر عنه لا به فلا واعلم أن بعض الاعتراضات التي أوردناها في المقصد الأول من هذا المرصد متوجه على ماذكره في هذا المقصد أيضاً فلا حاجة إلى تكريرها فليطلب منه .
قوله : (كان اتفاقياً كما مر أي صادراً بلا سبب يقتضيه ويخصص وجوده بالنسبة إلى ذلك الوقت الذي وقع فيه فإن قلت : المرجح الأول يجوز أن يرجح وقت وجوده على سائر الأوقات كما أنه نفس وجوده على عدمه إذ لا ممانعة بين المرجحين. قلت: لما كان يرجح المرجح الأول هو الاختيار المعلق به في الوقتين معا على الغرض السابق كان نسبته إلى الوقتين حينئذ على السوية فلا معنى لترجيحه وقت وجوده على سائر الأوقات وهو ظاهر.
قوله : ( والأظهر أن يقال إلخ ) أي الأظهر أن يجعل كل من لزوم نفي الشرعيين وتكليف ما لا يطاق فساداً مستقلاً لا أن يجعل اللازم هو الأول ويجعل الثاني دليلاً عليه كما هو الظاهر من كلام المصنف وإن كان المتبادر من كلام الشارح أنه تعليل المقدر.
His saying: 'and it leads to an infinite regress and it is impossible, it has already been mentioned that infinite regress only follows if it issues from the servant by his choice, but if it issues from him without it then not. And know that some of the objections we raised in the first objective of this observation also apply to what is mentioned in this objective, so there is no need to repeat them; let them be sought from there.'
His saying: 'it would be coincidental as previously mentioned' meaning it issues without a cause that necessitates it and specifies its existence relative to that time in which it occurred. For if you say: The first preponderator may preponderate the time of its existence over other times just as it preponderates its existence over non-existence, since there is no incompatibility between the two preponderators. I say: Since the first preponderator is the choice attached to it in both times together on the previous purpose, its relationship to the two times is then equal, so there is no meaning to preponderating its time of existence over other times, and that is clear.
His saying: 'and it is more apparent to say etc.' meaning it is more apparent to make each of the necessary consequences, namely the negation of the two [legal judgments] and the obligation of what is beyond capacity, as independent corruptions, not to make the first the necessary consequence and the second a proof for it, as appears from the words of the author, even though the text of the commentator seems to indicate it is an interpretation of the implicit.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage contains three marginal notes from the commentator explaining specific phrases in the main text. The first note (on 'and leads to infinite regress') clarifies that infinite regress only follows if the act proceeds from the servant by his choice; if it proceeds without choice, regress is not implied. The commentator also notes that some objections from the first objective apply here and need not be repeated. The second note (on 'it would be coincidental') explains that 'coincidental' means occurring without a cause that necessitates it or specifies its time. It then addresses a possible question: could the first preponderator also preponderate the time of existence? The answer is that since the first preponderator is the choice attached to the act equally for both times, its relation to the times is equal, so it cannot preponderate one time over another. The third note (on 'the more apparent is to say') explains that the author's phrase 'the more apparent is to say' means that each of the two implications (negation of legal good/evil and impossible obligation) should be considered separate corruptions, not that one is the necessary consequence and the other its proof. This clarifies the structure of the refutation.

Key Terms
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite regress is always impossible in every context.
Correction: The commentator specifies that regress only follows when the act is by the servant's choice; otherwise, it does not.

Checkpoint
According to the first marginal note, when does infinite regress follow?
A) Whenever an act occurs from a created agent.
B) Only if the act is from the servant by his choice.
C) Only in God's actions.
D) Never in the case of voluntary acts.

Key Takeaway
Infinite regress is not an unconditional impossibility; it only applies to actions performed by a free agent's choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own actions, do you ever blame your 'nature' or circumstances for sins, thinking they are not by your choice? How does this lesson's distinction between chosen and non-chosen acts challenge that excuse? What one action this week will you take full responsibility for as a chosen act before Allah?
?
The passage says infinite regress only follows from chosen acts. If your repentance is merely a reflex rather than a deliberate choice, is it true repentance? Examine your last act of tawbah: was it a conscious choice or a routine habit? Commit to making your next repentance a fully conscious, chosen act before Allah.
?
The commentator notes that some objections from earlier apply here. Are you ever guilty of ignoring previous lessons, thinking new contexts require new answers? How can you better connect previous knowledge to current struggles? Name one lesson from before that you need to apply to a present issue.

Coincidence and the Preponderator’s Role
Definition and objection-reply on coincidence and preponderator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'coincidental' is defined in kalam and why the first preponderator cannot preponderate a specific time of existence.

Core Idea
A coincidental act has no cause; the first preponderator cannot favor one time over another.

This lesson explores the second marginal note, which defines 'coincidental' as an act without a specifying cause. It then addresses a potential objection: could the first preponderator also preponderate a specific time? The commentator's reply clarifies that because the preponderator is attached equally to two times, it cannot favor one over the other. This discussion is vital for understanding arguments about causal determinism and divine action.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of 'coincidental'
Read the first part of the passage where the commentator defines 'coincidental' as occurring without a cause that necessitates or specifies its time.
Note that 'coincidental' here means uncaused, not merely random.
2
Understand the objection
The objector says: the first preponderator could preponderate the time of existence just as it preponderates existence over non-existence. Grasp why someone might think this.
The preponderator is the factor that gives preference (tarjīḥ).
3
Follow the reply
The commentator responds that since the first preponderator (choice) is attached to the act in both times equally, its relation to the two times is equal, so it cannot preponderate one over the other.
This shows that the preponderator is not a 'time-specifier' but an existence-specifier.

Distinguishing Independent Corruptions
Methodological note on structuring two independent corruptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator advises treating the negation of the two legal judgments and the obligation of the impossible as two independent corruptions, rather than one being a proof for the other.

Core Idea
Two distinct corruptions should be considered independently, not as one being proof for the other.

This lesson examines a methodological note about structuring a refutation. The commentator explains that the phrase 'the more apparent is to say' indicates that each of the two implications—negation of the two legal judgments (good and evil) and the obligation of what is impossible—should be regarded as a separate corruption. This prevents a logical dependency that might weaken the argument. Understanding this helps students appreciate careful argumentative structure in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentator's note
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that the commentator is explaining the phrase 'and the more apparent is to say...' from the main text.
The note compares two interpretations: one where the second corruption is proof for the first, and the preferred one where both are independent.
2
Identify the two corruptions
The two corruptions are: (1) negation of the two legal judgments (نفي الشرعيين), and (2) obligation of what is beyond capacity (تكليف ما لا يطاق). These are two separate flaws.
Legal judgments here refer to good and evil (حسن وقبح).
3
Understand the preferred interpretation
The commentator says it is more apparent to treat each as an independent corruption, not to make the second a proof for the first. This avoids a potential circularity or unnecessary dependency.
The author's (ابن الحاجب) wording might imply a dependency, but the commentator clarifies the correct understanding.

The Mu'tazili Doctrine of Compensation for Pains
Defines Mu'tazili compensation doctrine and its flaws.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili concept of compensation (`iwad) for undeserved pains, including when it is obligatory and why the author considers the principle flawed.

Core Idea
Compensation for pain is a strict right, not a reward, and its logic is criticized as corrupt.

The Mu'tazila developed a detailed theory of divine justice, including compensation for undeserved suffering. This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif defines when compensation is due and highlights a fundamental problem with the principle. It distinguishes punishment from compensation and explains the obligations of both human agents and God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how the author presents the conditions for compensation.
Pay attention to the three distinct scenarios mentioned.
2
Identify the three scenarios
The passage outlines three cases: pain as punishment, pain caused by another with good deeds, and pain caused by another without good deeds. Note the outcome for each.
The first scenario exempts God from compensation; the second transfers compensation; the third obligates God to compensate or prevent the pain.
3
Understand the term 'iwad
The commentary clarifies that 'iwad is a deserved benefit without veneration, unlike thawab (reward). This distinction is crucial to the Mu'tazili argument.
The author later critiques this concept as leading to weak disagreements.
4
Reflect on the author's critique
Ask yourself why the author says the principle is corrupt. Consider how defining compensation as a strict right might conflict with traditional views of God's sovereignty.
This connects to the broader polemic against Mu'tazilism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الخامس : ) من تلك الأمور ( العوض عن (الآلام فإنهم (قالوا : الألم إن وقع جزاء لما صدر عن العبد من سيئة ) كألم الحد . ( لم يجب على الله عوضه وإلا أي وإن لم يقع جزاء ( فإن كان الإيلام من ) وجب العوض ( عليه ) فإن كان مكلف آخر فإن كان له حسنات أخذ من حسناته وأعطي المجني عليه عوضاً لإيلامه له، وإن لم يكن له حسنات وجب على الله إما صرف المؤلم عن إيلامه أو تعويضه من عنده بما يوازي إيلامه ) أي لا ينقص عن إيلامه، فهو احتراز عما دونه لا عما فوقه ( ولهم بناء على هذا الأصل) الذي هو وجوب العوض المعروف عندهم أنه نفع مستحق خال عن التعظيم والإجلال (اختلافات) ركيكة ( شاهدة بفساده ( أي بفساد الأصل
(The fifth:) among those matters is compensation for pains. They said: pain, if it occurs as a punishment for what has issued from the servant of an evil deed (such as the pain of a hadd penalty), God is not obligated to compensate it. Otherwise, i.e., if it does not occur as a punishment, then if the causing of pain is from someone else, compensation becomes obligatory upon him. If it is from another responsible agent, then if he has good deeds, good deeds are taken from him and given to the injured party as compensation for his causing him pain. If he has no good deeds, then it is obligatory upon God either to avert the one causing the pain from causing it, or to compensate him from His own side with something equivalent to his pain – i.e., not less than his pain, so it is a precaution against what is below it, not above it. And based on this principle – which is the obligation of compensation, known to them as a deserved benefit devoid of veneration and exaltation – they have weak disagreements, testifying to its corruption, i.e., the corruption of the principle.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage defines the Mu'tazili concept of 'iwad (compensation) for pains. It begins by stating that if pain is punishment for sin (e.g., a legal punishment), no compensation is due from God. But if the pain is not punishment and is caused by another responsible agent, that agent must compensate the victim. If the agent has good deeds, they are transferred; if not, God must either prevent the pain or provide compensation equivalent to the pain (but not less). The author then notes that this principle, which defines compensation as a deserved benefit without veneration (unlike reward), leads to several weak disagreements among the Mu'tazila, which the author says reveal the corruption of the principle.

Key Terms
العوض
الآلام
الجزاء
الحسنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some confuse `iwad (compensation) with thawab (reward), thinking they are the same.
Correction: Compensation is a strict right devoid of veneration, while reward is a gracious gift that includes honor.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazili view presented, when is God obligated to compensate for pain?
A) Always, because all pain is unjust
B) Only when the pain is a legal punishment
C) Only when the pain is not a punishment and no human can compensate
D) Never, because God is not obligated to anyone

Key Takeaway
Compensation (`iwad) for undeserved pain is a Mu'tazili doctrine that obligates either the human perpetrator or God to make whole the victim, but it is criticized as corrupt because it treats divine justice like a commercial transaction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a pain that feels undeserved, do you ever find yourself silently expecting God to 'make it up to you'? How does the Mu'tazili view of compensation as a strict right—rather than a gift—expose a subtle entitlement in your own heart? Today, consciously surrender that expectation and thank God for His mercy, not for what you think He owes you.
?
Think of a time you caused someone pain, whether intentionally or not. Did you feel the weight of needing to 'compensate' them, perhaps by doing something good for them? How does the Mu'tazili notion of compensating via good deeds make you reconsider the standard of justice you hold yourself to? Before the day ends, go and make a genuine effort to repair that relationship, without keeping score.
?
The Mu'tazila define compensation as 'devoid of veneration and exaltation'—it is purely transactional. In your relationship with God, are there areas where you treat His blessings as mere compensation for your troubles, rather than as gifts of His love? Commit this week to recognizing one blessing daily as an undeserved favor, not a payment.

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate
6 min
Divine Commands Expose to Punishment, Nullifying Purpose
Author counters that if command is for reward, it also exposes to punishment, and punishment predominates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that divine commands expose to punishment challenges the purpose of command, and how it is answered by appealing to the individual's choice.

Core Idea
The purpose of command is not nullified by punishment because punishment results from the recipient's own choice.

In this passage, the author presents a strong objection to the view that the purpose of divine commands is to expose to reward. The objection argues that commands also expose to punishment, and since punishment affects the majority, the purpose is nullified. The commentary then offers a response, shifting the responsibility to the individual's free choice. Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand how the author counters the idea that command is for reward by highlighting exposure to punishment. Notice the claim that punishment is more frequent.
The objection is based on a balance of benefit over harm.
2
Note the balance argument
The author asserts that the majority are disbelievers and sinners, so exposure to punishment predominates. Therefore, the benefit cannot be a purpose for the Wise.
This is an empirical claim about numbers.
3
Examine the response
The commentator suggests that the goodness of command remains because punishment is due to the recipient's own evil choice, not a defect in the command itself.
The response preserves the purpose by emphasizing individual accountability.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ثم إنه ) أي ما ذكرتم من أن التكليف تعريض للثواب ) معارض بما فيه من تعريض الكافر والفاسق للعذاب ) إذ لولا التكليف لم يستحقا عقاباً. ومن أين لكم أن ذلك التعريض للثواب (أكثر من هذا أي التعريض للعذاب ؟ بل نقول : إن الثاني أكثر من الأول لأن الغلبة للكفرة والفسقة وإذا لم تكن المنفعة أكثر من المضرة لم تصلح تلك المنفعة لأن تكون غرضاً للحكيم العالم بأحوال الأشياء كلها الآتي بالأفعال على وجهها، فبطل ما ذكرتموه من غرض التكليف . قوله : ( كالصلاة والصيام هذا يدل على أن كلمة الشهادة أفضل منهما، وفيه بحث إذ الظاهر أن الصلاة أفضل منهما لاشتمالها عليها وعلى غيرها وسنذكر تمام الكلام فيه في الثواب في توضيح قوله الله : «الإيمان بضع وسبعون شعبة»، الحديث قبل التلفظ بكلمة الشهادة أشق على النفس الكافرة من الصلاة وأمثالها لأن فيه ترك دين اعتاده ولذا تراهم يبذلون أنفسهم وأموالهم دون كلمة الشهادة، نعم تلفظه بعد الإسلام أسهل لكن لبقاء الشيء حكم ابتدائه، وأنت خبير بأن في الصلاة أيضاً بل في كل عمل شرعي يتمثل به الكافر من حيث أنه متلقى من النبي عليه السلام ترك دين اعتاده على أنه ليس الكلام في تلفظ الكافر. قوله : ( معارض بمافيه من تعريض الكافر قد يجاب عنه بأن التعريض للثواب مع التمكين المحسنة للتكليف ولا يبطل حسنه بتعذيب الكافر والفاسق من اكتساب السعادة الأبدية هي . سوء اختيارهما.
Then indeed, what you have mentioned that command is an exposure to reward is countered by what it contains of exposure of the disbeliever and sinner to punishment, for were it not for the command, they would not deserve punishment. And how do you know that this exposure to reward is greater than this exposure to punishment? Rather, we say that the second is more than the first because the majority are disbelievers and sinners. And if the benefit is not greater than the harm, that benefit cannot be a purpose for the Wise One who knows all things and performs actions appropriately. Thus, what you mentioned of the purpose of command is nullified. [Commentary on 'like prayer and fasting'] This indicates that the word of testimony is better than them, but there is discussion; the apparent is that prayer is better than them because it includes the testimony and other things, and we will mention the full discussion in [the section on] reward in clarifying God's saying: 'Faith has seventy-some branches,' the hadith before uttering the word of testimony is harder on the disbelieving soul than prayer and its like because it involves abandoning the religion he was accustomed to; therefore you see them sacrificing themselves and their wealth rather than the word of testimony. Yes, its utterance after Islam is easier, but the thing retains the ruling of its beginning. And you are aware that in prayer also, indeed in every lawful action that the disbeliever imitates in that it is received from the Prophet, peace be upon him, there is abandoning the religion he was accustomed to, although the discussion is not about the utterance of the disbeliever. [Commentary on 'is countered'] It may be answered that the exposure to reward with the enabling is what makes the command good, and its goodness is not invalidated by the punishment of the disbeliever and sinner due to the acquisition of eternal happiness? ... their evil choice.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the previous argument: if command is for reward, it also exposes to punishment, and since punishment is more frequent, the purpose fails. The commentator then offers a possible answer: the good of command remains even if some are punished due to their own choice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if a divine command leads to punishment, it is unjust or purposeless.
Correction: The command is good in itself; punishment results from the individual's abuse of free will, not from a defect in the command.

Checkpoint

A) Because punishment is more severe
B) Because most people are disbelievers and sinners
C) Because reward is not promised
D) Because command is optional

Key Takeaway
The purpose of divine commands is not negated by the existence of punishment because punishment results from the individual's own choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage insists that punishment results from your own evil choice. Think of a recent sin you committed: did you blame circumstances, or did you honestly accept that it was your choice? How would your repentance change if you fully owned that responsibility?
?
If you truly believed that every command of Allah is full of wisdom and goodness even when you cannot see it, what would become easier for you to obey? What command do you struggle with most, and how would that struggle change if you trusted His purpose?

Name, Naming, and Named: Al-Ash'ari's Classification
Author defines name vs. naming and introduces al-Ash'ari's classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the name (ism), naming (tasmiya), and the named (musamma), and al-Ash'ari's threefold classification of divine names.

Core Idea
The dispute about names is about meaning, not words.

In this passage, the author begins a new section on the divine names by clarifying a crucial distinction between the name itself and the act of naming. He then introduces a famous theological dispute and presents al-Ash'ari's nuanced classification. Understanding this foundation is essential for discussing God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the flow from distinguishing 'ism' and 'tasmiya' to the classification.
The passage uses technical terms; focus on the overall structure.
2
Identify the two distinctions
The passage first distinguishes name from naming, then clarifies that the dispute about 'name equals named' is about meaning, not the word.
3
Examine al-Ash'ari's three categories
Al-Ash'ari divides names into three types: identical to essence, other, and neither identical nor other. Find examples for each.
The examples are Allah, al-Khaliq/al-Raziq, and al-'Alim/al-Qadir.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this classification matters for theology: it shows how different names relate to God's essence and attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ المرصد السابع في أسماء الله تعالى ] و به تنتهي مباحث الإلهيات ( وفيه مقاصد ) المقصد الأول : الاسم غير التسمية ] هي ( الاسم غير التسمية لأنها تخصيص الاسم ووضعه للشيء ولا شك أنه ) أي تخصيص الاسم بشيء مغاير (له) أي للاسم كما تشهد به البديهة (و) أيضاً التسمية فعل الواضع وأنه منقض) فيما مضى من الزمان ) وليس الاسم (كذلك) وذهب بعضهم إلى أن التسمية عين الأقوال الدالة التي هي الأسماء، كما سيرد عليك، ولم يلتفت إليه المصنف . ( وقد اشتهر الخلاف في أن الاسم هل هو نفس المسمى أو غيره ؟ ولا يشك عاقل في أنه ليس النزاع في لفظه ف ر س أنه هل هو نفس الحيوان المخصوص أو غيره ) فإن هذا مما لا يشتبه على أحد ( بل) النزاع ( في مدلول الاسم هو الذات من حيث هي هي أم ) هو الذات ) باعتبار أمر صادق عليه عارض له ينبئ عنه ، فلذلك قال الشيخ ( أبو الحسن الأشعري : (قد يكون الاسم ) أي : مدلوله ( عين المسمى ( أي ذاته من حيث هي ( نحو : الله، فإنه اسم علم للذات من غير اعتبار معنى فيه وقد يكون غيره نحو الخالق والرازق مما يدل على نسبته إلى غيره ولا شك أنها أي تلك النسبة ) غيره وقد يكون لا هو ولا غيره كالعليم والقدير مما يدل على صفة حقيقية ) قائمة بذاته ( ومن مذهبه أنها ) أي الصفة الحقيقية
The Seventh Observation on the Names of God, may He be exalted. With it the discussions of the divine (ilahiyyat) end. It contains several goals. The First Goal: The name (al-ism) is other than the naming (al-tasmiya). Because the naming is the assignment of the name and its placing for the thing, and there is no doubt that the assignment of the name to something is different from the name itself, as the intuition testifies. Also, the naming is the act of the positor, and it is transient in past time, while the name is not so. Some went to the view that the naming is identical to the indicative expressions which are the names, as will come to you, but the author did not pay attention to it. The dispute is well-known about whether the name is the same as the named or other. No sane person doubts that the dispute is not about the word 'f-r-s' whether it is the particular animal or other, for that is unambiguous. Rather, the dispute is about the meaning of the name: is it the essence in itself, or the essence with respect to an attribute accidental to it that indicates it? Therefore, al-Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari said: The name (i.e., its meaning) may be identical to the named (i.e., its essence in itself), such as 'Allah', for it is a proper name for the essence without consideration of a meaning in it; and it may be other, such as 'al-Khaliq' and 'al-Raziq', which indicate a relation to other than it, and there is no doubt that that relation is other; and it may be neither identical nor other, such as 'al-'Alim' and 'al-Qadir', which indicate a real attribute subsisting in His essence, and from his school [it is] that the real attribute...
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on the divine names. It first distinguishes the name (ism) from naming (tasmiya). The naming is the act of assigning the word to the meaning, while the name is the word itself that indicates. The author mentions that some object, but he ignores them. Then he discusses the famous dispute about whether the name is the same as the named or other. He clarifies that the dispute is not about the word, but about the meaning (madlul). He then presents al-Ash'ari's classification: some names (like Allah) are identical to the essence, some (like al-Khaliq) indicate a relation and are other, and some (like al-'Alim) indicate a real attribute and are neither identical nor other.

Key Terms
اسم
تسمية
مسمى
ذات
صفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dispute about whether the name is the same as the named is about the word itself (e.g., whether the word 'horse' is the animal).
Correction: No one disputes that the word is different from the thing; the dispute is about the meaning (madlul) of the name: does it refer to the essence alone or the essence with an attribute?

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari, which type of name is identical to the named?
A) Allah
B) al-Khaliq
C) al-'Alim
D) al-Raziq

Key Takeaway
The name may be identical to the essence, other, or neither, depending on whether it indicates a relation or an attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you invoke Allah by a name like 'al-Khaliq', do you consciously recognize that you are referring to His act of creation rather than His essence? How does this affect the sincerity and focus of your worship?
?
The passage teaches that naming (tasmiya) is a human act, not the name itself. How often do you treat the act of naming God as casual or routine? What would change if you approached every mention of a divine name with the awareness that you are engaging in a sacred act of reference?
?
Al-Ash'ari says that names like 'al-'Alim' indicate a real attribute subsisting in God's essence. Have you ever considered that your own knowledge is a contingent accident, while God's knowledge is a real, eternal attribute? How should this realization shape your humility before Him?

The Whole vs. Its Parts: Refuting an Objection to Tawātur
Ash‘arite denial of whole-equals-parts objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash‘arite response to the objection that tawātur is impossible because the whole is equal to its parts.

Core Idea
The collective strength of many reporters exceeds any single one.

One objection against tawātur claims that if each individual report is only probable, the whole set cannot yield certainty. The Ash‘arite response uses a simple analogy: ten people can move a weight that one alone cannot. This lesson unpacks that analogy and its theological significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the short response passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the first answer. Notice how it is only two lines yet carries a powerful logical point.
The objection itself is not included in this chunk, but the answer implies it.
2
Understand the analogy of the ten people
The response uses the example of ten people moving a weight that one person cannot. Ask yourself: does this prove that the whole is stronger than its parts? How does this relate to tawātur?
3
Connect to the broader debate
Consider why an opponent would assume the whole is equatable to its parts. The Ash‘arite denial allows that many reports together produce certainty even if each alone does not.
This is a fundamental principle in kalam epistemology.
