Refuting the Claim that Non-Existent is a Thing
Sanusi refutes the claim that non-existent is a thing and its implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between imputation (ilzam) and actual acceptance (iltizam) in theological arguments, and how this distinction allows refuting a view without declaring its proponents disbelievers.

Core Idea
Imputation of disbelief is not the same as actual disbelief.

Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that non-existence is a thing. He shows that this view logically leads to the belief in eternal essences without a creator, which is even worse than the belief in eternal matter. However, he immediately clarifies that pointing out this logical consequence is not the same as attributing disbelief to the opponents. Understanding this distinction is crucial for theological argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the English translation. Let the full argument settle before analyzing.
Pay attention to the shift in voice from 'they say' to 'we say'.
2
Identify the opponents' claim and its implications
The passage first presents the Mu'tazilite view that non-existence is a thing. Then it draws out a logical consequence: this leads to the belief that essences exist eternally without a creator, which is worse than the belief in eternal matter.
The author connects this to the doctrine of the deniers of states (nufat al-ahwal).
3
Notice the author's response and key distinction
Sanusi responds by saying that this is merely an imputation (ilzam) of disbelief, not an actual acceptance (iltizam) by the opponents. He distinguishes between logical entailment and actual commitment.
This is a standard technique in kalam: refuting by showing necessary consequences without accusing the opponent of holding those consequences.
4
Reflect on why this distinction matters
Think about how this distinction prevents unfair accusations in theological debates. It allows one to critique a view without misrepresenting the opponent's beliefs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : قولهم المعدوم شيء أي ثابت متقرر في الأزل وأنه تصريح بمذهب أهل الهيولي سيما نفاة الأحوال الذين كانوا قبل أبي هاشم لأن ذاته عندهم وجوده، يعني أن نافي الأحوال يلزمه القول بأن ذات الشيء عين وجوده، فإذا كانت الذوات عندهم حاصلة في الأزل بلا فاعل كانت وجوداتها كذلك، فذوات الممكنات حينئذ موجودة قديمة من غير استناد إلى فاعل أصلا وهذا أشنع من القول بوجود الهيولى القديمة المستندة إلى فاعل في الجملة قلنا : ما ذكرتم إلزام للكفر عليهم بما ذهبوا إليه والإلزام غير الالتزام واللزوم غير القول به كما مر عن قريب.
The fifth: Their statement that non-existence is a thing, i.e., subsistent and determined in eternity, and that it is an explicit statement of the doctrine of the People of Hyle (supporters of eternal matter), especially the deniers of states (ahwal) who were before Abu Hashim, because according to them the essence is its existence. That is, the denier of states is compelled to say that the essence of a thing is identical to its existence. So if essences according to them occur in eternity without an agent, then their existences are likewise. Then the essences of contingent things are eternal existents without any dependence on an agent, and this is uglier than the statement of the existence of an eternal hyle that depends on an agent in some way. We say: What you have mentioned is an imputation of disbelief against them due to their view, and imputation is not acceptance, and entailing is not the same as stating it, as has recently passed.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite view that non-existence is a thing. The author explains that this view implies that essences of things are eternal without a creator, which is worse than the belief in an eternal hyle that at least has some dependence on a creator. However, the author's response is that this is merely an imputation (ilzam) of disbelief, meaning that the opponents' view leads to disbelief logically, but that does not mean they actually accept that conclusion. The distinction between ilzam and iltizam is crucial.

نفاة الأحوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author declared the Mu'tazilites disbelievers.
Correction: He only pointed out a logical consequence (ilzam) and distinguished it from actual acceptance (iltizam).

Checkpoint

A) Between essence and existence
B) Between imputation of disbelief and actual acceptance of disbelief
C) Between eternal and created
D) Between hyle and atoms

Key Takeaway
The logical consequence of a view does not necessarily reflect the actual belief of its proponents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
In your own life, are there beliefs or opinions you hold that, if logically extended, would lead to something you know is false? Are you willing to follow the truth even if it means giving up a comfortable position?
?
Have you ever been quick to judge another person's faith based on the consequences of their words rather than their intention? How can you apply Sanusi's distinction between ilzam and iltizam in your daily interactions?

The Meaning of 'Mawla' in the Hadith of Ghadir
Presents the Hadith of Ghadir and argues that 'mawla' means 'awla'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Hadith of Ghadir is used as evidence for Ali's imamate by arguing that 'mawla' means 'the one more entitled to dispose'.

Core Idea
In Ghadir, 'mawla' means authority, not just friendship.

The Hadith of Ghadir is a central proof for the Imamate of Ali. However, its force depends on the meaning of the word 'mawla'. This passage presents the argument that 'mawla' in this context must mean 'the one more entitled' to rule and command, based on the logical elimination of other meanings and the correspondence with the Prophet's introductory question.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith text
Read the Arabic passage of the Hadith of Ghadir carefully. Notice that it begins with the Prophet asking 'Am I not more entitled (awla) to you than yourselves?' and then saying 'For whoever I am his mawla, Ali is his mawla.'
The introductory question sets up the key term 'awla'.
2
Understand the point of inference
The argument states that the word 'mawla' must correspond to 'awla' in the introduction. This is the starting point of the reasoning.
3
List the possible meanings of 'mawla'
The passage lists seven possible meanings: freed slave, manumitter, cousin, neighbor, ally, supporter, and the one more entitled to dispose. Note each one.
The first six are eliminated, leaving only 'al-awla bi al-tasarruf'.
4
Follow the elimination reasoning
Work through why each meaning except 'the one more entitled to dispose' is impossible or leads to falsehood. The passage also argues that all meanings share the concept of wilayah, so the word can be taken literally for this common meaning.
This step solidifies the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السنة فمن وجوه الأول : خبر الغدير وهو أنه عليه السلام أحضر القوم بعد رجوعه من حجة الوداع بغدير خم وهو موضع بين مكة والمدينة بالجحفة وأمر بجمع الرحال فصعد عليها، وقال لهم : أولست أولى بكم من أنفسكم قالوا بلى قال فمن كنت مولاه فعلي مولاه اللهم وال من والاه وعاد من عاداه وانصر من نصره وأخذل من خذله، وجه الاستدلال أن المراد بالمولى هاهنا هو الأولى ليطابق مقدمة الحديث ولأنه أي لفظ المولى يقال للمعتق والمعتق وابن العم والجار والحليف والناصر والأولى بالتصرف والسنة الأولى غير مرادة هاهنا قطعاً، فإن الحمل على المعتق والجار وابن العم يؤدي إلى الكذب والنبي عليه السلام لم يكن معتقاً ولا حليفاً لأحد، والحمل على الناصر ممتنع فإن كل واحد يعلم من دينه ضرورة وجوب تولي المؤمنين بعضهم لبعض فتعين الحمل على الأولى بالتصرف لما ذكرناه ولأنها أي المعاني المذكورة تشترك في الولاية فيجب الحمل عليها وجعل اللفظ حقيقة في هذا القدر المشترك دفعاً للاشتراك اللفظي.
As for the Sunna, among its proofs is the first: the Hadith of Ghadir, which is that he (peace be upon him) gathered the people after his return from the Farewell Pilgrimage at Ghadir Khumm, a place between Mecca and Medina near al-Juhfa, and ordered the saddles to be piled up and ascended them, and said to them: 'Am I not more entitled (awla) to you than yourselves?' They said: 'Yes.' He said: 'For whoever I am his mawla, Ali is his mawla. O Allah, befriend whoever befriends him and oppose whoever opposes him, and support whoever supports him and forsake whoever forsakes him.' The point of inference is that the intended meaning of mawla here is 'more entitled' (al-awla) to correspond with the introduction of the hadith, and because the word mawla is used for the freed slave, the manumitter, the cousin, the neighbor, the ally, the supporter, and the one more entitled to dispose (al-awla bi al-tasarruf), and the first six meanings are definitely not intended here, because interpreting it as the freed slave, neighbor, or cousin leads to falsehood, and the Prophet was not a manumitter nor an ally of anyone, and interpreting it as supporter is impossible because everyone knows from his religion the necessity of believers supporting one another, so it is determined that it means the one more entitled to dispose for the reasons we mentioned, and because these mentioned meanings share in the concept of wilayah (authority/close relationship), so it is necessary to interpret it as such and to make the word a literal term for this common meaning to avoid homonymy.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage is an argumentative presentation of the Hadith of Ghadir as evidence for Ali's immediate imamate. It begins by quoting the hadith, then provides the reasoning for interpreting 'mawla' as 'awla' (more entitled). The author lists the possible meanings of 'mawla' and systematically eliminates those that are inapplicable or lead to falsehood, leaving only 'al-awla bi al-tasarruf' (the one more entitled to dispose of affairs). He further argues that all the meanings share the underlying concept of wilayah, so the word is literal for that common meaning, thus avoiding homonymy. The argument is structured as a logical deduction from the hadith's introduction ('Am I not more entitled to you than yourselves?') to the conclusion that 'mawla' in the statement must mean the same as 'awla' in the question.

Key Terms

أولى
أولى بالتصرف
ولاية
اشتراك لفظي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mawla in the Hadith of Ghadir means simply a friend or supporter.
Correction: The passage argues that interpreting it as supporter is impossible because mutual support among believers is already obligatory, and the context of the Prophet's question requires a meaning of authority, specifically 'the one more entitled to dispose'.

Checkpoint

A) Supporter (nāsir)
B) Freed slave (mu'tiq)
C) The one more entitled to dispose (al-awla bi al-tasarruf)
D) Ally (ḥalīf)

Key Takeaway
The Hadith of Ghadir proves Ali's imamate because 'mawla' is interpreted as 'the one more entitled to dispose', corresponding to the Prophet's introductory claim of being 'more entitled' over the believers.

The Ruling on Commanding Right and Forbidding Wrong
Defines the ruling of commanding right and forbidding wrong as following the act and as a communal obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that commanding right and forbidding wrong is a communal obligation that varies according to the act, and that it is considered a branch of religion by Sunni scholars.

Core Idea
Commanding right and forbidding wrong follows the act: obligatory for obligatory, recommended for recommended.

This lesson establishes the foundational ruling for the obligation of commanding right and forbidding wrong. The text clarifies that its ruling depends on the nature of the commanded or forbidden act, and that it is a communal duty, not an individual one. It also highlights the difference between Sunni and Mu'tazila views on its place in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the basic ruling.
2
Understand the gradation
Note how the obligation of commanding right varies: obligatory for obligatory acts, recommended for recommended acts, etc.
3
Recognize communal obligation
Understand fard kifaya and its implications for community responsibility.
4
Note the theological status
See that this is a branch (far') for Sunnis but a root (asl) for Mu'tazila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة للمرصد الرابع
( في الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر ، أوجبه قوم ومنعه آخرون . والحق أنه تابع للمأمور به والمنهي عنه، فيكون الأمر بالواجب واجباً وبالمندوب مندوباً والنهي عن الحرام واجباً وعن المكروه مندوباً ثم إنه فرض كفاية لا فرض عين، فإذا قام به قوم الآخرين؛ لأن غرضه يحصل بذلك وإذا ظن كل طائفة أنه لم يقم به الآخر أثم الكل بتركه، وهو عندنا من الفروع وعند المعتزلة من الأصول ) . قال الآمدي : ذهب بعض الروافض إلى أنه لا يجب بل لا يجوز الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر إلا بنصب الإمام واستنابته كما في إقامة الحدود وذهب من عداهم إلى وجوبه مطلقاً، ثم اختلفوا فذهب أهل السنة إلى وجوبه شرعاً، والجبائي وابنه إلى وجوبه عقلاً ثم اختلفا فقال الجبائي : يجب مطلقاً فيما يدرك حسنه وقبحه عقلاً، وقال أبو هاشم : إن تضمن الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر دفع ضرر عن الآمر والناهي ولا يندفع عنه إلا بذلك ،وجب، وإلا فلا والذي يدل على وجوبه عندنا الإجماع فإن القائل :قائلان قائل بوجوبه مطلقاً وقائل بوجوبه باستنابة الإمام فقد اتفق الكل على وجوبه في الجملة والكتاب كقوله تعالى : ولتكن منكم أمة يدعون إلى الخير ويأمرون بالمعروف وينهون عن المنكر ﴾ [آل عمران : ١٠٤]، والسنة كقوله عليه السلام لتأمرون بالمعروف ولتنهن عن المنكر أو ليسلطن الله شراركم على خياركم فيدعو خياركم فلا يستجاب لهم. وأما عدم توقف جوازه على استنابة الإمام فيدل عليه أن كل واحد من آحاد الصحابة كان يشتغل بالأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر بلا استنابة وإذن من الإمام، وكان ذلك شائعاً ذائعاً فيما بينهم ولم يوجد نكير فكان إجماعاً على جوازه ( ولوجوبه ) بعد علمه بأن ما يأمر به معروف وأن ما ينهى عنه منكر وأن ذلك ليس من المسائل الاجتهادية التي اختلف فيها اعتقاد الآمر والمأمور والناهي والمنهي ( شرطان: أحدهما: أن يظن أنه لا يصير موجباً لثوران فتنة وإلا لم يجب، وكذا لا يجب إذا ظن أنه لا يفضي إلى المقصود بل يستحب حينئذ إظهاراً لشعار (الإسلام فوجوبه إنما هو إذا جوز حصول المقصود بلا إثارة فتنة ) وثانيهما : عدم التجسس) والتفتيش عن أحوال الناس للكتاب
Conclusion of the Fourth Observatory: On commanding right and forbidding wrong. Some people made it obligatory and others prohibited it. The truth is that it follows the commanded and forbidden act: thus commanding the obligatory is obligatory, commanding the recommended is recommended, forbidding the forbidden is obligatory, and forbidding the disliked is recommended. Moreover, it is a communal obligation (fard kifaya), not a personal obligation (fard 'ayn). If some people undertake it, the obligation falls from the others, because its purpose is achieved by that. If every group thinks that the other has not undertaken it, then all are sinful for abandoning it. In our view, it is a branch (far') of the religion, while according to the Mu'tazila it is a root (asl). Al-Amidi said: Some of the Rawafid held that commanding right and forbidding wrong is not obligatory and not even permissible except with the appointment of an Imam and his authorization, as in the execution of prescribed punishments. Those besides them held that it is absolutely obligatory. Then they differed: Ahl al-Sunna held that it is obligatory by revelation (shar'an), while al-Jubba'i and his son held that it is obligatory by reason ('aqlan). Then they differed further: al-Jubba'i said it is absolutely obligatory in matters whose goodness and badness are perceived by reason. Abu Hashim said: If commanding right and forbidding wrong involves repelling harm from the commander and the forbidden, and that harm cannot be repelled except by it, then it is obligatory; otherwise it is not. What indicates its obligation in our view is consensus (ijma'), for there are two groups: one says it is absolutely obligatory, the other says it is obligatory with the authorization of the Imam – thus all have agreed on its obligation in general. And [evidence from] the Book, as His saying: 'Let there be from you a nation inviting to good, commanding right and forbidding wrong' (Al-Imran 104). And the Sunna, as his saying, peace be upon him: 'You will surely command right and forbid wrong, or Allah will cause the worst of you to rule over the best of you, and the best of you will supplicate and their supplication will not be answered.' As for the non-dependence of its permissibility on the authorization of the Imam, it is indicated by the fact that every individual among the Companions used to engage in commanding right and forbidding wrong without authorization and permission from the Imam, and that was widespread among them with no objection, so it was a consensus on its permissibility. For its obligation, after he knows that what he commands is right and what he forbids is wrong, and that it is not among the ijtihadi matters in which the belief of the commander and the commanded differ, there are two conditions: One: that he conjectures that it will not cause a fitna to break out; otherwise it is not obligatory. Similarly, it is not obligatory if he conjectures that it will not lead to the intended goal; in that case it is recommended to show the symbol of Islam. So its obligation is only when he considers the goal achievable without stirring up fitna. The second: not spying and investigating the conditions of people, according to the Book.
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling on commanding right and forbidding wrong. It begins by stating the differing opinions: some made it obligatory, others prohibited it. The correct view is that it follows the commanded or forbidden act: commanding what is obligatory is obligatory, commanding what is recommended is recommended, forbidding what is haram is obligatory, and forbidding what is makruh is recommended. It is a communal obligation (fard kifaya), not a personal one; if some fulfill it, the rest are absolved. It is considered a branch (far') in Sunni theology but a root (asl) among the Mu'tazila. Al-Amidi reports that some Rawafid require the appointment of an Imam to authorize it, but the majority disagree. Sunni scholars say it is obligatory by revelation (shar'), while al-Jubba'i and his son say it is obligatory by reason ('aql), with differences among them on conditions. The proof of obligation is ijma' (consensus) that it is obligatory in general terms, plus Quran and Sunna. The proof that it does not require authorization from the Imam is that the Companions practiced it without such authorization, and that was widespread with no objection, thus constituting consensus. The conditions for its obligation are: 1) that one conjectures it will not lead to fitna (otherwise it is not obligatory, and if it may not achieve the goal, it is recommended to show the symbol of Islam), and 2) that one does not spy on people.

Key Terms
الأمر بالمعروف
النهي عن المنكر
فرض كفاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That commanding right and forbidding wrong is always individually obligatory (fard 'ayn).
Correction: The text clarifies it is a communal obligation (fard kifaya), not personal. If some undertake it, others are absolved.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on forbidding what is disliked (makruh)?
A) Obligatory
B) Recommended
C) Prohibited
D) Permitted

Key Takeaway
Commanding right and forbidding wrong is a communal duty that varies in obligation depending on the act, and it is a branch of religion according to Sunni scholars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a fellow Muslim committing a sin, do you feel a sense of communal responsibility to gently forbid it? Or do you assume someone else will handle it? How does the concept of fard kifaya challenge your personal passivity?
?
The passage teaches that forbidding the disliked (makruh) is recommended, not obligatory. Are you quick to criticize others for things that are merely disliked, while neglecting your own obligations? How can you balance your efforts?
?
The Mu'tazila made commanding right and forbidding wrong a root (asl) of religion—meaning essential for faith. In your daily life, do you treat this duty as a central pillar or just an optional activity? What would change if you truly saw it as a branch that follows the act?
